The Hanafi Maxim of Invalidating Uncertainty: Al-Jahalah al-Mufsidah
Defining the Hanafi maxim on invalidating uncertainty with four categories and an exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that only uncertainty leading to irresolvable dispute invalidates a contract, and distinguish between binding and non-binding terms.

Core Idea
Uncertainty invalidates only if it leads to a dispute the court cannot resolve without overriding one party's will.

In this passage, the author presents a critical refinement of the general prohibition of uncertainty in contracts. Not every unknown element voids the contract—only uncertainty that would lead to a problematic dispute (niza' mushkil) that the judiciary cannot settle justly. The passage then enumerates four typical sources of such harmful uncertainty, followed by an important exception for non-binding terms.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its condition.
The maxim: al-jahalah al-mufsidah hiya al-jahalah al-mufdiyah ila al-niza' al-mushkil. The condition is that the dispute must be 'mushkil' (problematic) meaning irresolvable without overriding a party's will.
2
Understand the refinement of 'niza'.
Note that 'dispute' here is not any dispute—even a valid contract can lead to disputes—but specifically a dispute where each party has equal grounds, making judicial resolution difficult.
3
List the four categories of harmful uncertainty.
1) Subject matter, 2) Price/consideration, 3) Binding terms (ajal mulzim), 4) Stipulated security means. For each, explain why it leads to problematic dispute.
4
Identify the exception: non-binding terms.
Non-binding terms, like partnership duration, do not require precise knowledge because the contract can be dissolved at will, so no irresolvable dispute arises.
5
Apply the maxim to a new scenario.
Given a scenario, determine whether the uncertainty is invalidating by checking if it leads to niza' mushkil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٥٥) الأسباب العامة لفساد العقد
وقالوا : لا يضرُّ ما فيه من جهالة المبيع عند العقد، لأنها غير مفضية إلى نزاع مشكل، فإنَّ تفويض التعيين إلى أحد المتعاقدين قد فتح طريق إزالة هذه الجهالة وجعل إرادة المفوض في التعيين ملزمة للآخر برضاه. وهذا صالح لحسم النزاع قضاء دون تجاوز على إرادة أحد العاقدين.
(ر) الدرر شرح «الغرر كتاب البيوع ١٥٤/٢).
٣/٥٥ - الجهات التي تتعلق بها الجهالة المفسدة:
هذا، وإن الجهالة المفسدة للعقد إنما ترجع غالباً إلى أربع جهات :
١ - الجهالة في المعقود عليه (محل العقد) وذلك كجهالة المبيع في عقد البيع أو المأجور في الإجارة، أو المُصالح عنه في الصلح، أو الموهوب في الهبة، وهكذا.
۲ - جهالة العوض في عقود المعاوضات المالية وذلك كجهالة الثمن في عقد البيع، وجهالة البدل المصالح عليه في عقد الصلح (۱).
٣ - جهالة الآجال في كل ما يجري فيه أجل ملزم وذلك كجهالة المدة المتعاقد عليها في عقد الإجارة، وجهالة موعد استحقاق الثمن المؤجل في عقد البيع، أو موعد استحقاق البدل المؤجل في عقد الصلح. أما الأجل غير الملزم فلا تضر جهالته كمدة استمرار عقد الشركة . فإنَّ الشركة عقد غير لازم في الاجتهاد الحنفي. فلو حدد لها أجل جاز للشريك فسخها قبله فلا حاجة إلى معرفة أجلها سلفاً، بل تستمر حتى يوجد ما ينهيها من فسخ قصدي أو موت.
٤ - جهالة وسائل التوثيق المشروطة في العقد وذلك كما لو اشترط البائع على المشتري تقديم كفيل أو رهن بالثمن المؤجل. فيجب أن يكون الكفيل والرهن معينين، وإلا فسد البيع.
(۱) بالنظر القانوني يعتبر كل من العوضين المبيع والثمن) محلاً للعقد.
ملاحظة :
هذا، ويلحظ في هذا المقام أن الفقهاء في بحثهم عن الجهالة المفسدة يصفونها بأنها «الجهالة المفضية إلى النزاع».
هكذا يطلقون التعبير بالنزاع، وإنما مرادهم النزاع المشكل الذي يتعذر حسمه لتساوي حجج الطرفين فيه على نحو ما أوضحناه، وذلك بدلالة الفروع التي يذكرونها وإلا فإنَّ مطلق النزاع لا يمكن اجتنابه بعد التعاقد ولو كان التعاقد صحيحاً. فالقضاء هو الكفيل بإحقاق الحق وحسم النزاع وفقاً لنصوص العقود وقواعد الأحكام. وإنما المشكلة عندما يكون في العقد جهالة تصلح متمسكاً لكل من الطرفين في إرادته، فيتعذر على القضاء حل النزاع دون استبداد وتجاوز على الإرادات.
Chapter (55): General Causes of Contract Invalidity
They said: Uncertainty in the sold item at the time of contract does not harm, because it does not lead to problematic dispute, since delegating specification to one of the contracting parties opens a way to remove this uncertainty and makes the will of the delegated party binding on the other with his consent. This is suitable for resolving the dispute judicially without overstepping the will of either party.
(Reference: Al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar, Book of Sales, 2/154).
3/55 - The aspects to which invalidating uncertainty relates:
Now, invalidating uncertainty in a contract generally goes back to four aspects:
1 - Uncertainty in the subject matter (object of contract), such as uncertainty of the sold item in a sale contract, or the rented item in a lease, or the item reconciled in a settlement, or the gifted item in a gift, etc.
2 - Uncertainty of the consideration in financial commutative contracts, such as uncertainty of the price in a sale contract, and uncertainty of the substitute reconciled upon in a settlement contract (1).
3 - Uncertainty of terms in everything that involves a binding term, such as uncertainty of the contracted period in a lease contract, uncertainty of the due date of a deferred price in a sale, or the due date of a deferred substitute in a settlement. As for a non-binding term, its uncertainty does not harm, like the duration of a partnership contract. For the partnership contract is not binding in Hanafi jurisprudence. If a term is set for it, a partner may dissolve it before that, so there is no need to know its term in advance; rather, it continues until something ends it, such as intentional dissolution or death.
4 - Uncertainty of stipulated documentation means, such as when the seller stipulates on the buyer to provide a guarantor or pledge for the deferred price. The guarantor and pledge must be specified; otherwise, the sale is invalid.
(1) Legally speaking, each of the two considerations (the sold item and the price) is considered the subject matter of the contract.
Note:
Now, it is observed here that the jurists, in their discussion of invalidating uncertainty, describe it as 'uncertainty leading to dispute.'
Thus they use the expression 'dispute', but they mean the problematic dispute that is difficult to resolve due to the equal strength of the parties' arguments in it, as we have explained, as indicated by the branches they mention. Otherwise, absolute dispute cannot be avoided after contracting even if the contract is valid. The judiciary is responsible for upholding truth and resolving disputes according to contract texts and legal rules. The problem arises only when the contract contains uncertainty that gives each party a foothold for their will, making it difficult for the judiciary to resolve the dispute without arbitrariness and overstepping wills.
Source pages p. 701

Commentary
This passage defines the legal rule that uncertainty (jahalah) in a contract is invalidating only if it is likely to lead to a dispute that cannot be resolved judicially without overriding the parties' intentions. The author first gives an example where uncertainty does not harm: delegating specification of the sold item to one party, because the delegate's choice is binding on the other. This prevents irresolvable conflict. Then four categories of harmful uncertainty are listed: (1) uncertainty in the subject matter itself, e.g., unknown item sold; (2) uncertainty in the price or exchange in commutative contracts; (3) uncertainty in binding terms like lease duration or payment dates; non-binding terms (like partnership duration) are exempt; (4) uncertainty in stipulated security (guarantor or pledge) must be specified. The note clarifies that jurists mean 'dispute that is difficult to resolve due to equal claims,' not any dispute. This is a Hanafi refinement: the test is whether the court can resolve it without imposing a solution on the parties.

Key Terms
الجهالة المفسدة (al-jahālah al-mufsidah): Uncertainty that invalidates a contract because it leads to irresolvable dispute
الجهالة المفضية إلى النزاع (al-jahālah al-mufḍiyah ilā al-nizā'): Uncertainty that leads to dispute; the juristic expression for invalidating uncertainty
الأجل الملزم (al-ajal al-mulzim): A binding term that must be known to avoid dispute

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about uncertainty in a contract?
A) All uncertainty invalidates the contract.
B) Uncertainty in a non-binding term like partnership duration invalidates.
C) Uncertainty that leads to problematic dispute invalidates.
D) Uncertainty can never be removed after the contract.

Key Takeaway
Invalidating uncertainty (al-jahalah al-mufsidah) is defined as uncertainty that leads to a problematic dispute (niza' mushkil), not every instance of ignorance.

The Hanafi Qawida on Gharar: Nullity vs. Voidability
Defining gharar, its two types, and applications in sales and partnerships.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida distinguishing gharar in essence (void) from gharar in descriptions (voidable) to classify contract defects and produce a fatwa on a given scenario.

Core Idea
Gharar in the essence voids; gharar in description makes the contract voidable but curable.

Welcome to the study of gharar – a key invalidating cause in Islamic contract law. The Hanafi school offers a nuanced approach: not all gharar renders a contract void. Some forms make it voidable (fāsid), giving the affected party a right to rescind. This lesson unpacks the famous Hanafi qawida on gharar and its application to real cases.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida
First, recite and understand the qawida: 'الغرر في أصل المعقود عليه يوجب بطلان العقد، والغرر في الأوصاف والمقادير يوجب فساد العقد.' This is the core principle that governs Hanafi contract law on uncertainty.
The qawida is wusta (intermediate) and kulliya (universal).
2
Identify the dalil
The passage cites the prophetic prohibition 'نهى عن بيع الغرر' as the source. This ḥadīth establishes the general unlawfulness of gharar, but the Hanafi school uses it to derive a graded response.
The ḥadīth is a dalīl naqlī; its exact wording is not quoted in full but its meaning is relied upon.
3
Distinguish the two types
Analyze the passage's classification: gharar in the essence (existence or fundamental identity) of the subject matter leads to nullity (bāṭil). Gharar in descriptions, quantities, or subsidiary matters leads to voidability (fāsid). Understand the legal consequence: bāṭil cannot be rectified; fāsid can be cured by removing the defect.
Examples of essence gharar: selling an embryo (may not exist) or future catch. Examples of description gharar: selling a cow with a fixed milk yield.
4
Apply to furū‘ from the text
Walk through each case: (1) selling embryo – bāṭil; (2) selling hunter's catch – bāṭil; (3) selling cow with condition of specific milk yield – fāsid; (4) selling goat as pregnant – fāsid; (5) partnership with fixed profit – fāsid. For each, identify which type of gharar is present.
Note the distinction between a condition of a specific quantity (gharar) and a general description (e.g., 'milking cow'), which gives an option but does not void the contract.
5
Understand terminological laxity
The passage notes that classical jurists sometimes interchange bāṭil and fāsid. Recognize that in precise Hanafi doctrine, the terms are distinct. As a mufti, you must apply the correct classification.
This is a common pitfall in fatwa: always refer to the substantive rule, not the loose terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً : الغرر
٤/٥٥ - الغَرَر (بفتحتين) هو في اللغة بمعنى التغرير، أي الإيهام والتوريط (ر : القاموس ). والمراد به عند الفقهاء أن يعتمد التعاقد على أمر موهوم غير موثوق .. كذلك لأن ظاهره يغر العاقد ويورطه في نتيجة موهومة. وأصله ما ثبت في السنة أنَّ النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام قد نهى عن بيع الغرر (1) .
والاجتهاد الحنفي يميز في هذا الصدد بين نوعين من الغرر : الأول: غرر في أصل المعقود عليه وهذا يوجب بطلان العقد وذلك كبيع الحمل في بطن أمه فإنه باطل لاحتمال أن يكون انتفاخاً، أو أن يولد ميتاً ... وكبيع ضربة القانص، والغائص أي ما سيخرجه الصياد في شبكته من السمك والغائص في غوصته من الدرر. فلا ينعقد البيع في شيء من ذلك للغرر، فإنه أشبه بالقمار. ولذا كانت المباحات لا يجوز بيعها قبل إحرازها.
والثاني: غرر في الأوصاف والمقادير ونحوها من النواحي الفرعية. وهذا يوجب فساد العقد، وهو المقصود هنا.
وأكثر ما يذكر الفقهاء الفساد للغرر في البيوع والشركات :
ففي البيوع لو باع شخص بقرة واشترط أنها تحلب كذا رطلاً، فالبيع فاسد للغرر، إذ يمكن أن لا تحلب هذا المقدار تماماً. وهذا بخلاف ما لو باعها على أنها حلوب أو غزيرة اللبن، فهذا وصف لا غرر فيه : فإذا تبين أنها ليس لها لبن تسمى به حلوباً أو غزيرة اللبن في العرف، يحق للمشتري الفسخ بخيار فوات الوصف المشروط. وكذا لو باع شاةً على أنها حامل أو حامل ذكراً. فالبيع فاسد للغرر.
وفي الشركات لو اشترط المتعاقدان في عقد الشركة أن يكون لأحدهما مقدار محدد من الدنانير أو الدراهم من الربح فإن ذلك غرر، لاحتمال أن لا تربح الشركة سوى هذا القدر أو أقل أو لا تربح أصلاً أو تخسر فيشترط لصحة الشركة أن يكون الربح موزّعاً بين الشركاء حصصاً شائعة نسبية كالنصف أو الربع أو كذا في المائة، فإذا شُرِط لأحدهم مقدار مقطوع فسدت الشركة (ر ) المجلة / ١٣٣٦ (١) .
(۱) يلحظ هنا أن المجلة قد عبرت في هذه المادة ببطلان الشركة إذا اشترط فيها مقدار مقطوع من الربح لأحد الشركاء. لكن المراد الفساد كما تصرح به نصوص الفقهاء الشركة وهو الموافق للقواعد (ر : الدر المختار ٣٤٤/٣) .
وكثيراً ما يتساهل الفقهاء في التعبير بين البطلان والفساد، فيطلقون الفاسد على الباطل، وبالعكس (ر ) باب البيع الفاسد في الدر المختار وفي الدرر، وفي الهداية. وقد نبه أيضاً على هذا التساهل في التعبير الأستاذ شفيق شحاتة (ر: رسالته نظرية الالتزامات في الشريعة الإسلامية، ف/ ١٦٣).
Secondly: Gharar. 4/55 - Gharar (with two fatha) in language means deception, i.e., illusion and involvement. The jurists mean by it that the contract is based on an illusory, unreliable matter... because its outward appearance deceives the contracting party and involves him in an illusory outcome. Its origin is what is established in the Sunnah that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited the sale of gharar. The Hanafi ijtihad distinguishes in this regard between two types of gharar: First: gharar in the essence of the subject matter, and this necessitates nullity of the contract, such as selling an unborn baby in its mother's abdomen, for it is void due to the possibility that it may be a swelling or that it may be born dead... and such as selling the catch of a hunter or a diver, i.e., what the fisherman pulls out in his net and the diver's pearls in his dive. So the sale does not conclude in any of that because of gharar, for it resembles gambling. Hence, permissible objects cannot be sold before acquisition. Second: gharar in descriptions and quantities and similar subsidiary aspects. This necessitates voidability of the contract, which is intended here. Most jurists mention voidability due to gharar in sales and partnerships: In sales, if a person sells a cow and stipulates that she yields such and such pounds of milk, the sale is voidable due to gharar, since it is possible that she does not yield exactly this amount. This is different from selling her as a milking cow or abundant in milk; this is a description without gharar: so if it becomes clear that she has no milk to be called milking or abundant milk in custom, the buyer has the right to rescind by option of non-fulfillment of the stipulated description. Likewise, if he sells a goat on the condition that she is pregnant or pregnant with a male, the sale is voidable due to gharar. In partnerships, if the parties stipulate in the partnership contract that one of them has a fixed amount of dinars or dirhams from the profit, then that is gharar, because it is possible that the partnership profits only that amount or less or does not profit at all or incurs a loss. For the validity of the partnership, it is required that the profit be distributed among the partners as proportional shares, like half or quarter or a percentage, so if a fixed amount is stipulated for one of them, the partnership becomes voidable (see Majallah article 1336). (1) Note here that the Majallah expressed in this article as nullity of the partnership if a fixed amount of profit is stipulated for one partner. But the intended meaning is voidability as stated by the jurists' texts on partnership, and that is in accordance with the rules (see al-Durr al-Mukhtar 3/344). The jurists often are lax in expression between nullity and voidability, so they use 'voidable' for 'void' and vice versa (see the chapter on voidable sale in al-Durr al-Mukhtar, al-Durar, and al-Hidayah). Professor Shafiq Shahata also pointed out this laxity in expression (see his thesis The Theory of Obligations in Islamic Sharia, p. 163).
Source pages p. 702

Commentary
This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's work discusses the theory of gharar (uncertainty) as a cause of invalidity in contracts. The author begins by defining gharar linguistically as deception, and technically as a contract based on an illusory matter. He cites the prophetic prohibition of 'sale of gharar' as the foundational proof (dalil naqli). The Hanafi school distinguishes two types of gharar: (1) gharar affecting the very existence or essence of the subject matter, which leads to nullity (bāṭil) because the contract is fundamentally impossible to execute; examples include selling an embryo or unspecified future catch. (2) gharar affecting descriptions, quantities, or other subsidiary aspects, which leads to voidability (fāsid) because the contract is valid in essence but defective in a condition. The author provides furū' (applications) from sales and partnerships. In sales, a condition that a cow yields a specific amount of milk is unenforceable (fāsid) due to uncertainty, whereas describing her as 'milking' is a valid description giving the buyer an option upon non-fulfillment. In partnerships, stipulating a fixed profit for one partner renders the contract fāsid because the profit must be shared proportionally. The passage concludes with a valuable terminological note: Hanafi jurists often use 'nullity' (bāṭil) and 'voidability' (fāsid) interchangeably, but the correct doctrine distinguishes them—a point emphasized by later scholars like al-Haskafi and al-Shurunbulali. This careful distinction is crucial for understanding Hanafi contract law.

Key Terms
الغرر (al-gharar): Uncertainty or risk in a contract due to unknown existence, quantity, or description of the subject matter.
الجهالة المفسدة (al-jahālah al-mufsidah): Ignorance that leads to dispute and renders the contract voidable.
بطلان (buṭlān): Nullity: the contract is legally non-existent from the start.
فساد (fasād): Voidability: the contract is defective but can be validated by removing the defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in this passage, what is the legal effect of gharar in the description of the subject matter (e.g., a cow stipulated to yield a specific quantity of milk)?
A) The contract is void (bāṭil) from the start.
B) The contract is voidable (fāsid) and the buyer has a right to rescind if the condition fails.
C) The contract is valid because the description is merely a recommendation.
D) The contract is suspended (mawqūf) pending confirmation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, gharar in the essence of the subject matter voids the contract (bāṭil), while gharar in descriptions or quantities makes it voidable (fāsid) and remediable.

General Causes of Contract Invalidity in Hanafi Fiqh
Overview of general and specific causes of fasād

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three general causes of contract invalidity (fasād) in Hanafi law and distinguish them from specific causes.

Core Idea
General causes of fasād invalidate many contracts; specific causes affect only certain contract types.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's introduction classifies the grounds of contract invalidity into general and specific. The three general causes—duress, vitiating condition, and another—are foundational for a mufti's analysis. Understanding this taxonomy prevents misapplication of rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three general causes mentioned
Read the passage to find the three general causes of fasād. Note that only one (ikrāh) is discussed in detail.
The third cause is not explicitly named; you may infer it from context.
2
Define 'vitiating condition' as a general cause
The text says al-sharṭ al-mufsid invalidates all commutative contracts. Understand its nature as a prohibited restrictive condition.
3
Distinguish general from specific causes
List the specific causes mentioned: co-ownership in pledge, timing in sale, lack of timing in lease, lack of hand-to-hand exchange in currency exchange. Note that these apply only to particular contracts.
4
Apply the classification to a new case
Given a scenario with a defective contract, determine whether the defect is general or specific and cite the relevant cause from this passage.
This trains the mufti's parsing skill.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٥٥) الأسباب العامة لفساد العقد
صرحت بأن العقود الواقعة بالإكراه غير معتبرة، ولكن إذا أجازها العاقد المستكره بعد زوال الإكراه عنه «تعتبر.» فعدم الاعتبار مفاده عدم ثبوت حكم العقد ولو تم تنفيذه. وهذا إنما يتمشى على رأي زفر بالتوقف، لأنَّ العقد الموقوف هو الذي لا يفيد حكمه قبل الإجازة، ولا تنتقل فيه الملكية إذا كان ناقلاً لها ولو تم تنفيذه. أما أبو حنيفة فيقول بانتقال الملكية في العقد المعقود بالإكراه إذا تم تنفيذه ككل عقد فاسد وإنما للمستكره حق الفسخ كما أسلفنا بيانه.
٦/٥٥ - هذه هي الأسباب العامة الثلاثة للفساد في الاجتهاد الحنفي. ويليها في العموم الشرط المفسد، وهو الشرط التقييدي الممنوع الذي تقدم بيانه في بحث الشروط العقدية من نظرية العقود (ف ١٤/٤٣) فإن الشرط المفسد يفسد جميع عقود المعاوضات كالبيع والاستصناع والإجارة والصلح والقسمة والمخارجة، وكذا عقود الشركة والمزارعة والمساقاة.
وما سوى ذلك فأسباب خاصة ينحصر تأثيرها في بعض العقود. فالشيوع مثلاً يفسد الرهن والتوقيت يفسد البيع، وعدم التوقيت يفسد الإجارة، وعدم التقابض في مجلس العقد يفسد عقد الصرف... إلى غير ذلك مما يرجع في معرفته إلى شرائط الصحة في كل عقد على حدة.
Chapter (55): The General Causes of Invalidity of a Contract. [The law] states that contracts made under duress are not considered valid, but if the coerced party ratifies it after the duress ceases, it is considered. So the meaning of non-consideration is that the ruling of the contract is not established even if it is executed. This is only consistent with the opinion of Zufar of suspension, because the suspended contract does not benefit its ruling before ratification, and ownership does not transfer if it is conveying ownership even if executed. As for Abu Hanifa, he says that ownership transfers in a contract made under duress if it is executed, like any invalid contract, but the coerced party has the right of rescission as we previously explained. 6/55 - These are the three general causes of invalidity in Hanafi ijtihad. Following them in generality is the vitiating condition, which is the prohibited restrictive condition previously explained in the discussion of contractual conditions from the theory of contracts (F 14/43). For the vitiating condition invalidates all commutative contracts like sale, istisna', lease, sulh, partition, and mukharaja, and also contracts of partnership, muzara'a, and musaqa. Beyond that are specific causes whose effect is limited to certain contracts. For example, co-ownership invalidates a pledge, timing invalidates a sale, lack of timing invalidates a lease, lack of hand-to-hand exchange in the contract session invalidates a currency exchange... and so on, whose knowledge is referred to the conditions of validity for each contract separately.
Source pages p. 705

Commentary
This passage begins with the ruling on contracts under duress (ikrāh). The Hanafi position is presented with a difference between Zufar and Abu Hanifa. Zufar holds the contract is suspended (mawqūf) and ownership does not transfer at all until ratification. Abu Hanifa holds ownership transfers upon execution, but the coerced party retains the right to rescind (faskh). This is the first general cause of invalidity (fasād). The author then states that there are three general causes in Hanafi law, though only one is detailed here. The vitiating condition (al-sharṭ al-mufsid) is described as a fourth general cause that invalidates all commutative contracts. Beyond these, specific causes apply to particular contracts, such as co-ownership in pledges, timing in sales, and lack of hand-to-hand exchange in currency exchange. The passage emphasizes that these are the general and specific causes a mufti must know.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Compulsion or duress that overcomes a person's will, rendering a contract non-consensual.
الفساد (al-fasād): A defect in a contract that makes it subject to rescission while still being considered concluded.
الشرط المفسد (al-sharṭ al-mufsid): A prohibited restrictive condition that invalidates a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a specific cause of fasād?
A) Vitiating condition
B) Duress
C) Co-ownership in pledge
D) Uncertainty (gharar)

Key Takeaway
General causes of fasād are few and apply broadly; specific causes are numerous and contract-specific.

The Effect of Duress on Contracts: Zufar vs. Abu Hanifa
Intra-Hanafi dispute on duress contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Zufar and Abu Hanifa on contracts made under duress and apply the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
Under duress, ownership transfers immediately (Abu Hanifa) but with right of rescission; Zufar suspends all effects until ratification.

This passage details a key intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a contract concluded under duress can transfer ownership upon execution. Zufar holds the contract is suspended (mawqūf) with no effect until ratification, while Abu Hanifa holds that ownership transfers immediately but the coerced party retains the right to rescind (faskh). Understanding this dispute is crucial for a mufti ruling on forced transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the passage to understand Zufar's view (suspension, no effect) and Abu Hanifa's view (effect with right of rescission).
2
Analyze the legal consequence
Under Abu Hanifa, if executed, ownership transfers but the coerced party can rescind. Under Zufar, ownership does not transfer until ratification.
3
Determine the mufta bihi
While not explicit in the passage, later Hanafi works rely on Abu Hanifa's view. Consider why this might be preferred.
The passage says 'as we previously explained' indicating this is settled elsewhere.
4
Apply to a case
Given a scenario of a sale under duress that has been executed, rule on ownership and the coerced party's options using the mufta bihi view.

الفصل (٥٥) الأسباب العامة لفساد العقد
صرحت بأن العقود الواقعة بالإكراه غير معتبرة، ولكن إذا أجازها العاقد المستكره بعد زوال الإكراه عنه «تعتبر.» فعدم الاعتبار مفاده عدم ثبوت حكم العقد ولو تم تنفيذه. وهذا إنما يتمشى على رأي زفر بالتوقف، لأنَّ العقد الموقوف هو الذي لا يفيد حكمه قبل الإجازة، ولا تنتقل فيه الملكية إذا كان ناقلاً لها ولو تم تنفيذه. أما أبو حنيفة فيقول بانتقال الملكية في العقد المعقود بالإكراه إذا تم تنفيذه ككل عقد فاسد وإنما للمستكره حق الفسخ كما أسلفنا بيانه.

The Rule of the Defective Contract: Effects and Adjustment
Explaining the legal consequences of a defective (fāsid) contract in Hanafi law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for a defective contract and apply it to sales and leases, identifying the deferred effect and the adjustment to fair value.

Core Idea
A defective contract is concluded but its effects are deferred until execution and then adjusted to fair value.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm explains the distinctive Hanafi approach to defective (fāsid) contracts. Unlike the void (bātil) contract which produces no effect, the defective contract is considered concluded but subject to rescission. The author outlines two key consequences: the ruling (e.g., transfer of ownership) is deferred until actual execution, and when it occurs, the reciprocal obligations are adjusted to ensure equivalence. This lesson unpacks these principles and their application to sales, leases, gifts, and other contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two poles of the defective contract
The defective contract is both mun'aqid (concluded) and mustahaqq li-l-faskh (subject to rescission). Understand that these two aspects coexist and produce distinct consequences.
2
Trace the deferral of effect
The ruling (e.g., ownership transfer) does not happen at contract formation but only upon execution (taslim, istifa'). The rationale: prevention is easier than removal (al-daf' ashal min al-raf').
A valid contract gives ownership at the moment of offer and acceptance; a defective contract requires delivery.
3
Apply the adjustment principle
Upon execution, rights are returned to principles of equivalence. For a defective sale: market value at day of receipt. For a defective lease: fair rent, capped by the named rent if specified (unless the defect is ignorance of the rent).
The named rent serves as a maximum (had a'la) due to agreement.
4
Summarize the qawida
The overarching rule: 'The defective contract is subject to rescission, but if executed, its ruling attaches and rights are adjusted to equivalence.' This is a sughra (minor) maxim in contract law.
Refer to Majalla articles 366, 371, 461-462 for codified applications.

Source Text

الفصل السادس والخمسون
نتيجة الفساد، وما إليها
١/٥٦ - رأينا فيما سلف: أنَّ العقد الفاسد يعتبر منعقداً (ف ٧/٥٤). ورأينا في تعريف الفساد : أنه يجعل العقد مستحقاً للفسخ (ف ٩/٥٤). ومن الواضح أن انعقاد العقد يستوجب أثراً له، لأن العقد الذي لا يترتب عليه أثره أصلاً هو الباطل. للفساد واستناداً إلى هذا النظر تقرر في الاجتهاد الحنفي أن فساد العقد ـ بالمعنى الاصطلاحي وهو أنه مرتبة متوسطة بين الصحة والبطلان - نتيجته هي : العقد مستحقاً للفسخ بإرادة كل من الطرفين وبإرادة القاضي أي أنّ العقد الفاسد يفقد قوته الإلزامية. فإذا طولب أحد الطرفين أن يصبح (1) بتنفيذه دفع عن نفسه بالفسخ . فإذا لم يطلب أحد الطرفين الفسخ فإنَّ القاضي يفسخه من تلقاء نفسه دون طلب، كما في حالة البطلان حماية لنظام التعاقد الذي هو من النظام العام، وهذا لا ينافي ترتب الأحكام الأصلية على العقد الفاسد بمقتضى انعقاده فترتب الحكم نتيجة للانعقاد، واستحقاق الفسخ نتيجة للفساد، بحسب التفصيل التالي في هاتين النتيجتين : :أولاً: ترتيب الحكم على العقد الفاسد:

(۱) (ر: البدائع ٣٠٠/٥).
٧٤٩
الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية الفقه
والقبول، كما في البيع الصحيح، بل متى تسلمه فعندئذ يملكه وينفذ تصرفه فيه. وكذا الهبة الفاسدة بالنسبة لامتلاك الموهوب والصلح الفاسد بالنسبة لامتلاك البدل، والقسمة الفاسدة بالنسبة لامتلاك الأقسام، وهلم جراً (ر: المجلة / ٣٦٦ و ٣٧١). فعلاً، لا وفي الإجارة الفاسدة يثبت حكمها وتلزم الأجرة باستيفاء المنفعة العقد (المجلة ٤٧١). بمجرد ووجهة نظر الفقهاء في تأخير ثبوت حكم العقد الفاسد إلى تاريخ التنفيذ أنه مادام مستحقاً للفسخ، وهو عرضة للإبطال - حتى بإرادة القاضي ولو لم يطلب إبطاله أحد الطرفين - فليس من المصلحة الشرعية تعجيل بناء حكم على عقد يوجب الشرع نقضه لأن الدفع أسهل من الرفع. ولكن إذا وقع التنفيذ والعقد منعقد، لم يبق مناص من إثبات حكم العقد (ر: الدرر شرح الغرر باب البيع الفاسد ١٧٤/٢).
٣/٥٦ - غير أن هذا الحكم الذي يثبت في العقد الفاسد عند تنفيذه لا تثبت فيه الحقوق المتقابلة بحدودها التي حددها العقد، بل يرد الفساد هذه الحقوق المتقابلة إلى قواعدها الأصلية التي توجب التعادل في التبادل، لأن الحدود الاتفاقية في العقد قد فسدت بفساده (ر: البدائع ٢١٨/٤). وعلى هذا قرروا في البيع الفاسد أنه إذا تم تنفيذه بالتسليم حتى انتقلت إلى المشتري ملكية المبيع، وجبت عليه قيمته يوم القبض. والقيمة هي سعر السوق لا ثمنه الاتفاقي المسمى في العقد (ر: المجلة / ۳۷۱). وفي الإجارة الفاسدة يستحق المؤجر بتنفيذها أجر المثل كقيمة للمنافع المستوفاة، لا الأجر المسمى في العقد
(۱) لكنهم في فساد الإجارة قد اعتبروا الأجر المسمى حداً أعلى بسبب الاتفاق عليه. فيجب أجر المثل على أن لا يتجاوز به الأجر المسمى إلا إذا كان فساد الإجارة ناشئاً عن جهالة الأجرة بسبب عدم تحديدها في العقد، فيجب فيها عندئذ أجر المثل بالغاً ما بلغ (ر : المجلة / ٤٦١ - ٤٦٢) .
Chapter Fifty-Six: Result of Invalidity and What Pertains to It
1/56 - We saw previously: that the defective contract is considered concluded (para 7/54). And we saw in the definition of invalidity: that it makes the contract subject to rescission (para 9/54). And it is clear that the conclusion of a contract entails an effect for it, because a contract that produces no effect at all is void. Based on this view, it was established in Hanafi ijtihad that the invalidity of a contract - in its technical sense, that it is an intermediate rank between validity and voidness - its result is: the contract becomes subject to rescission by the will of each of the two parties and by the will of the judge, i.e., the defective contract loses its binding force. So if one of the parties demands its enforcement, he defends himself by rescission. If neither party demands rescission, the judge rescinds it on his own without request, as in the case of voidness, to protect the contracting system which is part of public order. This does not contradict the attachment of original rulings to the defective contract by virtue of its conclusion: so the ruling attaches as a result of conclusion, and the entitlement to rescission as a result of invalidity, according to the following detail in these two results: First: Attaching the ruling to the defective contract:
2/56 - As for attaching the ruling to the defective contract by virtue of its conclusion, it is not established merely by the contract as in a valid contract; rather it is deferred until the execution of the defective contract. So if it is a defective sale, for example, the buyer does not own the sold item upon completion of offer and acceptance, as in a valid sale, but rather when he takes delivery, then he owns it and his disposition in it is effective. Likewise for a defective gift regarding ownership of the gifted item, a defective settlement regarding ownership of the substitute, a defective partition regarding ownership of the portions, and so on (see: Majalla 366, 371). In a defective lease, its ruling is established and the rent becomes obligatory upon actual utilization of the benefit, not upon the contract (Majalla 471). And the jurists' viewpoint in deferring the establishment of the ruling of a defective contract to the date of execution is that as long as it is subject to rescission, and it is liable to annulment - even by the judge's will even if neither party requests its annulment - it is not in the public interest to hasten building a ruling on a contract that the Law commands to be broken, because prevention is easier than removal. But if execution occurs while the contract is concluded, there is no escape from establishing the ruling of the contract (see: Durar Sharh al-Ghurar, chapter on defective sale, 2/174).
3/56 - However, this ruling that is established in the defective contract upon its execution does not establish reciprocal rights within the limits specified by the contract; rather, invalidity returns these reciprocal rights to their original principles that require equivalence in exchange, because the agreed-upon limits have become invalid due to its invalidity (see: Bada'i' 4/218). Accordingly, they ruled in a defective sale that if it is executed by delivery so that ownership of the sold item is transferred to the buyer, its value on the day of receipt is due from him. And the value is the market price, not the agreed price named in the contract (see: Majalla 371). And in a defective lease, the lessor is entitled by its execution to the fair rent as the value of the benefits received, not the rent named in the contract.
(1) However, in invalidity of lease, they considered the named rent as a maximum due to agreement. So the fair rent is due provided that it does not exceed the named rent, unless the invalidity of the lease arises from ignorance of the rent due to its non-specification in the contract, in which case fair rent is due whatever it may be (see: Majalla 461-462).
Source pages p. 706

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the Hanafi legal rule regarding defective (fāsid) contracts. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by clarifying the preliminary concepts: a defective contract is considered concluded (mun'aqid) but is subject to rescission (mustahaqq li-l-faskh). He emphasizes that a contract producing no effect at all is void, not defective. The key result of defectiveness is that the contract loses its binding force; either party can rescind it, and if they don't, the judge must rescind it ex officio to protect the public order of contracting. However, this does not negate that the defective contract may produce some effects if it is executed (i.e., performed). The author then explains the first consequence: the ruling of the contract (e.g., transfer of ownership) does not occur at the moment of contract formation (as in a valid contract) but is deferred until actual execution (taslim, taking delivery, or utilization of benefit). The rationale given is that since the contract is subject to rescission, it is not in the public interest to accelerate legal effects on a fragile contract; prevention is easier than removal. But once the contract is executed, there is no choice but to give it effect. The second consequence is that the reciprocal rights established upon execution are not the terms agreed in the contract, but are adjusted to ensure equivalence. For a defective sale, the buyer must pay the market value on the day of receipt, not the contracted price. For a defective lease, the lessor receives fair rent (ujrat al-mithl), with the nuance (from the footnote) that if the rent was specified in the contract, the fair rent is capped by that amount, unless the defect was due to uncertainty about the rent, in which case the fair rent is unlimited. These rules reflect the Hanafi principle that the defective contract is not entirely without effect, but its effects are modified to prevent exploitation and uphold justice. This passage is an excellent example of Hanafi legal reasoning (ta'lil) and the application of maxims like "الدفع أسهل من الرفع" (prevention is easier than removal).

Key Terms
فساد (fāsad): In Hanafi terminology, a defect in a contract that makes it subject to rescission but not void; an intermediate rank between validity and voidness.
عقد فاسد (ʿaqd fāsid): A contract that is concluded (mun'aqid) but due to a defect in a condition or pillar (except those preventing conclusion) is subject to rescission; its effects are deferred until execution and then adjusted.
قيمة يوم القبض (qīmat yawm al-qabḍ): The market value of the sold item on the day the buyer takes delivery, which replaces the agreed price in a defective sale.
أجر المثل (ujrat al-mithl): The fair market rent for a leased benefit, used when the contract is defective or the rent is not specified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a Hanafi defective sale (bay' fāsid), when does the buyer acquire ownership of the sold item, and what price must he pay?
A) Ownership transfers at the moment of contract; he pays the agreed price.
B) Ownership transfers upon delivery; he pays the market value on the day of delivery.
C) Ownership never transfers; the contract is void.
D) Ownership transfers upon delivery; he pays the agreed price.

Key Takeaway
A defective (fāsid) contract is concluded but not binding; if executed, its effects are adjusted to ensure fair equivalence.

The General Principle of Corrupt Contracts: Execution Triggers Effects, Balance Restored
Explaining the general Hanafi rule that corrupt contracts become effective only upon execution, with rights adjusted to market value.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that corrupt contracts produce effects only upon execution and that reciprocal rights are adjusted to market value, applying the maxim 'corruption returns rights to balance'.

Core Idea
A corrupt contract's effects are deferred until execution; then market value replaces agreed price to restore balance.

In Hanafi law, a corrupt (fasid) contract differs from a valid one: it does not transfer ownership or obligations immediately. Instead, if the parties execute it, the contract's rulings take effect, but the agreed-upon considerations are set aside and replaced by market-equivalent values. This lesson introduces the foundational maxim and the rationale for deferral.

Guided Path
1
Identify the timing of effects
Understand that a corrupt contract does not produce legal effects at formation, only upon execution (e.g., delivery or receipt of benefits). The text explains this via the analogy of sale, gift, compromise, and partition.
The deferral is based on the contract being subject to rescission.
2
Recall the corrective maxim
The key maxim: 'يرد الفساد الحقوق المتقابلة إلى قواعدها الأصلية التي توجب التعادل في التبادل' (corruption returns reciprocal rights to their original principles requiring balance in exchange). This is the guiding principle for adjusting rights.
3
Apply the maxim to corrupt sale
In a corrupt sale, once the buyer receives the item, he must pay the market value at the time of receipt, not the agreed price. The value is determined by the market, not the contract.
Reference: Majallah / 371.
4
Distinguish from valid contract
In a valid sale, ownership transfers upon contract formation and the agreed price is due. In a corrupt sale, ownership transfers only upon delivery, and the price is replaced by market value.
This reflects the principle of preventing unjust enrichment.

Source Text

الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية الفقه
والقبول، كما في البيع الصحيح، بل متى تسلمه فعندئذ يملكه وينفذ تصرفه فيه. وكذا الهبة الفاسدة بالنسبة لامتلاك الموهوب والصلح الفاسد بالنسبة لامتلاك البدل، والقسمة الفاسدة بالنسبة لامتلاك الأقسام، وهلم جراً (ر: المجلة / ٣٦٦ و ٣٧١).
فعلاً، لا
وفي الإجارة الفاسدة يثبت حكمها وتلزم الأجرة باستيفاء المنفعة بمجرد [؟] ووجهة نظر الفقهاء في تأخير ثبوت حكم العقد الفاسد إلى تاريخ التنفيذ أنه مادام مستحقاً للفسخ، وهو عرضة للإبطال - حتى بإرادة القاضي ولو لم يطلب إبطاله أحد الطرفين - فليس من المصلحة الشرعية تعجيل بناء حكم على عقد يوجب الشرع نقضه لأن الدفع أسهل من الرفع. ولكن إذا وقع التنفيذ والعقد منعقد، لم يبق مناص من إثبات حكم العقد (ر: الدرر شرح الغرر باب البيع الفاسد ١٧٤/٢).
٣/٥٦ - غير أن هذا الحكم الذي يثبت في العقد الفاسد عند تنفيذه لا تثبت فيه الحقوق المتقابلة بحدودها التي حددها العقد، بل يرد الفساد هذه الحقوق المتقابلة إلى قواعدها الأصلية التي توجب التعادل في التبادل،
لأن الحدود الاتفاقية في العقد قد فسدت بفساده (ر: البدائع ٢١٨/٤). وعلى هذا قرروا في البيع الفاسد أنه إذا تم تنفيذه بالتسليم حتى انتقلت إلى المشتري ملكية المبيع، وجبت عليه قيمته يوم القبض. والقيمة هي سعر السوق لا ثمنه الاتفاقي المسمى في العقد (ر: المجلة / ۳۷۱). وفي الإجارة الفاسدة يستحق المؤجر بتنفيذها أجر المثل كقيمة للمنافع المستوفاة، لا الأجر المسمى في العقد
(1)
(۱) لكنهم في فساد الإجارة قد اعتبروا الأجر المسمى حداً أعلى بسبب الاتفاق عليه. فيجب أجر المثل على أن لا يتجاوز به الأجر المسمى إلا إذا كان فساد الإجارة ناشئاً عن جهالة الأجرة بسبب عدم تحديدها في العقد، فيجب فيها عندئذ أجر المثل
بالغاً ما بلغ (ر : المجلة / ٤٦١ - ٤٦٢) .
الفصل (٥٦) نتيجة الفساد وما إليها
وفي الشركة الفاسدة إذا كانت شركة أموال، فالربح الحاصل فيها يستحقه الشركاء بنسبة حصصهم في رأس المال لا بحسب اتفاقهم في عقد الشركة (ر: المجلة / ١٣٦٨).
وإذا كان رأس المال من أحد الطرفين كما في شركة المضاربة إذا فسدت فالربح لرب المال وللمضارب أجر مثل عمله، على أن لا يتجاوز المقدار المشروط له في العقد (ر : المجلة / ١٤٢٦).
٤/٥٦ - ملاحظة : في عقد الرهن لا فرق بين صحيح وفاسد.
تستوي في نظر فقهائنا نتيجة الفساد والصحة في عقد الرهن.
فقد صرَّحوا بأنَّ الرهن الفاسد تثبت فيه - بالنسبة إلى الاستدانة التي تمت بسببه - أحكام الرهن الصحيح من حق احتباس المرهون، والامتياز
وهذا بخلاف قيمة المبيع في البيع الفاسد، فإنها تثبت ولو تجاوزت الثمن المسمى فيه . ويعلل فقهاء الحنفية هذا التفريق منهم بين البيع والإجارة الفاسدين بأن الشيء المبيع ذو قيمة بذاته. فتجب في حالة الفساد قيمته المعادلة مهما بلغت.
غير
أما المنافع فهي في الاجتهاد الحنفي غير متقومة في ذاتها، وإنما يقومها العقد. فإذا حدد الطرفان الأجر باتفاقهما لا يجوز تجاوزه (ر: البدائع آخر فصل حكم الإجارة ٢١٨/٤). النظرية الحنفية في عدم تقوم المنافع بذاتها هي نظرية ضعيفة. وهي عرضة للنقد المر في المذاهب الاجتهادية الأخرى التي تعتبر المنافع كالأعيان في التقوم الذاتي، كما سنرى في الجزء الثالث من هذه السلسلة الفقهية (المدخل إلى نظرية الالتزام العامة) في بحث العين والمنفعة من فصل الأموال.
وقد كان ينبغي في فساد العقود أن جميع يعتبر في الحقوق الثابتة فيها ما حدده العاقدان نظير ما قرروا في فساد الإجارة، لأن فساد العقد لا يعدم الرضا بالحقوق المتقابلة المحددة فيه، اللهم إلا إذا كان فساد العقد ناشئاً عن جهالة البدل، أو عن الإكراه عند من يرى الإكراه مفسداً للعقد لا موقفاً لحكمه، فعندئذ يجب أن يثبت العوض المعادل في العقد الفاسد بالغاً ما بلغ. وقد علمت أن نظرية الفساد بكل ما فيها من قواعد وأحكام هي اجتهادية حنفية المنشأ فهي أكثر من غيرها قابلية للتعديل بحسب المصلحة أو اختلاف الأنظار التي يعود تقديرها للاجتهاد ولولي الأمر استناداً إلى رأي الفقهاء الثقات.
Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Endorsements. Acceptance, as in a valid sale: whenever he receives it, he owns it and his disposition in it is effective. Likewise, a corrupt gift regarding ownership of the gifted item, a corrupt compromise regarding ownership of the substitute, a corrupt partition regarding ownership of the shares, and so on (ref: Majallah / 366 and 371). Actually, no. And in a corrupt lease, its ruling is established and the rent becomes obligatory upon receipt of the benefit merely [؟]. The jurists' viewpoint in deferring the establishment of the ruling of a corrupt contract to the date of execution is that as long as it is subject to rescission, and it is liable to annulment—even by the judge's will even if neither party requests its annulment—it is not in the interest of Sharia to hasten building a ruling upon a contract that Sharia requires to be voided, because prevention is easier than removal. But if execution occurs while the contract is concluded, there is no escape from establishing the contract's ruling (ref: Durar Sharh al-Ghurar, chapter on corrupt sale, 2/174). 3/56 - However, this ruling that is established in the corrupt contract upon its execution does not establish reciprocal rights within the limits set by the contract; rather, corruption returns these reciprocal rights to their original principles that require balance in exchange, because the contractual limits have been corrupted by its corruption (ref: Bada'i' 4/218). Accordingly, they ruled in corrupt sale that if it is executed by delivery such that ownership of the sold item transfers to the buyer, its value on the day of receipt is due from him. The value is the market price, not the agreed price named in the contract (ref: Majallah / 371). And in a corrupt lease, the lessor is entitled upon its execution to a fair rental value for the benefits received, not the named rent in the contract (1) (1) But in corrupt lease, they considered the named rent a maximum due to agreement. So fair rental is due on condition that it does not exceed the named rent, unless the corruption of the lease arises from ignorance of the rent due to its non-specification in the contract; then fair rental is due no matter how high (ref: Majallah / 461-462). Chapter (56): Result of Corruption and Related Matters. In a corrupt partnership if it is a partnership of capital, the profit obtained therein is owned by the partners in proportion to their shares of capital, not according to their agreement in the partnership contract (ref: Majallah / 1368). And if the capital is from one party as in a mudaraba partnership if it becomes corrupt, the profit is for the capital owner, and the mudarib gets a fair wage for his work, on condition that it does not exceed the amount stipulated for him in the contract (ref: Majallah / 1426). 4/56 - Note: In a mortgage contract, there is no difference between valid and corrupt. In the view of our jurists, the result of corruption and validity are equal in a mortgage contract. They explicitly stated that a corrupt mortgage establishes—with respect to the loan that occurred by means of it—the rulings of a valid mortgage: the right to retain the mortgaged property and the priority. And this is contrary to the value of the sold item in a corrupt sale, for it is established even if it exceeds the named price therein. The Hanafi jurists explain this distinction between corrupt sale and lease by saying that the sold item has inherent value. So in case of corruption, its equivalent value is due no matter how high. As for benefits, in Hanafi ijtihad they are not inherently valuable; rather, the contract values them. So if the two parties set the rent by agreement, it may not be exceeded (ref: Bada'i', end of chapter on ruling of lease, 4/218). The Hanafi theory that benefits are not inherently valuable is a weak theory, and is subject to severe criticism in other ijtihad schools that consider benefits like physical assets in inherent valuation, as we will see in the third part of this jurisprudential series (Introduction to the General Theory of Obligations) in the discussion of 'ayn and manfa'ah from the chapter on property. It would have been appropriate in corrupt contracts that all rights established therein should consider what the contracting parties determined, similar to what they ruled in corrupt lease, because corruption of a contract does not nullify consent to the reciprocal rights specified therein, unless the corruption arises from ignorance of the substitute, or from coercion according to those who consider coercion as corrupting the contract not suspending its ruling; then the equivalent consideration in the corrupt contract must be established no matter how high. And you have known that the theory of corruption with all its rules and rulings is of Hanafi origin, so it is more than others susceptible to modification according to benefit or difference of views, whose appreciation returns to ijtihad and the ruler based on the opinion of trustworthy jurists.
Source pages p. 707

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal effects of invalid (fasid) contracts in Hanafi law. The author explains that unlike valid contracts where ownership and rights transfer immediately upon contract formation, an invalid contract does not produce legal effects until actual execution (e.g., delivery of goods or receipt of benefits). The rationale is that since the contract is inherently defective and subject to annulment, Sharia postpones its effects until execution to avoid creating rights that would need to be undone. Once execution occurs, the contract's rulings are established, but the reciprocal rights are adjusted to restore balance: instead of the agreed-upon consideration, the market value or equivalent is imposed. For example, in a corrupt sale, the buyer must pay the market value at the time of receipt, not the agreed price. In a corrupt lease, the lessor gets fair rental value, but capped by the named rent (except when the corruption is due to ignorance of the rent, then no cap applies). Similar rules apply to partnership, mudaraba, and gift contracts. A notable exception is mortgage, where corrupt and valid are treated identically regarding the right to retain the property and priority. The author critically notes that the Hanafi view that benefits are not inherently valuable is weak and criticized by other schools, who treat benefits like physical assets. He suggests that ideally, the agreed consideration should be respected in corrupt contracts unless the defect involves ignorance or coercion. The passage concludes that the Hanafi theory of corruption, being jurisprudential, is flexible and can be adapted by ijtihad or state authority based on the opinions of reliable scholars.

Key Terms
عقد فاسد (‘aqd fāsid): A contract that is defective in its essence (e.g., missing a condition) but not void; it can become effective upon execution with adjusted rights.
قيمة (qīmah): Market value at the time of receipt, not the agreed price, imposed in corrupt sale to restore balance.
أجر المثل (ajr al-mithl): Fair or comparable rent/compensation for services or benefits, determined by market rates.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi maxim, what happens to reciprocal rights in a corrupt contract that is executed?
A) They are enforced exactly as agreed in the contract
B) They are returned to their original principles requiring balance, so market value replaces agreed terms
C) The contract is void and no rights arise
D) The judge decides the rights arbitrarily

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim that corruption returns rights to balance governs all corrupt contracts, overriding agreed terms with market values upon execution.

The Rule: Ratification Does Not Cure a Defective Contract
Stating a rule with exception and supporting maxim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a defective contract cannot be validated by ratification, and apply the exception when the cause of defect is removed during the contract session.

Core Idea
Ratification cannot cure a defective contract; only removing the defect itself can.

In Hanafi contract law, a distinction is made between a void (batil) and a defective (fasid) contract. While a fasid contract is concluded, it is impaired due to a violation of the contract's systemic requirements. A common misconception is that simply agreeing later (ijazah) can fix the defect. This lesson clarifies that ratification alone is insufficient, but the defect can be cured if its cause is removed during the contract session. The underlying maxim 'إذا زال المانع عاد الممنوع' governs this exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main rule
The passage states that the invalidity (fasad) of a contract cannot be cured by the ratification of one or both parties. Understand why: the defect arises from violating the contract system, not from mere objection by a party.
2
Understand the rationale
The reason ratification does not work is that the defect is objective – it contravenes the system laid down by the Lawgiver. No one has the authority to approve such a violation.
3
Identify the exception
The text then gives an exception: if the cause of the defect is removed during the contract session, the fasad disappears and the contract becomes valid. Examples include waiving a prohibited condition or specifying an unknown term.
Removal must occur in the same session (majlis al-aqd).
4
Recognize the supporting maxim
The exception is justified by the maxim 'إذا زال المانع عاد الممنوع' (If the obstacle is removed, the prevented thing returns). This is a subsidiary legal principle (qāʿida sughrā) applied in contract law.
5
Apply to examples
Work through the examples: (a) sale with unknown price – if price is specified in session, sale becomes valid. (b) partnership with unknown profit shares – if shares are specified, partnership becomes valid.

Source Text

الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه
٨/٥٦ - الفساد لا يرتفع بالإجازة :
هذا، ويجب أن يلحظ أن فساد العقد كبطلانه لا يرتفع بإجازة أحد العاقدين أو كليهما، لأنه ناشيء عن مخالفة نظام العقد، وليس لأحد أن يُقرّ هذه المخالفة. بل يبقى العقد الفاسد مستحقاً للفسخ ولو أجازه المتعاقدان :
ولكن الفساد يمكن أن يزول بإزالة سببه كما لو كان الفساد لشرط ممنوع مفسد فتخلى الطرفان عن الشرط في مجلس العقد وكذا لو كان الفساد لجهالة أحد العوضين في البيع، أو لجهالة نسبة الأرباح في الشركة، فعيَّن الطرفان العوض أو الربح المجهول فعندئذ يزول الفساد وينقلب العقد صحيحاً؛ فإنَّ من القواعد المقررة أنه إذا زال المانع عاد الممنوع» (ر: ف .(۷/۲۸
Chapter Seven: The Theory of Legal Invalidities in Fiqh
8/56 - Invalidity cannot be cured by ratification:
This, and it must be noted that the invalidity of a contract, like its nullity, cannot be cured by the ratification of one or both contracting parties, because it arises from violating the contract system, and no one may confirm this violation. Rather, the defective contract remains subject to rescission even if the two contracting parties ratify it.
But invalidity can be removed by removing its cause, as if the invalidity is due to a prohibited corrupting condition, and the two parties waive the condition in the contract session; likewise if the invalidity is due to ignorance of one of the two considerations in a sale, or ignorance of the proportion of profits in a partnership, and the two parties specify the unknown consideration or profit, then at that point the invalidity disappears and the contract becomes valid; for among the established rules is: 'If the obstacle is removed, what was prevented returns.' (See: F, 7/28)
Source pages p. 713

Commentary
This passage states a key rule of Hanafi contract law: a defective contract (فاسد) cannot become valid simply by the parties' subsequent approval (إجازة). The defect arises from violating the contract's systemic requirements, so mere agreement cannot cure it. However, the rule has an exception: if the cause of the defect is removed during the contract session, the defect disappears and the contract becomes valid. Examples include waiving a prohibited condition or specifying unknown terms (price, profit share). The underlying maxim is 'إذا زال المانع عاد الممنوع' (When the obstacle is removed, the prevented thing returns), which is a subsidiary principle applied in contract law.

Key Terms
فساد (fasad): A defect in a contract that makes it voidable; the contract is concluded but impaired.
إجازة (ijazah): Ratification or approval by a contracting party after the contract is made.
بطلان (butlan): Nullity; a contract that is void ab initio.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for a defective contract to become valid?
A) Ratification by both parties
B) Removal of the cause of invalidity in the contract session
C) A new contract
D) A court order

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a defective contract cannot be cured by mere ratification; only removal of the defect's cause during the contract session can make it valid.

The Qawida on Capacity: Financial Donations vs. Acts of Worship
Classification of acts based on required capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida on capacity requirements to classify acts of a minor and determine whether complete or deficient capacity is required.

Core Idea
Financial donations need full capacity; acts of worship need only discernment.

In Hanafi fiqh, the legal capacity (ahliyyah) of a person determines the validity of their actions. This lesson introduces a key legal maxim (qawida) that distinguishes between acts requiring complete capacity (kamil ahliyyat al-ada) and those where deficient capacity (qasirah) suffices. Understanding this maxim is essential for a mufti when advising on the validity of acts performed by minors.

Guided Path
1
State the Qawida
Identify the precise Arabic wording of the maxim and its English translation. This qawida is a minor (sughra) legal principle that governs the capacity requirements for different types of acts.
The qawida is 'التبرعات المالية تحتاج إلى كمال الأهلية، والعبادات تكفي فيها أهلية الأداء القاصرة'.
2
Analyze the Rationale (Ta'lil)
Understand the reasoning behind the classification: financial donations are a pure loss, so complete capacity is needed to protect the individual's wealth; acts of worship benefit the doer spiritually, so deficient capacity (discernment) suffices.
The text states 'لأنها خسارة مالية محضة في حق الفاعل'.
3
Apply the Qawida to Furu' (Branches)
Take concrete examples from the furuat provided: gift (hiba) and endowment (waqf) require complete capacity; prayer (salah) and fasting require only a discerning minor.
The furuat are listed in the qawida panel.
4
Identify the Istithna' (Exception)
Note the exception: acquisition of permissible things (mubahat) by a discerning minor is valid even without complete capacity, because the minor can own them by intention like an adult.
The footnote from Ibn Nujaym clarifies this exception.
5
Apply as a Mufti
In a fatwa scenario, first determine whether the minor is discerning. Then classify the act: if it is a financial donation, require baligh and rashid; if it is worship, accept the act as valid.
Always check the minor's age and rationality.

هذا القبيل أيضاً جميع العبادات الدينية من صلاة وصيام وحج
ثم إن هذه الأفعال من حيث درجة الأهلية المطلوبة فيها نوعان: أ - فمنها ما يشترط له كمال أهلية الأداء بأن يكون الفاعل بالغاً ،راشداً، وذلك كالتبرعات المالية من هبة ووقف ونحوهما، لأنها خسارة مالية محضة في حق الفاعل فيجب لصحة ممارسته لها أن يكون متمتعاً بكمال أهليته احتياطاً في صيانة أمواله وحقوقه.

وتفصيل ذلك في الفصول التالية ٦٠ - ٦٤ .
(۱) بخلاف الصغير المميز، فإنه يملك ما يحرزه من المباحات عن قصد كالكبير الراشد ر الأشباه والنظائر لابن نجيم في أحكام الصبيان ١٤٥/٢).
Chapter (59): The basis of capacity, its definition and its types.
... in his hand from it (1), etc., and other than that.
And of this kind also are all religious acts of worship such as prayer, fasting, and Hajj.
Then these acts, in terms of the degree of capacity required for them, are of two types: A) Those for which complete capacity of performance is required, such that the doer must be an adult, rational. That includes financial donations such as gift, endowment, and the like, because they are a pure financial loss on the part of the doer, so for the validity of his practice of them, he must be in possession of complete capacity, as a precaution to safeguard his wealth and rights.
B) Those for which deficient capacity of performance suffices, such that the doer must be discerning (mumayyiz), i.e., conscious, as in religious acts of worship and some civil contracts and dispositions; so if the discerning minor performs them, they are valid from him.
Detail of that is in the following chapters 60-64.
(1) Unlike the discerning minor, for he owns whatever he acquires of permissible things intentionally, like the rational adult. (Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir by Ibn Nujaym on rulings of minors, 2/145).
Source pages p. 743

Commentary
This passage is a legal classification of acts according to the degree of capacity (ahliyyah) required. The author, a Hanafi scholar, presents two categories. The first category includes acts that require complete capacity (kamil ahliyyat al-ada), meaning the actor must be an adult (baligh) and rational (rashid). Examples are financial donations like gifts (hiba) and endowments (waqf). The reason given is that these involve pure financial loss, so to protect the individual's wealth and rights, full capacity is needed. The second category includes acts where deficient capacity (qasirah) suffices, meaning the actor only needs to be discerning (mumayyiz), i.e., having the ability to distinguish. Acts of worship such as prayer, fasting, and Hajj fall into this category, as well as some civil contracts. If a discerning minor performs them, it is valid. The footnote clarifies that a discerning minor can own what he acquires of permissible things intentionally, like an adult. This classification is foundational in Hanafi fiqh for determining the legal capacity of minors and the validity of their actions.

Key Terms
أهلية الأداء (ahliyyat al-ada): The capacity to perform legal acts; either complete (kamilah) or deficient (qasirah) as classified here.
مميز (mumayyiz): A child who has reached the age of discernment (tamyeez), able to understand the nature of acts and their consequences, typically around age 7.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following acts requires complete capacity (kamil ahliyyat al-ada) according to this passage?
A) Performing salah
B) Giving a gift (hiba)
C) Fasting in Ramadan
D) Performing Hajj

Key Takeaway
Financial donations require complete capacity (baligh and rational), while acts of worship and acquisition of mubahat are valid from a discerning minor.

Criminal vs Civil Acts of a Child: Liability Distinction
Critical refinement of a maxim on child's liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between criminal and civil acts of a child to determine liability, and correctly interpret the maxim 'restriction applies to statements not acts'.

Core Idea
A child's criminal act incurs liability; his civil act does not — but the owner may bear the loss.

A common legal question is whether a child is liable for damages caused by his acts. The answer depends on the nature of the act: criminal (direct transgression) or civil (based on a contract). The Hanafi school uses the maxim 'restriction applies to statements not acts', but it is often misunderstood. This lesson refines that maxim by showing it refers only to criminal acts, while civil acts follow contract validity rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act type
Determine whether the child's act is criminal (e.g., taking or destroying property on his own) or civil (e.g., receiving property under a contract).
Criminal acts are direct violations of another's right; civil acts arise from a transaction.
2
Analyze the capacity required
Criminal liability only requires ahliyyat al-wujūb (passive capacity), present from birth. Civil acts require ahliyyat al-adā' (active capacity), acquired at puberty.
This explains why even a non-discerning child is liable for criminal acts.
3
Apply the maxim correctly
The maxim 'الحجر يقع على الأقوال دون الأفعال' means the child's statements and contracts are void, but his criminal acts incur liability. It does NOT mean all acts are valid or liable.
The maxim is restricted to criminal acts; civil acts are governed separately.
4
Handle the civil act exception
For civil acts, if the child destroys what he received, he is not liable because the owner enabled the loss by delivering the property (owner is the cause).
The owner's delivery is considered the cause of loss, not the child's act.
5
Consider the edge case
If both parties are children (e.g., a child deposits with another child), the depositary child is liable because the depositor's empowerment is invalid, so the destruction is a pure crime.
This nuance shows the principle is consistent: absence of valid contract makes the act criminal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦١ / ٦ - وعلى هذا يجب التمييز في ضمان الأفعال التي يباشرها الطفل بين الأفعال الجنائية التي يجني بها على حق أو نفس، وبين الأفعال المدنية : فأما الأفعال الجنائية كأخذ الطفل من تلقاء نفسه مال الغير أو إتلافه فهي مضمونة عليه، لأن الأفعال الجنائية لا تتوقف سببيتها للضمان على أهلية الأداء، بل على أهلية الوجوب فقط . وأما الأفعال المدنية كتسلم مبيع أو قرض أو وديعة سلمها إليه صاحبها بناء على عقد، فإنها تعتبر من الطفل باطلة، وإذا أتلف الطفل فيها ما تسلمه لا يضمنه لأنَّ المالك هو المفرط المسلّط للطفل على إتلافها بتسليمه إياها (۱) . وهذا هو مراد الفقهاء في قولهم : إنَّ الحجر يقع على الأقوال دون الأفعال، ويفرعون عليه أنَّ أقوال الطفل وعقوده باطلة مهدرة، أما أفعاله فتوجب عليه الضمان ( ر ) م / ٩٤١ والدر المختار كتاب الحجر ٨٩/٥ و ۹۱)، فإن مقصودهم هو أفعال الطفل الجنائية، أما الأفعال المدنية، فإنها تخضع لقاعدة الصحة والبطلان كما تقدّم في بحث البطلان (ر: ف ٥١/ ه، فالطفل غير مسؤول فيها بالضمان بدليل هذه الأحكام التي نقلناها إتلافه لما سلم إليه عن بيع أو إقراض أو استعارة إلخ ... (۱) وعلى هذا لو كان المودع أيضاً طفلاً كالوديع فأتلف الطفل الوديع الوديعة يضمنها لعدم صحة التسليط من الطفل المودع فبقي الإتلاف وحده جناية من الطفل على مال غيره فتوجب الضمان (ر) الأشباه والنظائر لابن نجيم الفن الثاني، كتاب الحجر ج ۲ ص ۷۸ - ۷۹).
61/6 - Based on this, one must distinguish in the liability of acts performed by a child between criminal acts by which he transgresses against a right or person, and civil acts. As for criminal acts, such as a child taking another's property on his own accord or destroying it, they are subject to liability from him, because the causal basis of criminal acts for liability does not depend on capacity of execution, but only on capacity of obligation. As for civil acts, such as receiving a sold item, a loan, or a deposit handed to him by its owner based on a contract, they are considered void from the child, and if the child destroys what he received, he does not guarantee it because the owner is the one who caused the loss by empowering the child to destroy it by delivering it to him (1). This is the intent of the jurists in their statement: 'Restriction applies to statements, not acts,' and they derive from it that the child's statements and contracts are void and disregarded, whereas his acts obligate him to liability (see: M/941, al-Durr al-Mukhtar, Kitab al-Hajr, 5/89 and 91). So their intent is the child's criminal acts; as for civil acts, they are subject to the rule of validity and invalidity as previously mentioned in the discussion of invalidity (see: F/51). Thus the child is not responsible for liability in them, as evidenced by these rulings we have reported: his destruction of what was delivered to him through sale, lending, borrowing, etc... (1) Based on this, if the depositor was also a child, like the depositary, and the child depositary destroyed the deposit, he guarantees it because the empowerment by the child depositor is invalid, so the destruction alone remains a crime by the child against another's property, thus obligating liability (see: al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir by Ibn Nujaym, second art, Kitab al-Hajr, vol. 2, pp. 78-79).
Source pages p. 751

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on a child's liability. The author distinguishes between criminal acts (like theft or destruction) and civil acts (like receiving property under a contract). Criminal acts always entail liability because they only require the passive capacity to have obligations (ahliyyat al-wujud). Civil acts are void because they require active capacity (ahliyyat al-ada'). The maxim 'restriction applies to statements not acts' is clarified: it does not mean all acts are valid; it means criminal acts are liable, while civil acts follow contract validity rules. The footnote adds that if both depositor and depositary are children, the depositary is liable because the child depositor's empowerment is invalid, so the destruction is a crime.

Key Terms
أهلية الوجوب (ahliyyat al-wujūb): Passive legal capacity: the capacity to have rights and obligations imposed upon one, present from birth.
أهلية الأداء (ahliyyat al-adā'): Active legal capacity: the capacity to perform legally valid acts, acquired at puberty.
الطفل (al-ṭifl): A minor who has not reached puberty, including both discerning and non-discerning.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or damage, typically monetary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a child (non-pubescent) voluntarily takes and destroys another person's mobile phone, is he liable for its value according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because criminal acts always incur liability.
B) No, because children are not liable for any acts.
C) Yes, but only if he is a discerning child.
D) No, because the owner was negligent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes criminal from civil acts of a child: criminal acts incur liability based on passive capacity, while civil acts do not because the owner's delivery is the cause of loss.

Applying Legal Maxims: The Discerning Minor's Will and the Mufti's Tarjih
Dispute on a minor's will with author's critical refinement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a Hanafi ruling should be followed as stated or revised based on legal principles, using the minor's will as a case study.

Core Idea
A minor's will is void in Hanafi school, but the author argues suspension on heirs' permission is more consistent with principles.

In this passage, the author presents the inter-madhhab dispute on a discerning minor's will. After stating the Hanafi position of voidness, he adds a critical footnote questioning the consistency of this ruling with the principle that void acts cannot be ratified. This lesson examines the dispute, the principles at play, and the mufti's role in tarjih when the madhhab's explicit texts conflict with logical extension of its own maxims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
Compare the Shafi'i and Hanafi positions: Shafi'i permits a minor's will for charity; Hanafi rules it void. Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).
Note that the Shafi'i rely on 'Umar's practice and the lack of harm to the minor's wealth.
2
Analyze the Hanafi dalil
Why does Abu Hanifa rule it void? Two rationales: (1) harm to the heirs' rights, (2) the minor is not mukallaf and does not need the reward. Trace the reasoning.
The Hanafi argument is based on maqasid (preserving heirs' interests) and taklif (legal accountability).
3
Examine the author's critique
The author argues that the Hanafi reasoning (harm to heirs) should lead to suspension (mawquf) on the heirs' permission, not voidness. He cites al-Ustrushni's report that a minor's will can be ratified after majority.
This critique uses the principle 'al-batil la yuqbal al-ijaza' (void cannot be ratified).
4
Apply the maxim 'al-batil la yuqbal al-ijaza'
If the will is void, it cannot be validated later. If suspended, it can be ratified after majority. Which aligns better with the principle? Note the author prefers suspension.
The author acknowledges that the madhhab's texts uniformly say void, but hints that suspension is more logical.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and the mufti's adab
The author says all madhhab texts indicate voidness. How should a mufti handle this tension? Follow the explicit text (void) or the more consistent qiyas (suspension)? Discuss the adab al-mufti: preference for zahir al-riwayah over personal reasoning.
The author leaves it as a reflection: 'fa-ta'ammal' (so contemplate).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٧/٦٢ - واختلفت الاجتهادات في حكم وصية الصغير المميز: فأجاز الإمام الشافعي وفقهاء المدينة وصيَّته في وجوه البر. واستدلوا من جهة النظر بأنها خير لا يلحق الصغير منها ضرر في ماله، لأنها إنما بعد وفاته واستدلوا من جهة الأثر بما روي عن عمر بن الخطاب الله عنه أنه أجاز وصية صغير يافع.
