(۱) أقول : إن التعليل بحقوق الورثة كان يقتضي أن تعتبر الوصية من الصغير المميز موقوفة على إجازة ورثته لا، باطلة وذلك شأن جميع التصرفات القولية المضرة بحقوق الغير. وقد نقل العلامة محمد بن محمود الأستروشني في كتابه «جامع أحكام الصغار» عن شرح الطحاوي: أن الصبي لو أوصى في حال صغره بوصية لا تجوز إلا إذا أجاز ذلك بعد بلوغه، فيعتبر حينئذ كالابتداء» أي كابتداء الوصية بعد البلوغ (ر: جامع . أحكام الصغار أول فصل الوصايا).
وهذا يؤيد رأينا أن هذه الوصية ينبغي أن تعتبر موقوفة لا باطلة، لأن من القواعد المقررة أن الباطل لا يقبل الإجازة، كما تقدم في بحث البطلان (ف١٠/٥٣) ونص عليه الأستروشني أيضاً أول فصل مسائل البيوع من كتابه (جامع أحكام الصغار». لكن جميع نصوص المذهب التي اطلعت عليها متضافرة على البطلان، فتأمل. ولو قيل بالتوقف على إجازة الورثة كما بينا لكان أوجه وأجرى مع القواعد.
٨٠٦
7/62 - The juristic opinions differed on the ruling of the will of a discerning minor: Imam al-Shafi'i and the jurists of Medina permitted his will in charitable causes. They argued from the perspective of reasoning that it is good that does not cause harm to the minor's wealth because it is only after his death. And they argued from the perspective of transmitted evidence by what was narrated from 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, may Allah be pleased with him, that he permitted the will of a young minor. Abu Hanifa and his companions ruled its voidness due to its harm to the rights of the minor's heirs. The will is only legislated for the major responsible person to make up through its reward what he missed of his religious obligations. As for the minor, he does not need it because he is not legally accountable. And the benefit of his heirs is a benefit for him, so in his will there is a deviation from what is better for him, namely saving for his heirs. And leaving the better is pure harm in his right inevitably, so it is impermissible for the minor, so his will is void. (Ref: Usul al-Bazdawi and its commentary Kashf al-Asrar, 4/259-260). Footnote: I say: The reasoning based on the rights of the heirs would require that the will of a discerning minor be considered suspended upon the permission of his heirs, not void, and that is the status of all verbal transactions that harm the rights of others. The scholar Muhammad ibn Mahmud al-Ustrushni reported in his book 'Jami' Ahkam al-Sighar' from Sharh al-Tahawi: that if a minor made a will during his minority, it is not valid unless he ratifies it after reaching majority, then it is considered like a new beginning, i.e., like beginning the will after majority. This supports our view that this will should be considered suspended, not void, because it is established that 'void' does not accept ratification, as was discussed in the section on voidness (F 10/53), and al-Ustrushni also stated it at the beginning of the chapter on sales in his book. But all the texts of the madhhab that I have seen are consistent on voidness, so contemplate. And if one were to say suspension upon the permission of the heirs as we explained, it would be more fitting and in line with the principles.
Source pages p. 757

Commentary
This passage presents the disagreement (ikhtilaf) between schools regarding the will (wasiyya) of a discerning minor. The Shafi'i school and Medinan jurists permit a minor's will for charitable purposes. Their two proofs: (1) rational: it benefits the minor without harming his wealth since it only takes effect after death; (2) transmitted: the report that Caliph 'Umar permitted the will of a young minor. The Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and his students Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule it void. Their reasoning: the will is intended for a major responsible person (mukallaf) to compensate for missed good deeds; a minor has no such need. Moreover, the will harms the heirs, and the minor's benefit lies in preserving the estate for them. Thus, the will constitutes a pure harm for the minor and is invalid. The author then adds a critical footnote: he argues that the Hanafi reasoning should logically lead to considering the will as suspended (mawquf) on the heirs' permission, not void, similar to other harmful transactions. He cites al-Ustrushni who reports from Sharh al-Tahawi that a minor's will becomes valid if ratified after majority. However, he acknowledges that the dominant Hanafi position is voidness. This is an example of critical refinement: the author questions the consistency of the madhhab's ruling.

Key Terms
وصية (wasiyya): A disposition of property to take effect after death for charitable purposes (فى وجوه البر).
صغير مميز (ṣaghīr mumayyiz): A minor who has reached the age of discernment and can understand the nature of a transaction.
باطل (bāṭil): Void – a transaction that has no legal effect and cannot be ratified.
موقوف (mawqūf): Suspended – a transaction that is not immediately effective but can become effective upon fulfillment of a condition (e.g., permission of heirs).
يجيز (yujīz): To permit or ratify; in legal context, to give consent that makes a suspended transaction effective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on a discerning minor's will according to the relied-upon position in the madhhab?
A) Valid
B) Void
C) Suspended on heirs' permission
D) Valid only for charity

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules a minor's will void, but the tension between textual ruling and legal principle invites the mufti to reflect on the consistency of the madhhab.

Pure Benefit Transactions for the Discerning Minor
Defines pure benefit transactions for a minor and their validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a pure benefit transaction for a discerning minor and apply the rule that it is valid and effective without guardian permission.

Core Idea
A discerning minor's pure benefit transactions are valid without guardian permission.

This lesson introduces the Hanafi rule that certain transactions, which bring only benefit to a discerning minor, are valid without guardian approval. Understanding this rule helps differentiate between transactions that require guardian oversight and those that do not, a crucial distinction for muftis advising on minors' dealings.

Guided Path
1
Define pure benefit (naf' mahd)
Identify transactions that bring only benefit to the minor with no potential loss, such as accepting gifts or charity.
Contrast with transactions involving potential loss (e.g., selling property).
2
Recognize the minor's capacity
The discerning minor possesses 'deficient capacity of performance' (ahliyyat al-ada' al-qasira) sufficient for pure benefit acts.
Discerning (mumayyiz) means capable of distinguishing beneficial from harmful acts.
3
State the rule
Such transactions are valid and effective without guardian (wali or wasi) permission, as if done by an adult.
4
Apply to examples
Accepting a gift, charity, or taking possession of ownerless things (mubahat) are pure benefit transactions.
5
Extend to agency
A discerning minor can act as an agent (wakil) in permissible matters; the principal is bound by the minor's actions.
This is an expansion of the rule because the minor benefits (e.g., fee) and the principal's interest is served.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٨/٦٢ - النوع الثاني: التصرفات التي هي نفع محض في الصغير. وذلك كقبول الهبة أو الصدقة وكإحراز المباحات . وهذا النوع تكفي فيه أهلية الأداء القاصرة لدى الصغير فتصح منه ،ممارسته ويكون نافذاً منه دون توقف على إجازة وليه أو وصيه، بل يكون شأنه كما لو صدر التصرف من كبير ،راشد لأن ممارسته هي منفعة للصغير على كل حال (ر: المجلة / ٩٦٧). وقد اعتبروا من هذا النوع قبول الصغير المميز للوكالة عن غيره في كل ما يجوز فيه التوكيل من بيع وشراء وزواج وطلاق وخصومة وقبض وغيرها. وتصرفاته بالوكالة في حدود ما وكل به تلزم الموكل كما لو كان الوكيل كبيراً. وذلك لأن ممارسة الصبي الصغير هذه التصرفات بالوكالة -
8/62 - The second type: transactions that are pure benefit for the minor, such as accepting a gift or charity and acquiring permissible things. For this type, the deficient capacity of performance (ahliyyat al-ada' al-qasira) suffices for the minor, so its practice is valid from him and is effective from him without depending on the permission of his guardian or executor. Rather, its status is as if the transaction came from a major, rational person, because its practice is a benefit for the minor in any case. (Ref: Majalla, Article 967). They have considered from this type: the acceptance by a discerning minor of agency on behalf of another in everything where agency is permitted, such as sale, purchase, marriage, divorce, litigation, receipt, and others. And his transactions as an agent within the limits of what he is authorized to do bind the principal just as if the agent were an adult, because the minor's practice of these transactions as an agent is...
Source pages p. 758

Commentary
This passage classifies transactions that are pure benefit (naf' mahd) for the discerning minor. These include accepting gifts, charity, and taking possession of ownerless things (mubahat). For such acts, the minor's limited capacity of performance is sufficient; they are valid without guardian permission and take effect as if done by an adult. The reason is that these acts are beneficial in all circumstances. The author also includes acceptance of agency (wakala) by a minor. The minor's acts as an agent bind the principal, because the minor is merely executing the principal's will and benefiting himself by possibly earning a fee. This is an expansion of the rule to include agency transactions, which encompass many areas of fiqh.

Key Terms
نفع محض (naf' maḥḍ): Pure benefit: a transaction that brings only benefit to the minor with no potential loss.
أهلية الأداء القاصرة (ahliyyat al-adā' al-qāṣira): Deficient capacity of performance: the legal capacity of a discerning minor to perform actions that are beneficial.
تمييز (tamyīz): Discernment: the ability to distinguish beneficial from harmful; a condition for the minor's capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Selling his own property
B) Accepting a gift
C) Entering into a marriage contract
D) Making a will

Key Takeaway
A discerning minor's pure benefit transactions are valid and effective without guardian permission, including agency, because they bring only benefit.

Express and Implied Permission for a Minor
Explanation of how permission can be given explicitly or implicitly through conduct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'silence in a context requiring explanation is a statement' to determine when a minor gains trading capacity through implied permission.

Core Idea
Silence can be permission when the guardian should have spoken.

In Hanafi fiqh, a minor's ability to trade depends on the guardian's permission. This permission can be given explicitly or, crucially, through conduct. The maxim 'silence in a context requiring explanation is a statement' transforms the guardian's silent observation of the minor trading into legal consent. This lesson unpacks that rule and its application, drawing from the passage in al-Zarqā's introduction and the Majalla.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two forms of permission
Locate in the passage the contrast between explicit (ṣarāḥa) and implied (dalāla) permission. Explicit is verbal; implied is by conduct, especially silence.
The passage gives examples: 'I have permitted you to trade' vs. guardian seeing and remaining silent.
2
Analyze the implied permission scenario
Focus on the case: guardian sees the minor engaging in trade and stays silent. Understand that this silence is not passive but legally significant because the guardian is aware and could have objected.
The context requires explanation: if the guardian truly did not permit, he would speak up.
3
Extract the governing legal maxim
The passage explicitly cites the qāʿida: 'Silence in a context requiring explanation is a statement' (السكوت في معرض الحاجة إلى البيان بيان). Write it down and note its rank (wusṭā) and type (kulliyya).
This maxim overrides the general rule that 'silence is not attributed to a speaker' when the situation demands a response.
4
Understand the rationale (taʿlīl)
The passage gives the reason: otherwise people would be deceived by the guardian's conduct into thinking the minor is permitted when he is not. This is a form of sadd al-dharāʾiʿ (blocking harmful means) and istiḥsān.
The rationale is to protect third parties who rely on the guardian's apparent consent.
5
Apply the maxim to a new furūʿ case
Take a concrete scenario: guardian sees minor selling goods in the market for weeks and never objects. Conclude that the minor becomes a permitted trader (ma'dhūn) and his transactions are valid.
This is the furūʿ listed in the qawida panel: Guardian sees minor trading and remains silent → minor becomes ma'dhūn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٤/٦٢ - الصراحة والدلالة في الإذن :
والإذن للصغير يكون إما صراحة بأن يقول له ممثله : أذنت لك بالتجارة، أو اذهب إلى السوق فَبغ واشتر، ونحو ذلك؛ وإما دلالة، كما لو رآه وليه يتعاطى مع الناس البيع والشراء فظل ساكتاً عنه، فإنه يصبح مأذوناً، وذلك لحاجة التعامل ودلالة الحال، إذ من المقرر في القواعد أن السكوت في معرض الحاجة إلى البيان بيان (ر: ف ۲۳۲ والمجلة / ٦٧) وإلا تغرّر الناس بدلالة حال الولي، وتورطوا في التعامل مع الصغير وهو غير مأذون.
14/62 - Express and Implied Permission: Permission for the minor is either express, such as when his representative says 'I have permitted you to trade' or 'Go to the market and sell and buy', etc.; or implied, as when his guardian sees him engaging in selling and buying with people and remains silent about it. In that case, he becomes permitted (ma'dhūn), due to the need for transactions and the implication of the situation, for it is established in the rules that silence in a context requiring explanation is a statement. Otherwise, people would be deceived by the guardian's implicit indication and would engage in transactions with the minor while he is not permitted.
Source pages p. 762

Commentary
This passage explains the two ways a guardian can grant permission to a discerning minor: explicitly by words, or implicitly by conduct. The focus is on the latter: if the guardian sees the minor trading in the market and remains silent, this silence is considered a declaration of permission. The rule (qāʿida) cited is that 'silence in a context requiring explanation is a statement' (السكوت في معرض الحاجة إلى البيان بيان). This maxim is applied here: since the guardian's silence occurs when people are dealing with the minor, and the guardian is aware, the silence indicates consent. The reason given is that otherwise, people would be misled by the guardian's conduct, thinking the minor is authorized when he actually is not. This rule is recorded in the Majalla (Article 67) and other Hanafi sources. The application (furūʿ) of this qāʿida is that such silence transforms the minor into a permitted (ma'dhūn) minor with full transactional capacity. It is important to note that this only applies when the guardian is present and sees the transaction; otherwise, the minor may be acting without authority.

Key Terms
صراحة (ṣarāḥa): Explicit verbal expression of permission.
دلالة (dalāla): Implication from conduct or circumstances that conveys permission.
سكوت (sukūt): Silence that, when occurring in a context where speech is expected, carries legal weight.
مأذون (ma'dhūn): A minor who has been granted permission to trade by the guardian.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rule, what is the effect of a guardian seeing his minor child trading in the market and remaining silent?
A) The minor is still interdicted.
B) The minor becomes a permitted (ma'dhūn) minor.
C) The guardian must explicitly forbid for the minor to be interdicted.
D) The minor's transactions are void.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a guardian's silent observation of a minor's trading activity constitutes implied permission, protected by the maxim that silence in a context demanding a response is a statement.

Rulings of Terminal Illness on Donations and Bequests
Explains legal effects of terminal illness and two governing rules for donations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two Hanafi rules governing donations of the terminally ill and evaluate the prohibition of bequests to heirs using the principle of sadd al-dhara'i.

Core Idea
A terminally ill patient can donate only one-third of wealth; no donation to an heir without others' consent.

Terminal illness triggers special legal restrictions to protect creditors and heirs from unfair deprivations. This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi explains two fundamental rules that govern any donation—gift, endowment, or bequest—by a terminally ill patient. Understanding these rules is essential for scholars drafting wills, advising patients, or adjudicating estate matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal results of terminal illness
Terminal illness begins the removal of legal capacity and the attachment of creditors' and heirs' rights to the estate. Recognize that debts now attach to both liability and assets.
This sets the stage for interdicting the patient's disposal of wealth.
2
Distinguish indebted vs. non-indebted patient
If the debt covers all wealth (dayn mustaghriq), the patient is absolutely interdicted from donations. If debt is partial or none, the rules for non-indebted apply: one-third limit and no donation to heirs.
Focus on the non-indebted scenario for the two qawaid.
3
Extract the two qawaid for non-indebted patients
Rule 1: Donations are effective only up to one-third of the estate; excess depends on heirs' permission. Rule 2: Donation to an heir is invalid without consent of all other heirs, regardless of amount.
These rules apply simultaneously.
4
Apply to furuat (case studies)
Apply Rule 1 to a gift to a stranger exceeding one-third: effective only up to one-third. Apply Rule 2 to a bequest to an heir: invalid unless other heirs permit.
Notice how Rule 2 overrides Rule 1 when the donee is an heir.
5
Recognize the rationale and istithna'
The prohibition of bequest to an heir is justified by sadd al-dhara'i (blocking means to evil) to prevent family discord. The exception: all right-holders (creditors or heirs) can consent, waiving the restriction.
The underlying maqasid is protection of family harmony and creditors' rights.

Source Text

المرض إنذار بحادثة الموت التي تنتهي بها الشخصية والأهلية، ويرتب الشرعُ عليها أحكاماً جديدة منها الإرث وحلول الديون التي على الميت إذ ينتقل تعلقها من ذمَّة المدين إلى أمواله لخراب ذمته بالموت، فتصبح تركته كالمرهونة شرعاً بالديون.
فمرض الموت مقدّمة إنذارية لنتيجتين حقوقيتين سلبية وإيجابية سيكون حادث الموت المنذر به سبباً لهما : ٢ ـ وهو ١ - فهو مقدمة لزوال شخصية المريض وانسلاخ أهليته وملكيته. أيضاً مقدمة لثبوت الحقوق العينية في أموال المريض لمن ستنتقل إليهم هذه الأموال بعد موته من دائنين أو ورثة. ففي مرض الموت بدء ذاك الزوال وهذا الثبوت معاً صيانة للحقوق التي أثبتها الشرع في التركة لهاتين الزمرتين (الدائنين والورثة).

وقد ألحق الشرع حالة المرض الأخير من هذه الناحية بالموت. فالتصرف خلالها بالتبرع وما في حكمه، ولو مُنجزاً، يُعتبر كالتصرف المضاف إلى ما بعد الموت.
فمن هنا يعتبر المريض بمرض الموت محجوراً بالنسبة إلى الدائنين حَجْراً عاماً مطلقاً غير مقيد بحد محدود من ماله ومحجوراً بالنسبة إلى الورثة حجراً محدوداً بثلثي ماله.

وإن كان دَيْن المريض غير مستغرق فإنَّ للورثة وللوصية عندئذ ما يزيد عن وفاء الدين فينفذ تبرع المريض كما تنفذ وصيته في ثلث الباقي بعد وفاء الدين فإن زاد تبرعه على ثلث الباقي توقف الزائد على إجازة الورثة.

وهذا الحكم من قبيل سد الذرائع الذي تقدم إيضاحه في بحث الاستصلاح (ف) (٧/٥ كي لا يُتَّخذ من طريق الوصية لأحد الورثة سبيل لتفضيله على غيره في الحصة الإرثية، فتزرع بذلك أحقاد دائمة بين أفراد الأسرة.
Chapter (65): Impediments of Legal Capacity. Illness is a warning of the event of death which ends personality and legal capacity, and the Law establishes new rulings upon it including inheritance and the maturity of debts owed by the deceased, as their attachment transfers from the liability of the debtor to his assets due to the destruction of his liability by death, so his estate becomes as if legally pledged for debts. So terminal illness is a warning precursor for two legal results, negative and positive, which the warned event of death will cause: (1) It is a precursor for the removal of the patient's personality and shedding of his capacity and ownership. (2) Also a precursor for the establishment of in rem rights in the patient's assets for those to whom these assets will transfer after his death, creditors or heirs. So in terminal illness begins that removal and this establishment together, as a safeguard for the rights that the Law has established in the estate for these two groups (creditors and heirs). And from that arises that debts become attached to both the patient's asset and his liability, after they were attached before illness only to his liability, due to his inability to strive and earn, so his liability weakens, so his assets are joined to it as security. And the Law has permitted a person to dispose of one third of what he will leave of wealth in a disposition attached to after his death by bequest and the like in whatever charitable ways he desires seeking reward of the Hereafter. And the Law has attached the condition of final illness in this respect to death. So disposition during it by donation and what is in its ruling, even if immediate, is considered like a disposition attached to after death. Hence, the terminally ill patient is considered interdicted with respect to creditors with a general absolute interdiction not limited by a specific limit of his wealth, and interdicted with respect to heirs with an interdiction limited to two thirds of his wealth. And accordingly, the terminally ill patient is either indebted or not indebted. (a) If the patient is indebted with a debt encompassing all his wealth, then he is considered interdicted from every donation or endowment by consensus of jurists. If he donates in any form, even by selling at a cheap price (i.e., with favor in price), or endows something from his assets, his disposition is not effective; rather it depends on the permission of the creditors; if they reject it, it is void; if they permit it, it is effective. And if the patient's debt is not encompassing, then there is for the heirs and for bequest what exceeds paying the debt; so the patient's donation is effective as his bequest is effective in one third of the remainder after paying the debt; if his donation exceeds one third of the remainder, the excess depends on the permission of the heirs. (b) And if the patient is not indebted, then in all his donations, whether gift, endowment, bequest, or otherwise in any manner, two rules must be observed and applied together: First: That the donation of the terminally ill patient is restricted in its effectiveness to one third of his wealth. So what exceeds one third depends on the permission of the heirs. And second: That the donation of the patient to one of his heirs is not effective; rather it is suspended upon the permission of the remaining heirs, no matter how small its amount, whether it comes from one third of the estate or not. That is due to the saying of the Messenger (peace be upon him) after the revelation of the verse of inheritance: 'Indeed Allah has assigned to every rightful claimant his right; indeed no bequest to an heir.' (Reported by Ahmad, Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah from the hadith of Abu Umamah). And this ruling is of the type of blocking the means, which was explained earlier in the discussion of istislah, so that the path of bequest to an heir should not be used as a way to favor him over others in the inheritance share, thus sowing permanent enmity among family members.
Source pages p. 786

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Aam discusses the impediments of legal capacity arising from terminal illness (marad al-mawt). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that terminal illness is a precursor to death and triggers several legal effects. First, debts become attached to both the patient's liability and his assets, unlike before illness when they were only on his liability. Second, the Law analogizes dispositions made during terminal illness to post-mortem dispositions, so the rule of the one-third limit applies. Consequently, the terminally ill patient is interdicted (mahjur) in two ways: absolutely towards creditors (since all wealth may be needed to pay debts) and limited to two-thirds towards heirs (the patient can freely donate one-third of his wealth). The passage then details three scenarios: (1) if the patient is fully indebted (debt covers all wealth), any donation is suspended on creditor approval; (2) if partially indebted, donations are effective up to one-third of the surplus after debt payment; (3) if not indebted, two rules apply: (a) donations are limited to one-third of the estate, (b) no donation to an heir is valid without consent of other heirs. The prohibition of bequest to an heir is derived from the hadith 'No bequest to an heir' and is justified by the legal principle of blocking the means (sadd al-dhara'i) to prevent family discord.

Key Terms
مرض الموت (marad al-mawt): Terminal illness that is a precursor to death, causing legal restrictions on disposal of wealth.
دين مستغرق (dayn mustaghriq): A debt that covers all of the debtor's wealth.
سد الذرائع (sadd al-dhara'i): Blocking the means that lead to unlawful outcomes; used here to justify prohibition of bequest to an heir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two rules that apply to donations of a terminally ill patient who is not indebted?
A) Donations are unrestricted; bequest to an heir is allowed.
B) Donations are limited to one-third of the estate; donation to an heir is invalid without other heirs' permission.
C) Donations are limited to one-half; donation to an heir is valid if within one-third.
D) Donations are entirely prohibited; all property must go to heirs.

Key Takeaway
The terminally ill patient's freedom of donation is restricted to one-third, and any donation to an heir is void unless all other heirs consent.

Strength of Guardianship Over Minors: The Maxim on Personal Authority
Classifies guardianship by strength and states the key maxim with applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that strong guardianship over the person grants authority for specific actions and identify the emergency exception that overrides liability.

Core Idea
Strong personal guardianship gives powers of forced marriage, discipline, circumcision, and surgery; weak guardianship does not.

In Hanafi guardianship law, the scope of a guardian's authority depends on the strength of their guardianship over the minor's person and property. This lesson unpacks a key maxim that defines what actions a strong personal guardian may take—and the liability that arises when a weak guardian oversteps. We will also examine an important exception for emergency medical care.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its source
Identify the exact wording of the maxim from the passage: 'Strong guardianship over the person gives its holder the authority of forced marriage, discipline, circumcision, and medical treatment by cauterization and surgery.' Note the classical and modern terms used.
The source is Ibn Nujaym's al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair.
2
Determine the scope: who qualifies as a strong personal guardian?
From the passage, identify which guardians have strong authority over both person and property (father, son, 'asabi grandfather). Understand that others (e.g., property-only guardians) have only weak personal authority.
Strong personal guardians are those with original wilayah over the person.
3
Apply the maxim to the furuat (sub-cases)
Work through the two applications given: (1) a weak personal guardian authorizes surgery causing harm → liable for damages. (2) a stranger performs emergency surgery on a minor → no liability because necessity overrides.
The emergency exception is a key istithna'.
4
Identify the governing qawida for the exception
The exception is governed by the maxim 'الضرورة تبيح المحظورات' (Necessity permits prohibitions). Recognize that this maxim limits the scope of the main rule.
This shows the hierarchical relationship between qawaid.
5
Derive the practical consequence (thamarah)
Conclude that any guardian lacking strong personal authority who performs acts like circumcision or surgery without valid necessity is liable for harm. The mufta bihi is that only strong personal guardians may consent to such procedures.
This ruling is relied upon in Hanafi fatwa.

Chapter (66): The Strength and Weakness of Guardianship. Examination of the fiqh rulings related to types of guardianship and their powers indicates that: Guardians whose original authority is limited only to the person also have weak authority over the minor's property by way of dependency due to need. Likewise, guardians of property only also have weak authority over the minor's person. As for guardians of both person and property together, their authority is strong in both the personal and financial aspects, and they are the son, the father, and the 'asabi grandfather as previously mentioned. Accordingly, guardianship over minors according to its strength and weakness in Islamic fiqh is divided into four types: strong in person and property, weak in both, strong in one and weak in the other. 1. Strong guardianship over the person gives its holder the authority of forced marriage, discipline, circumcision, and medical treatment by cauterization and surgery. Therefore, whoever does not have strong guardianship over the minor's person has no right to charge a doctor to perform surgery on him or to circumcise him; and if he does so and harm or death results from it, he is liable for compensation or blood money. It appears that this applies in normal cases, not exceptional cases that require rapid first aid, such as if a child is hit or run over, and delaying his treatment and waiting for his guardian would be feared for his life, especially in today's car accidents.
Source pages p. 797

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on guardianship (wilayah) classifies the types of guardians based on the scope and strength of their authority. The text explains that guardians who originally have authority only over the person (like the son? actually the guardians listed as strong in both are son, father, and 'asabi grandfather) also have weak authority over the minor's property by dependency. Similarly, guardians of property only (like wasiyy) have weak authority over the person. The strong guardians in both areas are the son, father, and grandfather (in the paternal line). This classification yields four types: strong in both, weak in both, strong in one and weak in the other. The key example of strong authority over the person is the power to force marriage (ijbari marriage for minors), discipline (like hitting for educational purposes), circumcision, and medical treatment including surgery. Anyone without such strong authority who performs these actions causing harm is liable for damages or blood money. However, the author adds an exception for emergencies where immediate medical care is needed (e.g., car accidents) because delaying treatment could be fatal. The footnote explains that the classical term 'ط القَرْحة' (lancing a boil) is now understood broadly as performing any surgery.

Key Terms
ولاية قوية على النفس (wilayah qawiyyah 'ala al-nafs): Strong guardianship over the person, granting powers like forced marriage, discipline, circumcision, and medical treatment.
الجد العصبي (al-jadd al-'asabi): The paternal grandfather in the male line; considered a strong guardian in Hanafi law for both person and property.
بطّ القَرْحة (batt al-qarhah): Classical term for lancing a boil; used metaphorically for any surgical operation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification in this text, which of the following is a consequence of lacking strong guardianship over a minor's person?
A) The guardian cannot enroll the minor in school.
B) The guardian is liable for damages if they authorize surgery that causes harm.
C) The guardian must seek court permission for all decisions.
D) The guardian cannot manage the minor's property.

Key Takeaway
Strong personal guardianship is necessary to authorize acts like surgery without liability; otherwise, liability falls on the unauthorized actor unless necessity intervenes.

Weak Guardianship (Al-Wilayah Al-Da'ifah) and Its Limitations
Defines weak guardianship and contrasts it with strong guardianship regarding trade permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between weak and strong guardianship in Hanafi law and state the ruling that only a strong guardian may authorize a discerning minor to trade.

Core Idea
Weak guardianship covers the minor's person but not full property authority; only the strong guardian can permit trade.

In this section, Shaykh Mustafa al-Zarqa defines 'weak guardianship' (al-wilayah al-da'ifah) — a limited form of guardianship over a minor's person, not his property. He illustrates with the examples of a foundling caregiver and a stepfather. The key legal consequence is that a weak guardian cannot authorize a discerning minor to engage in trade; that power belongs exclusively to the strong guardian: the father, grandfather, or appointed executor (wasi). This distinction is foundational to understanding the hierarchy of guardianship in the Hanafi school and its application in modern family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of guardianship
Locate the description of weak guardianship (al-wilayah al-da'ifah) and strong guardianship (al-wilayah al-qawiyah). Note that weak guardianship is established for someone who takes in a foundling or a stepfather over a stepchild. Strong guardianship is held by the father, then the grandfather, then the executors.
Weak guardianship is over the person only, not full property authority.
2
Extract the ruling on trade permission
Find the statement that only the strong guardian (wali qawi) can give permission to a discerning minor to trade. The weak guardian does not have this authority.
The text explicitly says: '…لا يملك الإذن للقاصر المميز بالتجارة إلا الولي القوي…'
3
Understand the rationale
Consider why the weak guardian cannot give permission: because his guardianship does not extend to property matters (al-wilayah fi al-mal). The text states 'أما ولي النفس الضعيف الولاية في المال فلا يملك هذا الإذن'.
This is a limitation inherent in the definition of weak guardianship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٦٦) الولاية أو النيابة الشرعية القاصر في حجره وعياله من الأجانب عنه، أو من الأقارب الذين لم يصل إليهم دور الولاية على نفسه. حتى إنَّ الذي يلتقط طفلاً لقيطاً غير معروف الأهل، ويُعنى بتربيته في حجره وعياله تثبت له هذه الولاية الضعيفة على نفسه وماله بالحدود التي بيناها في الفقرة السابقة.، وكذلك ولاية زوج الأم على ربيبه القاصر الذي يضمه إلى عياله. يتضح من ذلك أنه لا يملك الإذن للقاصر المميز بالتجارة إلا الولي القوي الولاية في المال وهو الأب، ثم الجد والأوصياء. أما ولي النفس الضعيف الولاية في المال فلا يملك هذا الإذن، لأن التجارة لا يملك الإذن بها. (ر )) رد المحتار (٦١١/٥)(١) . ٦٦ / ٩ - السلطان أو القاضي ولي من لا ولي له : وجدير بالتنبيه أن من لا يملك حق جميع صلاحيات النيابة الشرعية للأولياء والأوصياء تنتقل إلى السلطان عند عدمهم بمقتضى ولايته العامة يمارسها بنفسه أو بواسطة من يأذن له لمصلحة القاصر حتى ينصب لها وصي يمارسها. وينوب القاضي عن السلطان ذلك . في والمبدأ الفقهي العام في هذا المقام هو أنَّ السلطان ولي من لا ولي له . (۲) وهو نص مروي في الحديث النبوي (٢) (ر: آخر كتاب اللقيط في رد المحتار) (1) يلحظ في هذا المقام أن نظام إدارة أموال الأيتام العثماني وبعده قانون الأحوال الشخصية الجديد لدينا في المادة / ۱۸۲ / منه قد حدًا كثيراً من سلطة الأوصياء، ومنعاهم من التجارة للقاصر بأمواله لكثرة الخيانات في الوصاية . ويلحظ أيضاً أن قانون الأحوال الشخصية الجديد لدينا (في المادتين ١٦٤ - ١٦٥ منه) قد جعل حق الإذن للقاصر بالتجارة محصوراً بالقاضي الشرعي، كما جعل للقاضي حق تسليم الصغار المميزين بعد إتمامهم الخامسة عشرة من العمر، والسفهاء المحجورين جانباً من أموالهم لإدارتها على سبيل التمرين والتجربة . (۲) أخرجه أبو داود (۲۰۸۳) والترمذي (۱۱۰۲) وابن ماجه ،(۱۸۷۹)، من حديث عائشة وحسنه الترمذي وصححه ابن حبان (١٢٤٨) والحاكم ١٦٨/٢. وبسط الكلام عنه البيهقي في السنن ۱٠٥/٧ - ۱۰۷ ، والحافظ في التلخيص ١٥٦/٣. : الباب الثامن : نظرية الأهلية والولاية لكن يجب أن يلحظ هنا أنه ليس للقاضي ممارسة هذه الصلاحيات من بيع وشراء وإيجار واستئجار وجباية وغير ذلك لمصلحة القاصر مع وجود النائب الشرعي المختص بها من وليّ أو وصي؛ لأن ولاية هذا النائب مخصوصة بهذه الشؤون. فهي فيها مقدّمة على ولاية القاضي العامة عليها، لأن من القواعد الفقهية المقررة: أنَّ الولاية الخاصة أقوى في موضوعها من الولاية العامة (المجلة/ ٥٩). وهذا ما تقضي به الأصول المقررة في علم الإدارة والقوانين الإدارية الحديثة اليوم، وفقاً لقاعدة توزيع الصلاحيات والمسؤوليات ومبدأ التدرج. فليس للموظف الرئيس أن يقوم هو بالعمل أو التوقيع العائد لمرؤوسه، ولكن إذا تمرَّد هذا الموظف المرؤوس عن عمله دون مبرر يُعزل وينصب غيره ليقوم بالعمل العائد إليه . ٦٦ / ١٠ - هذا، وإن هناك ولاية مالية محضة ليست ناشئة عن نقص أهلية، ولا علاقة لها بالنفس أصلاً كولاية المتولي على الوقف، والولاية الضعيفة لوصي الميت على حصة الراشد الغائب من ورثته. وهذا النوع لا صلة له ببحثنا هنا لعدم علاقته بالأهلية وقصورها (۱) (۱) هذا التقسيم المرتب في الولاية من حيث الضعف والقوة هو الذي حققه بالاستناد إلى فروع الأحكام الفقهية والدي الشيخ أحمد الزرقاء رحمه الله تعالى في شرحه الواسع : الضافي للقواعد الكلية في المجلة تحت القاعدة القائلة: الولاية الخاصة أقوى من : الولاية العامة وهي المادة / ٥٩/ من المجلة . ر شرح القواعد الفقهية للشيخ أحمد الزرقا ط ۲ دار القلم، دمشق (١٤٠٩) هـ .
Section (66): Guardianship or Legal Representation. The minor under the care and household of strangers, or of relatives who have not attained guardianship over themselves. Even whoever picks up a foundling child of unknown family, and undertakes his upbringing under his care and household, this weak guardianship over his person and property is established for him within the limits we mentioned in the previous paragraph. Likewise, the guardianship of the stepfather over his minor stepchild whom he includes in his household. It becomes clear from this that no one has the authority to give permission to the discerning minor for trade except the strong guardian in property, who is the father, then the grandfather, and the executors (awsiya'). As for the weak guardian of the person (who has weak authority over property), he does not have this permission, because he does not have the authority to permit trade. (Ref: Radd al-Muhtar 5/611) (1). 66/9 - The Sultan or the judge is the guardian of one who has no guardian: It is worth noting that whoever does not have the right to all the powers of legal representation of guardians and executors — those powers transfer to the Sultan in their absence by virtue of his general guardianship. He exercises them himself or through someone he authorizes for the benefit of the minor until he appoints an executor (wasi) to exercise them. The judge deputizes for the Sultan in that. The general legal principle in this context is: The Sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian. (2) It is a text reported in the prophetic hadith (2) (see: end of Kitab al-Laqit in Radd al-Muhtar). (1) It is noted in this context that the Ottoman orphan fund administration, and after it our new Personal Status Law in Article 182, have greatly restricted the authority of executors and prevented them from trading with the minor's property due to frequent betrayals in guardianship. It is also noted that our new Personal Status Law (in Articles 164-165) has made the right to give permission to the minor for trade restricted to the religious judge, and also gave the judge the right to hand over to discerning minors after they complete fifteen years of age, and to interdicted spendthrifts, a portion of their wealth to manage on a trial and experimental basis. (2) Narrated by Abu Dawud (2083), al-Tirmidhi (1102), and Ibn Majah (1879) from the hadith of Aisha; al-Tirmidhi deemed it hasan and Ibn Hibban (1248) and al-Hakim (2/168) declared it sahih. Al-Bayhaqi discussed it extensively in al-Sunan (7/105-107), and al-Hafiz in al-Talkhis (3/156). Chapter Eight: Theory of Capacity and Guardianship. But it must be noted here that the judge may not exercise these powers of sale, purchase, lease, hiring, collection, and so forth for the benefit of the minor if the competent legal representative (wali or wasi) exists, because the guardianship of this representative is specific to these matters. Thus it is given precedence over the general guardianship of the judge, because among the established legal maxims is: Specific guardianship is stronger in its subject matter than general guardianship (Majalla Article 59). And this is what is required by the established principles in administrative science and modern administrative laws today, according to the rule of distribution of powers and responsibilities and the principle of hierarchy. The superior officer is not to do the work or sign what belongs to his subordinate, but if this subordinate officer rebels against his work without justification, he is dismissed and another is appointed to do the work belonging to him. 66/10 - Moreover, there is a pure financial guardianship that does not arise from incapacity and has no connection to the person at all, such as the guardianship of the trustee (mutawalli) over the waqf, and the weak guardianship of the executor of the deceased over the share of the absent adult heir. This type has no relevance to our discussion here because of its lack of connection to capacity and its deficiency. (1) (1) This classification of guardianship in terms of weakness and strength is what was verified by relying on the branches of fiqh rulings by the late Sheikh Ahmad al-Zarqa (may Allah have mercy on him) in his extensive commentary 'al-Dafi li al-Qawa'id al-Kulliyya fi al-Majalla' under the maxim: 'Specific guardianship is stronger than general guardianship' which is Article 59 of the Majalla. See: Sharh al-Qawa'id al-Fiqhiyya by Sheikh Ahmad al-Zarqa, 2nd ed., Dar al-Qalam, Damascus (1409 AH).
Source pages p. 799

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm discusses the Hanafi classification of guardianship (wilayah) into strong and weak types, and the principle that the Sultan (or judge) serves as guardian for those without a specific guardian. It also presents the qawida: 'Specific guardianship is stronger than general guardianship' (الولاية الخاصة أقوى في موضوعها من الولاية العامة). The author first defines weak guardianship: it is the guardianship over a minor's person (but not full property authority) enjoyed by someone who takes in a foundling (laqit) or a stepfather over a stepchild. This weak guardian does not have the authority to give the discerning minor permission to engage in trade; only a strong guardian (father, grandfather, or appointed wasi) has that authority. Then the author states the rule: 'The Sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian' (السلطان ولي من لا ولي له). This is derived from a hadith. This means that when there is no specific guardian, the general guardianship of the Sultan (or judge) covers all powers of legal representation for the minor. However, if a specific guardian (wali or wasi) exists, the judge may not exercise those powers because specific guardianship is stronger in its domain. The author also notes exceptions and modern legal applications, citing the Ottoman orphan fund administration and the modern personal status law that restricts the permission to trade to the judge only. Finally, the author distinguishes a type of pure financial guardianship that is not related to incapacity, such as the trustee of a waqf or the weak guardianship over an absent heir's share, which are outside the scope of this discussion. The passage reinforces the hierarchy of guardianship in Hanafi law and the importance of the principle that specific authority takes precedence over general authority.

Key Terms
الولاية الخاصة (al-wilayah al-khassah): Specific guardianship held by a designated person (father, grandfather, wasi) over a minor's property and person.
الولاية العامة (al-wilayah al-'ammah): General guardianship held by the Sultan/judge over those without a specific guardian.
الولاية الضعيفة (al-wilayah al-da'ifah): Weak guardianship over the person of a minor (e.g., foundling caregiver, stepfather) with limited property authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who can grant a discerning minor permission to trade?
A) The stepfather (zawj al-umm)
B) The guardian of a foundling (mulqat)
C) The father (al-ab)
D) The judge (al-qadi)

Key Takeaway
Weak guardianship covers the minor's person but not full property authority; only a strong guardian may authorize trade.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate
10 min
The Sultan as Guardian and the Qawida of Specific vs General Guardianship
Presents the hadith of the Sultan's guardianship and the qawida on specific vs general guardianship, with furuat and practical applications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that specific guardianship is stronger than general guardianship, and explain the scope of the Sultan's (judge's) guardianship as 'guardian of the guardianless'.

Core Idea
The judge's general guardianship suspends when a specific guardian exists; specific guardianship is stronger in its domain.

This section presents one of the most important legal maxims in Hanafi guardianship law: 'Specific guardianship is stronger in its subject matter than general guardianship' (الولاية الخاصة أقوى في موضوعها من الولاية العامة). The author derives this from the hadith 'The Sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian' and applies it to the relationship between the judge's general authority and the specific authority of a wali or wasi. The lesson also draws a parallel to modern administrative hierarchy, reinforcing the principle that a superior should not bypass a subordinate's jurisdiction.

Guided Path
1
State the hadith and its legal implication
Find the hadith 'The Sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian' (السلطان ولي من لا ولي له). Understand that this establishes the judge's general guardianship (wilayah 'ammah) over minors and others without a specific guardian.
The hadith is cited from Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, and others.
2
Identify the limitation on the judge's authority
Locate the statement that the judge may not exercise his general powers (sale, purchase, etc.) for a minor when a specific guardian (wali or wasi) exists. This is because specific guardianship (al-wilayah al-khassah) is stronger.
The text explicitly says 'ليس للقاضي ممارسة هذه الصلاحيات... مع وجود النائب الشرعي المختص'.
3
Extract the qawida and understand its rank
Identify the maxim 'الولاية الخاصة أقوى في موضوعها من الولاية العامة'. Note that it is from Article 59 of the Majalla and is classified as an intermediate (wusta) maxim governing guardianship.
The author also mentions that this principle aligns with modern administrative principles of hierarchy.
4
Apply the qawida to the furuat
Consider the two furuat given: (1) the judge cannot act when a specific guardian exists; (2) in modern administration, a superior should not do a subordinate's work. Understand that the same logic of specificity grounding authority applies.
The second furua is an analogy (qiyas) to non-fiqh contexts, showing the maxim's broader applicability.

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm discusses the Hanafi classification of guardianship (wilayah) into strong and weak types, and the principle that the Sultan (or judge) serves as guardian for those without a specific guardian. It also presents the qawida: 'Specific guardianship is stronger than general guardianship' (الولاية الخاصة أقوى في موضوعها من الولاية العامة). The author first defines weak guardianship: it is the guardianship over a minor's person (but not full property authority) enjoyed by someone who takes in a foundling (laqit) or a stepfather over a stepchild. This weak guardian does not have the authority to give the discerning minor permission to engage in trade; only a strong guardian (father, grandfather, or appointed wasi) has that authority. Then the author states the rule: 'The Sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian' (السلطان ولي من لا ولي له). This is derived from a hadith. This means that when there is no specific guardian, the general guardianship of the Sultan (or judge) covers all powers of legal representation for the minor. However, if a specific guardian (wali or wasi) exists, the judge may not exercise those powers because specific guardianship is stronger in its domain. The author also notes exceptions and modern legal applications, citing the Ottoman orphan fund administration and the modern personal status law that restricts the permission to trade to the judge only. Finally, the author distinguishes a type of pure financial guardianship that is not related to incapacity, such as the trustee of a waqf or the weak guardianship over an absent heir's share, which are outside the scope of this discussion. The passage reinforces the hierarchy of guardianship in Hanafi law and the importance of the principle that specific authority takes precedence over general authority.

Key Terms
الولاية الخاصة (al-wilayah al-khassah): Guardianship that is specific to a particular person (father, grandfather, wasi) and takes precedence over general guardianship.
الولاية العامة (al-wilayah al-'ammah): General guardianship of the Sultan/judge over those without a specific guardian.
قاعدة الولاية الخاصة أقوى (qa'idat al-wilayah al-khassah aqwa): The legal maxim that specific guardianship is stronger than general guardianship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when can the judge exercise his general guardianship over a minor?
A) Whenever he deems it in the minor's best interest.
B) Only when there is no specific guardian (wali or wasi).
C) Only when the specific guardian gives permission.
D) Whenever the minor requests it.

Key Takeaway
The judge's general guardianship is subsidiary; specific guardianship takes precedence, grounded in the maxim 'specific guardianship is stronger than general guardianship'.

Special Guardianship Overrides General Guardianship
Explanation of a fiqh maxim with furuat and modern administrative analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-wilāyah al-khāṣṣah aqwā min al-wilāyah al-‘āmmah' to cases of conflicting guardianships and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Special guardianship (father, executor) blocks the judge's general authority over the minor's affairs.

This passage from Sheikh Mustafa al-Zarqa' presents a key Hanafi maxim that settles conflicts between special and general guardians. You will explore how the maxim works in classical fiqh and its resonance with modern administrative principles. The goal is to equip you to apply this maxim in fatwa contexts where a special guardian exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim
Locate the exact Arabic wording of the maxim in the passage. Note its source (Majalla article 59).
The maxim is: الولاية الخاصة أقوى في موضوعها من الولاية العامة
2
Understand the scope
The passage applies it to a minor's guardianship: a judge cannot transact for a minor when a father or executor (special guardian) is present. Why? Because special guardianship is 'stronger in its subject matter'.
3
Follow the expansion
The author expands the maxim with an analogy from administrative law: a senior manager must not do a subordinate's work; the correct action is dismissal and replacement. This illustrates the principle of delegated authority.
4
Distinguish sub-categories
Note the mention of purely financial guardianships (mutawalli, wasi for absent heir) that are not based on capacity deficiency. These are outside the main discussion but still governed by the same maxim.
5
Apply in fatwa context
When a mufti receives a question about a judge intervening in a minor's property, he must first check if a special guardian exists. If yes, the judge cannot act.

الباب الثامن : نظرية الأهلية والولاية
لكن يجب أن يلحظ هنا أنه ليس للقاضي ممارسة هذه الصلاحيات من بيع وشراء وإيجار واستئجار وجباية وغير ذلك لمصلحة القاصر مع وجود النائب الشرعي المختص بها من وليّ أو وصي؛ لأن ولاية هذا النائب مخصوصة بهذه الشؤون. فهي فيها مقدّمة على ولاية القاضي العامة عليها، لأن من القواعد الفقهية المقررة: أنَّ الولاية الخاصة أقوى في موضوعها من الولاية العامة (المجلة/ ٥٩).
وهذا ما تقضي به الأصول المقررة في علم الإدارة والقوانين الإدارية الحديثة اليوم، وفقاً لقاعدة توزيع الصلاحيات والمسؤوليات ومبدأ التدرج. فليس للموظف الرئيس أن يقوم هو بالعمل أو التوقيع العائد لمرؤوسه، ولكن إذا تمرَّد هذا الموظف المرؤوس عن عمله دون مبرر يُعزل وينصب غيره ليقوم بالعمل العائد إليه .

Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship
But it must be noted here that it is not permissible for the judge to exercise these powers of sale, purchase, lease, hiring, collection and so forth for the benefit of the minor in the presence of the legal proxy competent for them, whether a guardian or executor; because the guardianship of this proxy is specific to these matters. Thus, it takes precedence in them over the general guardianship of the judge, because it is established among the fiqh principles that special guardianship is stronger in its subject matter than general guardianship (Al-Majalla/59). And this is what is required by the established principles in administrative science and modern administrative laws today, in accordance with the rule of distribution of powers and responsibilities and the principle of hierarchy. So it is not for the senior employee to perform the work or sign that belongs to his subordinate, but if the subordinate employee disobeys his work without justification, he is dismissed and another is appointed to perform the work that belongs to him.
66/10 - Moreover, there is a purely financial guardianship that does not arise from a deficiency in legal capacity, and has no connection with personal matters at all, such as the guardianship of the mutawalli over the endowment, and the weak guardianship of the death executor over the share of the absent adult heir. This type has no connection with our discussion here due to its lack of relation to capacity and its deficiency. (1)
(1) This classification of guardianship in terms of weakness and strength is what was established by relying on the branches of fiqh rulings by the esteemed Sheikh Ahmad al-Zarqa', may Allah have mercy on him, in his extensive commentary 'al-Dafi li al-Qawa'id al-Kulliyya' on the Majalla under the maxim: special guardianship is stronger than general guardianship, which is Article 59 of the Majalla. See Sharh al-Qawa'id al-Fiqhiyya by Sheikh Ahmad al-Zarqa', 2nd edition, Dar al-Qalam, Damascus (1409 AH).
Source pages p. 800

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi tradition that clarifies the hierarchy of guardianships. It opens by stating a key fiqh maxim: 'Special guardianship is stronger in its subject matter than general guardianship' (from Majalla article 59). The author applies this to a specific scenario: when a minor has a natural guardian (such as a father or appointed executor) who is competent to handle financial transactions on their behalf, the judge (who has general guardianship over minors) must not step in and take over those tasks. The special guardian's authority takes precedence. This is not merely a fiqh rule; the author notes that modern administrative law also follows the same principle of delegation. A senior official should not do the work of a subordinate. If the subordinate fails to perform, the proper procedure is to dismiss and replace them, not for the superior to take over. The passage then mentions another category: purely financial guardianships (like that of a waqf trustee) that don't relate to capacity; these are outside the scope of the current discussion. The footnote credits Sheikh Ahmad al-Zarqa' for the classification of guardianship strengths.

Key Terms
الولاية الخاصة (al-wilāyah al-khāṣṣah): Guardianship that is specific to a particular person or matter, such as the guardianship of a father over his minor child, or an executor over a specific property.
الولاية العامة (al-wilāyah al-‘āmmah): General guardianship that extends over all individuals lacking legal capacity, primarily exercised by the judge or state authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'special guardianship is stronger than general guardianship' is a foundational Hanafi principle that restricts a judge's general authority when a competent special guardian exists.

The Definition of 'Adah (Custom) and Its Distinction from Rational Necessity
Defining 'adah linguistically and terminologically with its scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'adah in Hanafi usul al-fiqh, distinguish it from rational necessity (talazum 'aqli), and classify customs as good or evil.

Core Idea
Custom is recurring action without rational cause; only beneficial, non-contradictory customs are legally valid.

This passage from al-Zarqa's 'Introduction to Islamic Law' lays the foundation for understanding custom ('adah) as a subsidiary source of law. It clarifies that not every widespread practice is acceptable—only those arising from genuine need and free from rational necessity qualify. The definition distinguishes custom from necessary cause-effect relationships, which are outside its scope. This distinction is crucial for a mufti evaluating whether a community practice can be upheld as a valid 'urf.

Guided Path
1
Identify Good vs Evil Customs
Review the examples of evil customs (e.g., burying daughters alive, enslaving debtors) and note that they must be opposed through legislation and education. Good customs are those based on genuine need and benefit.
This sets the normative framework: custom is not automatically authoritative.
2
Understand the Linguistic Definition
The word 'ādah derives from al-'awd (return) or al-mu'āwadah (repetition). It refers to an action repeated until it becomes easy, like a second nature.
3
Grasp the Terminological Definition
Scholars of usul define 'ādah as 'the recurring matter without a rational connection.' The key is the absence of a cause-effect relation determined by reason.
Pay careful attention to the phrase 'without a rational connection' (min ghayr 'ilāqah 'aqliyyah). This is the distinguishing line.
4
Distinguish from Rational Necessity (al-talāzum al-'aqli)
If repetition is due to a necessary causal link (e.g., a ring moving with a finger), it is not custom but rational necessity. The mind compels the connection; no inclination or habit is involved.
Example: Day following night is not custom but a natural law; a habitual eating pattern is custom.
5
Recognize the Scope: Individual vs Collective Custom
Custom applies to individual habits (e.g., sleeping position) and collective practices (which are synonymous with 'urf). The footnote clarifies that individual custom may be established by two or three repetitions, while collective custom requires countless repetitions to become widespread.
This nuance is important for a mufti: individual customs are easier to prove; collective customs need more evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف

يتضح من ذلك أنَّ العادات والأعراف منها الحسن ومنها القبيح، إذ ليس كل ما يعتاده الناس ويتعارفونه ناشئاً عن حاجة صادقة ومصلحة حكيمة يكون الأمر المعتاد وسيلة ميسرة لها.
فقد يعتاد الناس عادات تقوم على جهالات وضلالات موروثة يشقى بها المجتمع وليس فيها منتفع كاسترقاق المدين المعسر عند الرومان وفي جاهلية العرب وكوأد البنات والغزو الداخلي والسائبة في الجاهلية وكدفن الزوجة حيَّة مع زوجها إذا مات عند الهنود الوثنيين، وكدفن نفائس الأموال مع أصحابها الموتى عند قدماء المصريين وكأخذ أولياء البنات مهورهن عند تزويجهن في عادة البدو من العربان في بلادنا السورية وغيرها إلى اليوم.
فكل هذا وأمثاله عادات وأعراف قبيحة يجب أن تكافح بالتعليم والتشريع وبالأنظمة التي يقرها ويصدرها أولياء الأمر.

المطلب الثاني: تعريف العادة

العادة في اللغة مأخوذة من العود أو المعاودة بمعنى التكرار. وهي اسم لتكرير الفعل والانفعال حتى يصير سهلاً تعاطيه كالطبع. ولذلك قيل: العادة طبيعة ثانية.
وقد عرف علماء أصول الفقه العادة بأنها: الأمر المتكرر من غير علاقة عقلية.
فإذا كان التكرر ناشئاً عن علاقة عقلية، وهي التي يحكم فيها العقل بهذا التكرار لم يكن عندئذ من قبيل العادة، بل من قبيل التلازم العقلي. وذلك كتكرر حدوث الأثر كلما حدث مؤثره بسبب أن المؤثر علة لا يتخلف عنها معلولها كتحرك الخاتم بحركة الإصبع، وتبدل مكان الشيء بحركته. فهذا لا عادة مهما تكرر لأنه ناشيء عن تلازم وارتباط في العلة والمعلول يقضي به العقل وليس ناشئاً عن ميل أو طبع أو عامل طبيعي.

يتبين من هذا التعريف أن العادة في الاصطلاح لها مفهوم شامل واسع الحدود، لأن قولهم في تعريفها: «الأمر المتكرر» يشمل كل حادث يتكرر من حيث ان لفظ «الأمر» كلفظ الشيء» من أوسع ألفاظ اللغة عموماً وشمولاً:
- فالعادة تطلق تارة على ما يعتاده الفرد من الناس في شؤونه الخاصة، كعادته في نومه، وأكله ونوع مأكوله، وحديثه، وكثير أفعاله. وهذه هي العادة الفردية.
- وتطلق تارة أخرى على ما تعتاده الجماعات والجماهير، مما ينشأ في الأصل عن اتجاه عقلي، وتفكير حسناً كان أم قبيحاً. وهذه ترادف العرف، كما سيأتي بيانه.

(1) يقول الفقهاء في بعض المواطن إن العادة تتحقق بتكرر الفعل مرتين أو ثلاثاً، لأنها مأخوذة من العود أو المعاودة (ر) الأشباه والنظائر لابن نجيم، الفن الأول تحت القاعدة السادسة ١٢٨/١). ولا يخفى أن مرادهم بذلك عادة الفرد أما عادة الجماعة التي هي: عرف الجمهور، فلا ينطبق عليها هذا الحد، لأن العادة لا تشيع وتنتشر بين الجماهير إلا بعد أن تتكرر بين الناس مرات لا تحصى.
Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. It becomes clear from this that customs and 'urf include good and bad, for not everything that people habitually do and recognize arises from a genuine need and wise benefit making the customary matter a facilitated means for it. People may adopt customs based on inherited ignorance and misguidance that make society miserable and have no benefit, such as enslaving an insolvent debtor among the Romans and in the Arab Jahiliyyah, burying female infants alive, internal raiding, and the sa'ibah in Jahiliyyah, burying a wife alive with her husband when he dies among the Hindu idolaters, burying precious possessions with their dead owners among the ancient Egyptians, and the fathers of girls taking their dowries when marrying them in the Bedouin custom of the Arabs in our Syrian lands and elsewhere to this day. All this and its like are evil customs and 'urf that must be combated through education, legislation, and regulations that the authorities approve and issue. Second sub-topic: Definition of 'adah. 'Adah linguistically is derived from al-'awd or al-mu'awadah meaning repetition. It is a name for repeating an action and reaction until it becomes easy to practice like nature. Hence it is said: custom is a second nature. The scholars of usul al-fiqh have defined 'adah as: the recurring matter without a rational connection. If the repetition arises from a rational connection, i.e., one that the mind judges as repetition, then it is not considered custom, but rather rational necessity. Such as the repeated occurrence of an effect whenever its cause occurs because the cause is an effective cause whose effect does not fail, like the movement of a ring with the movement of a finger, and the change of place of an object by its movement. This is not custom no matter how often repeated because it arises from a necessary connection and relation between cause and effect determined by reason, not from inclination, nature, or a natural factor. It becomes clear from this definition that 'adah in terminology has a comprehensive concept with wide boundaries, because their statement in its definition: 'the recurring matter' includes every event that repeats, since the word 'matter' like the word 'thing' is among the broadest words of language in generality and inclusiveness: - 'Adah is sometimes applied to what an individual habitually does in his private affairs, like his habit in sleeping, eating, type of food, speaking, and many of his actions. This is individual custom. - And it is sometimes applied to what groups and masses habitually do, originating from a rational direction and thinking, whether good or bad. This is synonymous with 'urf, as will be explained. (1) The jurists say in some places that 'adah is realized by repeating an action two or three times, because it is derived from al-'awd or al-mu'awadah (cf. al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir by Ibn Nujaym, first chapter under the sixth rule, 1/128). It is obvious that they mean by that individual custom; as for group custom which is the 'urf of the public, this limit does not apply to it, because custom does not become common and widespread among the masses except after being repeated countless times.
Source pages p. 814

Commentary
This passage is a legal definition and classification of 'adah (custom) as a source in Islamic law, particularly within the Hanafi usul al-fiqh framework. The author first distinguishes between good and evil customs, emphasizing that not all prevalent practices are beneficial; some arise from ignorance and must be opposed through education and legislation. Then, he defines 'adah linguistically as repetition leading to ease, and terminologically as 'the recurring matter without a rational connection.' This distinction is crucial: if repetition is due to a necessary cause-effect relationship (e.g., a ring moving with a finger), it is not 'adah but rational necessity. The definition is broad, covering both individual habits and collective practices (which are synonymous with 'urf). A subtle point is that jurists sometimes say 'adah is realized by two or three repetitions for individuals, but for collective custom, countless repetitions are required for it to become widespread. This aligns with the Hanafi reliance on custom (al-'adah muhakkamah) as a subsidiary source of law, provided it does not contradict a clear textual evidence.

Key Terms
العادة (al-'ādah): الأمر المتكرر من غير علاقة عقلية
العرف (al-'urf): ما تعتاده الجماعات والجماهير من اتجاه عقلي وتفكير، وهو مرادف للعادة الجماعية
التلازم العقلي (al-talāzum al-'aqli): ارتباط سببي بين العلة والمعلول يحكم به العقل، وليس من العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the usul definition in the passage, what distinguishes 'ādah from rational necessity (talāzum 'aqli)?
A) 'Ādah requires repetition three times; rational necessity only once.
B) 'Ādah has no rational connection; rational necessity has a cause-effect link.
C) 'Ādah is always good; rational necessity can be bad.
D) 'Ādah is individual; rational necessity is collective.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'ādah is defined as a recurring matter without a rational connection; only beneficial customs that do not contradict a clear text are legally authoritative (al-'ādah muhakkamah).
