Analysis of the Definition of 'Urf and its Distinction from 'Adah
Analyzing the definition of 'urf and its relationship to 'adah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of 'urf in Hanafi usul, identify its conditions (majority prevalence and rational choice), and distinguish 'urf from 'adah using the relationship of absolute generality-specificity.

Core Idea
'Urf is a type of 'adah that requires majority prevalence and rational choice; not every habit is 'urf.

In Hanafi jurisprudence, custom ('urf) is a recognized source of law, but not every widespread habit qualifies. This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm dissects the definition of 'urf, clarifying its necessary conditions—majority practice and rational deliberation—and its relationship to the broader concept of 'adah. Understanding these distinctions is essential for applying the Hanafi maxim 'al-'adah muhakkamah' correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of 'urf
Locate the definition implicit in the passage: 'urf is a habit common among the majority of people in a saying or action arising from thinking and choice.
The author uses this definition to analyze.
2
Analyze the condition of majority prevalence
Note that 'urf requires a numerical quorum: the habit must be practiced by the majority (al-jumhur) in the place of its occurrence. If it is only individual or shared equally, it is not 'urf.
The word 'al-jumhur' in the definition signals this.
3
Analyze the condition of rational choice
Recognize that the habit must arise from thinking and choice, not from nature. Examples include commercial practices; natural regularities like puberty ages are excluded.
The phrase 'in a saying or action' implies volition.
4
Identify the types of 'urf
From the definition, deduce two divisions: verbal vs. practical (from 'in a saying or action'), and general vs. specific (from the indefinite 'qawm').
General 'urf binds all; specific 'urf binds only its domain.
5
Understand the relationship between 'adah and 'urf
Recognize that 'adah is the broader genus, 'urf is a species. The relationship is absolute generality-specificity: every 'urf is an 'adah, but not every 'adah is an 'urf.
This is a foundational logical distinction in Hanafi legal theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١١/٦٧ - تحليل هذا التعريف:
الفصل (٦٧) منشأ العادة والعرف وتعريفهما
۱ - يفيد هذا التعريف أنَّ العرف نوع من العادة، وهذا هو الواقع كما يعلم مما تقدم في تعريف العادة. فالعادة جنس أعم تحته أنواع منها العرف كما سنرى في بيان النسبة بين العرف والعادة.
۲ - ويفهم من هذا التعريف أيضاً أن تحقق العرف يعتمد على نصاب عددي من الناس لا بد منه، وهو أن يكون موضوع العرف قد اعتاده أكثر القوم في مكان جريانه. وهذا مستفاد من لفظ «الجمهور».
فإذا لم يكن الأمر المعتاد فاشياً بين أكثر القوم لا يتكون به عرف معتبر بل يكون من قبيل العادة الفردية أو العادة المشتركة (وهي التي يتساوى معتادوها وغيرهم عدداً). وقد يسمونها تساهلاً باسم العرف المشترك وهي في الحقيقة لم تبلغ حد العرف فلا يتناولها عند الإطلاق، ولا تأخذ حكمه.
٣ - ويشير التعريف أيضاً إلى أنواع العرف فهو يدل على انقسامه من وجه إلى عرف لفظي، وعرف عملي، وهذا مستفاد من قولنا : «في قول أو فعل. وكذا يدل على انقسامه من وجه آخر إلى عرف عام وعرف خاص، وهذا مستفاد من تنكير لفظ «قوم»، فإنه يشمل أهل بلد معين أو أهل حرفة خاصة، فيكون عرفهم خاصاً بهم؛ كما يشمل الناس أجمعين في جميع البلدان، فيكون عرفاً عاماً. وسيأتي بيان ذلك في بحث تقسيم العرف.
٤ - ويفيد هذا التعريف أخيراً أن العادة لا تسمى عرفاً إلا في الأمور المنبعثة عن التفكير والاختيار كالتعامل مثلاً على تقدير الكميات في بعض الأشياء بالوزن، وفي بعضها بالكيل، وفي بعضها بالعدد إلخ ... وكالتعامل في الزواج على أن المرأة تشتري بمهرها جهازاً من ملبوس ومفروش تحضره معها إلى بيت الزوج، وأنها لا تزف قبل أن يدفع الرجل معجل مهرها كله أو بعضه وكالتعامل على بعض أنواع من العقود كالاستصناع .
فيخرج عن معنى العرف ما يكون من الأمور الشائعة ناشئاً عن الطبيعة لا عن التفكير والاختيار كإسراع بلوغ الأشخاص في الأقاليم الحارة وبطئه في الأقاليم الباردة. فمثل هذا الأمر، وإن كان مطرداً أو غالباً في بعض البيئات، لا يسمى عرفاً، بل يسمى «عادة» كما رأينا في تعريف العادة.
وهذا المعنى يستفاد أيضاً من قولنا في قول أو فعل» لأن عادة من الناس في قول أو فعل لا تكون إلا عن عقل واختيار (۱)، وإلا كانت حادثاً طبيعياً عادياً، لا قولاً أو فعلاً.
المطلب الرابع: النسبة بين العادة والعرف
ما ١٢/٢٧ - يتضح من جميع تقدم أنَّ العادة أعم من العرف، لأنها تشمل العادة الناشئة عن عامل طبيعي والعادة الفردية وعادة الجمهور التي هي العرف.
فتكون النسبة بين العادة والعرف هي العموم والخصوص المطلق لأن العادة أعم مطلقاً وأبداً، والعرف أخص، إذ هو عادة مقيدة، فكل عرف هو عادة وليست كل عادة عرفاً، لأنّ العادة قد تكون فردية أو مشتركة .
(1) وهذا هو المراد بقول الفقهاء في تعريف العرف هو ما استقر في النفوس من جهة العقول، كما نقلناه آنفاً في الحاشية عن كتاب المستصفى للغزالي (ر: ف ١٠/٦٧ الحاشية).
(۲) العموم والخصوص في اصطلاح علم المنطق نوعان مطلق، ووجهي فالعموم والخصوص المطلق عندما يكون أحد الشيئين أعم من الآخر دائماً، والآخر أخص دائماً كما هنا، وقد تقدّم بيان ذلك أول نظرية العقود. (ف ١/٢٧). والوجهي عندما يكون كل منهما أعم من الآخر من وجه وأخص منه من وجه كالنسبة بين مفهومي «الأبيض» و«الملبوس» مثلاً: فالأبيض أعم من وجه لوجوده في الملبوس وغيره، والملبوس أعم من وجه لوجوده في الأبيض وغيره.
11/67 - Analysis of this definition:
Chapter (67) Origin of Custom ('adah) and 'Urf and their Definitions
1 - This definition indicates that 'urf is a type of 'adah, and this is the reality as known from what preceded in the definition of 'adah. So 'adah is a more general genus under which there are types, including 'urf, as we will see in the statement of the relationship between 'urf and 'adah.
2 - It is also understood from this definition that the realization of 'urf depends on a numerical quorum of people that is indispensable, which is that the subject of 'urf must be habitually practiced by the majority of the people in the place of its occurrence. This is derived from the word 'al-jumhur' (the majority).
If the customary matter is not prevalent among the majority of the people, a recognized 'urf is not formed by it; rather, it is of the type of individual habit or shared habit (which is that whose practitioners and non-practitioners are equal in number). They may loosely call it 'common 'urf', but in reality it has not reached the level of 'urf, so it is not included in the unrestricted term, nor does it take its ruling.
3 - The definition also points to the types of 'urf, as it indicates its division from one perspective into verbal 'urf and practical 'urf. This is derived from our statement: 'in a saying or action.' Likewise, it indicates its division from another perspective into general 'urf and specific 'urf. This is derived from the indefinite form of the word 'qawm' (people), for it includes the people of a particular country or the people of a particular craft, so their 'urf is specific to them; and it also includes all people in all countries, so it is general 'urf. This will be explained in the discussion on the division of 'urf.
4 - Finally, this definition indicates that a habit is not called 'urf except in matters arising from thinking and choice, such as transactions like estimating quantities of some things by weight, some by measure, some by number, etc., and such as transactions in marriage where the wife purchases with her dowry household goods of clothing and furnishings which she brings to the husband's house, and that she is not escorted to her husband before the man pays all or part of her prompt dowry, and such as transactions on some types of contracts like istisna' (commission to manufacture).
Therefore, what falls outside the meaning of 'urf are matters that are prevalent but arising from nature, not from thinking and choice, such as the rapid attainment of puberty in hot regions and its delay in cold regions. Such a matter, even if consistent or predominant in some environments, is not called 'urf, but rather 'adah, as we saw in the definition of 'adah.
This meaning is also derived from our statement 'in a saying or action', because the custom of people in a saying or action is only by reason and choice (1); otherwise it would be a natural habitual occurrence, not a saying or action.
Fourth Section: The Relationship between 'Adah and 'Urf
12/27 - It is clear from all that preceded that 'adah is more general than 'urf, because it includes the habit arising from a natural factor, individual habit, and the habit of the majority which is 'urf.
Thus the relationship between 'adah and 'urf is absolute generality and specificity, because 'adah is absolutely and always more general, and 'urf is more specific, since it is a restricted habit. So every 'urf is a habit, but not every habit is 'urf, because habit may be individual or shared.
(1) This is what is meant by the jurists' definition of 'urf as 'what is established in the souls from the aspect of intellects', as we previously quoted in the footnote from al-Ghazali's al-Mustasfa (see: section 10/67 footnote).
(2) Generality and specificity in the terminology of logic are of two types: absolute and verbal. Absolute generality and specificity is when one of two things is always more general than the other, and the other always more specific, as here. This has been explained earlier at the beginning of the Theory of Contracts (section 1/27). Verbal generality and specificity is when each is more general than the other from one aspect and more specific from another, like the relationship between the concepts 'white' and 'clothing' for example: 'white' is more general from one aspect due to its existence in clothing and other things, and 'clothing' is more general from one aspect due to its existence in white and other things.
Source pages p. 817

Commentary
This passage is an analytical expansion of the definition of 'urf (custom) as a type of 'adah (habit). It is from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm, a modern treatise on general fiqh theory. The passage first dissects the definition, then explains the relationship between 'adah and 'urf. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, proceeds point by point:

First, the definition shows that 'urf is a species of 'adah. 'Adah is the broader genus, and 'urf is one of its types.

Second, for a practice to constitute 'urf, it must be prevalent among the majority (al-jumhur) of a community in a given locale. If it is only practiced by a minority or is equally practiced by half the people, it is an individual or shared habit, not a recognized 'urf. Such weak habits do not have the legal force of 'urf.

Third, the definition indicates the divisions of 'urf: (a) by category into verbal 'urf (usage of words) and practical 'urf (customary actions), derived from 'in a saying or action'; (b) by scope into general 'urf (common to all people) and specific 'urf (particular to a region or profession), derived from the indefinite 'qawm' which can refer to specific groups or all humanity.

Fourth, 'urf is distinguished from mere habit by being based on conscious choice and reflection. Examples include commercial practices like estimating quantities by weight or measure, marriage customs like bringing household goods with the dowry, or contracts like istisna'. In contrast, natural regularities such as early puberty in hot climates are not 'urf because they are involuntary. The phrase 'sayings and actions' implicitly requires rationality will; otherwise it is a natural event, not a subject of custom.

Finally, the relationship between 'adah and 'urf is absolute generality and specificity: every 'urf is an 'adah, but not every 'adah is an 'urf. 'Adah includes natural habits, individual habits, and the majority habit that is 'urf. This logical relationship is explained with a footnote on absolute vs. verbal generality.

In Hanafi usul, 'urf is a recognized source of law. This analysis clarifies its definition and scope, ensuring that only widely accepted, rationally chosen practices qualify as legally binding custom.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): عادة شائعة بين جمهور الناس في قول أو فعل ناشئة عن التفكير والاختيار
العادة (al-'adah): جنس أعم يشمل العرف وغيره من العادات الفردية والطبيعية
الجمهور (al-jumhur): أكثر القوم في مكان جريان العرف
العرف العام (al-'urf al-'amm): عادة شائعة بين الناس أجمعين في جميع البلدان
العرف الخاص (al-'urf al-khass): عادة شائعة بين أهل بلد معين أو أهل حرفة خاصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the passage, which of the following is a condition for a practice to be considered 'urf?
A) It must be practiced by at least half the people.
B) It must arise from thinking and choice.
C) It must be written down in legal texts.
D) It must be universal in all countries.

Key Takeaway
For a custom to be legally binding in Hanafi jurisprudence, it must be practiced by the majority and originate from rational deliberation, not from nature.

Division of Custom: Verbal, Practical, General, and Specific
Classification of 'urf into types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any instance of 'urf as verbal or practical, general or specific, and distinguish it from metaphor based on the presence of a qarina or 'alaqa.

Core Idea
Verbal custom is understood immediately; if a qarina or 'alaqa is needed, it is majaz, not 'urf.

In Hanafi legal theory, custom ('urf) serves as a subsidiary source of law, especially in mu'amalat. This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' provides the foundational classification: custom is either verbal (relating to word usage) or practical (relating to habitual actions). It further divides into general (widespread) and specific (limited to a place or group). Understanding these categories is crucial for a mufti when deriving rulings from custom, as each type has different evidentiary force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two primary categories
Note that 'urf is first divided by its subject matter: verbal (lafzi) and practical ('amali). Verbal custom concerns the meanings of words; practical custom concerns habitual actions or transactions.
This is the most fundamental division.
2
Define verbal custom precisely
Read the definition: verbal custom is when a word or phrase is used so commonly for a meaning that it becomes the immediate understanding when used absolutely, without need for a contextual indicator (qarina) or rational connection ('alaqa).
If a qarina or 'alaqa is required, it is metaphor, not custom.
3
Distinguish verbal custom from metaphor
The author gives examples: 'dirhams' means current currency, not silver coins; 'house' may mean room or building. Contrast with metaphor examples like 'the court ruled' (attributing the judge's action to the court) which relies on a rational connection.
Verbal custom = immediate understanding; metaphor = requires extra step.
4
Understand practical custom
Practical custom is the habituation of certain actions or transactions, like how people eat/drink or conduct business. It is not about words but about behavior.
Practical custom is often invoked in mu'amalat, not 'ibadat.
5
Apply the cross-cutting division: general vs. specific
Both verbal and practical custom can be general (widespread in all regions) or specific (limited to a place or a profession). This affects the scope of legal application.
General custom has broader authority than specific custom.

Chapter Sixty-Eight: Division of Custom. 1/68 - Custom either pertains to the use of certain words in meanings that people customarily use them for, or it pertains to the habituation of types of actions or transactions. Hence custom is divided by its subject matter into two types: verbal custom and practical custom. Then from another perspective, custom - whether verbal or practical - in terms of its scope is either general, widespread in all countries among all people, or limited to a particular country or place, or specific to a group of people such as professionals, artisans, or scholars. Hence custom is also divided into general custom and specific custom. Here is an explanation of these types and examples. First: Verbal Custom and Practical Custom. 2/68 A - Verbal Custom: Verbal custom is that the use of certain words or phrases becomes widespread among people for a specific meaning, so that meaning becomes the immediate understanding that comes to their minds when the word is used absolutely, without a contextual indicator or rational connection. For example, the use of the word 'dirhams' to mean the current currency in the country, whatever its type and value, even paper money today, rather than silver coinage of a specific weight and value. Similarly, the use of the word 'house' in some countries to mean 'room' and in others to mean the entire 'dwelling'. This type of custom is essentially a private language for its users. If understanding the intended meaning requires a contextual indicator or rational connection, it is not custom but rather metaphor. An example of a contextual indicator: If a person picks up a small stick and swears he will kill someone with it, it is understood from this indicator that he means painful beating. Likewise, if someone says to another, 'I give you this thing for ten dinars,' it is understood from the consideration of exchange that he means sale by way of metaphor. An example of a rational connection: Our saying 'The court ruled against so-and-so' means that the judge ruled, because the court is the place. This attribution is considered permissible and acceptable due to a connection or link perceived by the mind between the circumstance and the place, whereby the action can be considered as emanating from the place regardless of the actual agent. Similarly, you say: 'The prince made a pact with such-and-such city' or 'fought it,' meaning the people of the city. These rhetorical styles of expression are forms of metaphor in eloquence because they are based on the presence of a contextual indicator or relationship, and they have nothing to do with verbal custom, which is considered a special positive language whose meanings are customary facts derived from the mere word. 3/68 B - Practical Custom: As for practical custom, it is people's habituation to something from ordinary actions or civil transactions. By ordinary actions is meant people's personal actions in their vital affairs that are not based on exchange of interests or creation of rights, such as eating and drinking.
Source pages p. 819

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of 'urf (custom) as a source of law. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by stating the two fundamental categories based on subject matter: verbal custom (al-'urf al-lafzi) and practical custom (al-'urf al-'amali). Verbal custom concerns the usage of words and phrases, while practical custom concerns habitual actions and transactions. Then he introduces a cross-cutting classification based on scope: general custom (al-'urf al-'amm) widespread across all regions, and specific custom (al-'urf al-khass) limited to a particular place or group. The author proceeds to elaborate on verbal custom with examples: the word 'dirhams' in commercial contexts refers to current currency, not specifically silver coins; the word 'house' may mean a single room or an entire building depending on local convention. He emphasizes that verbal custom is essentially a specialized language; if understanding requires a contextual indicator (qarina) or rational connection ('alaqa 'aqliyya), it is not custom but metaphor (majaz). He provides two examples of contextual indicators: a man swearing to 'kill' with a small stick (clearly meaning beat, not slay), and offering a 'gift' in exchange for money (meaning sale). He also illustrates rational connection with the phrase 'the court ruled' where the action is attributed to the court despite the judge being the true subject, similar to 'the prince made a pact with the city' meaning its inhabitants. These are rhetorical metaphors, not verbal custom. Finally, the author introduces practical custom, defining it as habitual behavior in personal affairs (like eating and drinking) or in civil transactions. This classification is foundational for understanding how custom operates as a subsidiary source of law in the Hanafi madhhab.

Key Terms
العرف اللفظي (al-'urf al-lafzi): شيوع استعمال لفظ أو تركيب في معنى معين بحيث يصبح هذا المعنى هو المتبادر إلى الذهن عند الإطلاق بلا قرينة
العرف العملي (al-'urf al-'amali): اعتياد الناس على نوع من الأفعال العادية أو المعاملات المدنية
العرف العام (al-'urf al-'amm): العرف الفاشي في جميع البلدان بين جميع الناس
العرف الخاص (al-'urf al-khass): العرف المقتصر على بلد أو مكان أو فئة معينة كأرباب الصنائع
قرينة (qarina): ما يصحب اللفظ من دليل سياقي يبين المراد
علاقة عقلية ('alaqa 'aqliyya): ارتباط عقلي بين المعنى الأصلي والمعنى المجازي يسوغ استعمال اللفظ في غير معناه
مجاز (majaz): استعمال اللفظ في غير معناه الأصلي لعلاقة مع قرينة مانعة من المعنى الأصلي

The Authority of Verbal Custom over Literal Meaning
Explains the principle that custom overrides literal meaning in speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'al-haqiqah tutraku bi dalalat al-‘adah' and apply it to interpret the speech of a person making an oath, vow, will, endowment, or contract.

Core Idea
Custom transfers words to new meanings; the literal is abandoned when custom speaks.

This passage establishes a cornerstone of Hanafi legal interpretation: when custom changes the common understanding of words, the literal meaning gives way. Without this principle, every contract, oath, and vow would be interpreted against the speaker's intent. This lesson unpacks the maxim and its wide application across fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its source
Locate the maxim 'al-haqiqah tutraku bi dalalat al-‘adah' and note its codification in the Majallah (M/40) and its emphasis by later Hanafi scholars like Ibn 'Abidin.
The maxim is a sughra (minor) qa'ida within the broader Hanafi qawa'd system.
2
Understand the reasoning: why custom overrides literal
Analyze the passage's argument: prevailing custom transfers words to new customary meanings (al-haqiqah al-urfiyyah). If literal meanings were enforced, speakers would be bound to unintended consequences.
The change happens because the speaker's community no longer uses the word in its original linguistic sense.
3
Apply the maxim to each of the five categories
Work through the five furu' given: a person making an oath (حالف), a vow (ناذر), a will (موصي), an endowment (واقف), and any contract (عاقد). For each, explain how custom determines the intended meaning.
The principle is general: 'the speech of every contractor is carried upon his custom'.
4
Recognize the scope: all speech acts
Note that the passage says the maxim applies to 'all verbal dispositions' (sā'ir tasarrufātihi al-qawliyyah), including divorces and acknowledgments. Custom is the default interpretive lens.
This is broader than the five examples; it covers any utterance with legal effect.
5
Distinguish linguistic vs. customary reality
Define al-haqiqah al-lughawiyyah (original lexical meaning) and al-haqiqah al-urfiyyah (customary meaning). Understand that the latter takes precedence when there is a conflict.
The existence of a widespread custom is essential; isolated or random usages do not override the literal.

(١) العقوبة التعزيرية في الإسلام هي التي لم يحدّد الشرع لها مقداراً ولا نوعاً، وإنما تركها مفوضة لآراء الحكام وأنظارهم بحسب المصلحة في كل زمن، كما تقدم في نظرية المؤيدات ( ر ف ١/٥٠)
٨٨٠
Chapter Sixty-Nine: The Authority of Custom. 1/69: Custom in the view of Islamic law is considered a highly important basis for many practical rulings among people in all branches and chapters of fiqh, and it has wide-reaching authority in generating rulings, renewing them, amending them, specifying them, making them unrestricted, and restricting them. Custom is born from developing and renewed needs, then becomes a governing system by which and upon which the wheel of transactions among people turns, and it reveals the meanings of their speech and intentions, and delineates the boundaries of rights and obligations, and illuminates the path of judiciary. So in considering custom, there is great facilitation that dispenses with many detailed texts in legislative rulings and in contracts of transactions, relying on what is known and familiar in various potential incidents. 2/69: And texts of legislation and codification cannot substitute for custom, because they cannot encompass all details and possibilities, and also many of their imperatives themselves are built upon custom and the ruling changes with the change of custom, so a fixed ruling cannot be established therein. There is hardly any chapter of fiqh where custom does not have a role in its rulings, even the chapter of crimes and punishments. For example, in the crime of assault on dignity by insult and humiliation, only that speech is considered a crime deserving ta'zir punishment that is, according to people's custom, insult and humiliation. [PAGE_824] Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. And the ta'zir punishment itself (1) is only legislated to the extent considered sufficient for suppression in the view of rational people and their custom according to the degree of the crime; so that it is not more than the crime demands, thus becoming oppression and crime itself, and not less, which would involve leniency in people's rights, and remove deterrence. We will present below the fiqh theory of the authority of custom and its ruling in its various types previously mentioned. First: Authority of Verbal Custom. 3/69: The fiqh view on the ruling of this verbal custom and the extent of its authority is that every speaker carries his speech according to his language and custom, so it is directed to the meanings intended by custom at the time of speaking, even if they contradict the real meanings for which the word was placed in the original language. This is because the prevailing custom has transferred those words to other meanings that have become the customary reality intended by the word, as opposed to the linguistic reality. For if the speaker's speech were directed to its linguistic reality rather than the customary reality which is its meaning in the speaker's custom, it would result in binding the speaker in his contracts, acknowledgments, oaths, divorces, and all his verbal dispositions to something he does not intend and people do not understand from his speech. From this, the fuqaha established the rule: "The literal meaning is abandoned by indication of custom" (M/40), and they laid down the general principle: "The speech of the one making an oath, the one making a vow, the testator, the endower, and every contractor is carried upon his language and custom, even if they contradict the language of the Arabs and the language of the Lawgiver" (See Majmu'at Rasa'il Ibn 'Abidin 2/133 quoting from Fatawa al-'Allamah Qasim). (1) Ta'zir punishment in Islam is that for which the Law has not specified a measure or type, but rather left it to the opinions and views of rulers according to interest in every time, as previously mentioned in the theory of sanctions (see 1/50).
Source pages p. 823

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the authority of verbal custom (al-urf al-lafzi) in Islamic law. The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, opens by establishing custom as a foundational source for many practical rulings, with vast authority in generating, renewing, modifying, and specifying them. He notes that custom arises from evolving needs and becomes a governing system for transactions. A key point is that custom facilitates the law by rendering many detailed texts unnecessary, as it provides understood meanings in diverse situations. The passage then emphasizes that legislation cannot substitute for custom because statutes cannot cover every detail, and many rules themselves change with custom. Custom penetrates all areas of fiqh, including criminal law where the determination of insult depends on customary standards. The focus then shifts to verbal custom: every person's speech is interpreted according to his own language and custom, even if it departs from the original linguistic meanings. This is because custom transfers words to new customary meanings that become the intended reality. If speech were taken literally, it would impose unintended obligations on speakers in contracts, oaths, divorces, etc. The fuqaha therefore established the maxim: "The literal meaning is abandoned by indication of custom" (al-haqiqah tutraku bi dalalat al-'adah). They also formulated a general principle: the speech of a person making an oath, vow, will, endowment, or any contract is interpreted according to his custom, even if that contradicts Arabic or the language of the Lawgiver. This principle is crucial for muftis and judges when interpreting speech in legal contexts.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Custom: what is established and habitual among people in their speech and actions
الحقيقة العرفية (al-haqiqah al-urfiyyah): The customary meaning of a word as understood by the people of a particular community, as opposed to its original linguistic meaning
الحقيقة اللغوية (al-haqiqah al-lughawiyyah): The original lexical meaning of a word as defined by Arabic lexicographers
العادة (al-adah): Habit or custom; used interchangeably with urf in this context

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does custom override the literal meaning in interpreting speech?
A) Because literal meanings are unreliable
B) Because custom transfers words to new intended meanings, and if we enforced literal meanings we would bind speakers to what they did not intend
C) Because the Lawgiver commanded it in the Quran
D) Because all scholars have agreed that custom is more important than text

Key Takeaway
Custom transfers words to new intended meanings; the maxim 'al-haqiqah tutraku bi dalalat al-‘adah' ensures that speakers are bound only by what they intend.

The Authority of Verbal Custom in Hanafi Jurisprudence
Explains the principle that words are interpreted by custom, with applications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-haqiqah tutraku bi dalalat al-'adah' to interpret oaths, endowments, and bequests according to customary meaning.

Core Idea
Words are interpreted by the speaker's custom, not by their literal dictionary definition.

This lesson introduces the theory of verbal custom (al-'urf al-lafzi) in Hanafi law. The principle that literal meaning is abandoned for customary meaning is a cornerstone for understanding contracts, oaths, and endowments. We will see how this maxim operates through concrete examples from Hanafi fiqh, including an oath about placing a foot and endowment stipulations.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Identify the maxim: 'al-haqiqah tutraku bi dalalat al-'adah' (the literal meaning is abandoned due to the indication of custom). Note its scope: all verbal dispositions.
This is a sughra (minor) maxim, but widely applied.
2
Identify the customary meaning
For each case, determine what the word/phrase customarily means in the speaker's community at the time of speaking, even if it differs from the original linguistic meaning.
The 'custom' here refers to the prevalent usage of the speaker's milieu.
3
Apply to the oath example
The oath 'I will not place my foot in this house' is interpreted customarily as 'I will not enter the house.' Thus, entering riding breaks the oath; merely inserting the foot without entering does not.
This is the ruling in al-Durr al-Mukhtar.
4
Apply to endowment examples
The term 'nazir' (supervisor) in a waqf deed is interpreted according to the custom of the time: if it meant 'trustee' in that era, then trustee; if 'overseer', then overseer. Similarly, 'al-faridah al-shar'iyyah' in a family endowment customarily means inheritance shares (male:female 2:1), not equal distribution.
The testator's intent, expressed through his custom, is paramount.
5
Recognize the general principle
The general principle is that every speaker's words are interpreted according to his language and custom, even if they contradict the Arabic language or the language of the Lawgiver.
This is from Ibn Abidin's collection.

الفصل (٦٩) سلطان العرف
فقد يكون معنى اللفظ في أصل اللغة طلاقاً فيصبح في العرف زجراً لا دلالة له على الطلاق أو بالعكس.
وقد يكون معنى الكلام في أصل اللغة عقداً ملزماً فيصبح في العرف وعداً غير ملزم وقد يكون معناه بيعاً ومعاوضة فيصبح هبة، ونحو ذلك. فالعبرة دائماً إنما هي للمعنى العرفي حين التكلم، لتثبيت الأحكام والالتزامات على وفقه ومقتضاه .
٤/٦٩ - وعلى هذا الأساس قرر الفقهاء أحكاماً كثيرة منها : أنه لو حلف الإنسان أن لا يضع قدمه في دار فلان انصرفت اليمين إلى معنى دخول الدار ، لأنه المعنى العرفي، لا إلى مجرد وضع القدم الذي هو الحقيقة اللغوية. فلو دخلها راكباً دون أن تمس قدمه أرضها يحنث في يمينه شرعاً وتجب عليه كفارة ولو مد رجله من خارجها فوضعها فيها دون أن يدخل لا يحنث . ( ر ) الدر المختار ٨٤/٣) .
۲ - إذا شرط الواقف في صك وقفيته تعيين «ناظر» على وقفه، وكان معنى الناظر في عرف زمانه المتولي الذي يتولى إدارة الوقف من جباية وتعمير وإنفاق، حمل على معنى المتولي كما عليه العرف القديم، وإن كان معناه المشرف المراقب على المتولي انصرف إليه كما عليه عرفنا الحديث . وكذا لو وقف مالاً على ذريته وشرط أن توزع الغلة بينهم على الفريضة الشرعية فالراجح من رأي فقهائنا أنه يعطى للذكر منهم مثل حظ الأنثيين، لأنَّ هذا هو المعنى العرفي بين الناس لكلمة «الفريضة الشرعية» التي تفسر بها إرادة الواقف، إذ المراد بها التفاضل المعروف بين الذكر والأنثى في الميراث فيحمل هذا التعبير على هذا المعنى العرفي له، وإن كان الأحسن شرعاً أن يسوي الإنسان في العطية بين الذكور والإناث من أولاده، لأن العبرة إنما هي لإرادة الواقف في توزيع غلة وقفه. وإن كلامه يجب تنزيله على لغته وعرفه التي تعبر عن إرادته المحترمة، لا على المسلك الأفضل إذا كان خلاف إرادته.
·
٨٨١
Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. And the discretionary punishment itself (1) is only legitimate to the extent deemed sufficient for deterrence in the view of rational people and their custom according to the degree of the crime; such that it is not more than what the crime necessitates, thereby becoming oppression and crime, nor less, resulting in leniency toward people's rights and loss of deterrent fear. We will present below the jurisprudential theory regarding the authority of custom and its ruling in its various types previously mentioned.
First: The Authority of Verbal Custom
369 - The jurisprudential view regarding the ruling of this verbal custom and the extent of its authority is that every speaker's words are interpreted according to his language and custom, and they are directed to the meanings intended by custom at the time of speaking, even if they contradict the real meanings for which the word was originally coined in the language. This is because the emergent custom has transferred those words to other meanings that have become the customary reality intended by the word in contrast to the linguistic reality. If the speaker's words were construed according to their linguistic reality rather than the customary one that is his meaning in his custom, it would result in binding the speaker in his contracts, acknowledgments, oaths, divorces, and all his verbal dispositions to what he does not intend and what people do not understand from his speech.
Hence, the jurists established the maxim: 'The literal meaning is abandoned due to the indication of custom.' (M/40) And they laid down the general principle: 'The words of one who swears, vows, bequeaths, endows, and every contracting party are interpreted according to his language and custom, even if they contradict the Arabic language and the language of the Lawgiver.' (Ref: Ibn Abidin's Collection of Treatises 2/133, quoting from Fatwas of Allama Qasim).
(1) The discretionary punishment in Islam is that for which the Law has not specified a quantity or type, but rather left it to the discretion of rulers and their views according to expediency in every time, as previously discussed in the theory of subsidiary means (see 1/50).
880

Chapter (69): Authority of Custom
Thus the meaning of a word in the original language may be divorce, but it becomes in custom a reprimand with no indication of divorce, or vice versa. The meaning of an utterance in the original language may be a binding contract, but it becomes in custom a non-binding promise; its meaning may be a sale and exchange, but it becomes a gift, and so on. The consideration is always for the customary meaning at the time of speaking, for establishing rulings and obligations accordingly and as required.
470 - On this basis, the jurists established many rulings, including: If a person swears that he will not place his foot in someone's house, the oath is directed to the meaning of entering the house, because it is the customary meaning, not merely placing the foot, which is the linguistic reality. So if he enters it riding without his foot touching its ground, he breaks his oath legally and must pay expiation. And if he extends his leg from outside and places it inside without entering, he does not break his oath. (Ref: al-Durr al-Mukhtar 3/84).
2. If the endower stipulates in his endowment deed the appointment of a 'nazir' (supervisor) over his endowment, and the meaning of 'nazir' in the custom of his time is the trustee who manages the endowment—collecting revenue, maintaining, and spending—it is interpreted as meaning trustee, as per the old custom. But if its meaning is the overseer who supervises the trustee, it is directed to that, as per our modern custom. Similarly, if one endows money for his descendants and stipulates that the revenue be distributed among them according to 'the prescribed share (al-faridah al-shar'iyyah),' the preponderant view of our jurists is that the male is given the share of two females, because this is the customary meaning among people of the phrase 'prescribed share' by which the testator's intent is interpreted, as what is meant is the known differentiation between male and female in inheritance. Thus this expression is interpreted according to this customary meaning, even though legally it is better for a person to equalize between male and female children in gifts, because the consideration is only the testator's intent in distributing the revenue of his endowment. His words must be understood according to his language and custom which express his respected intent, not according to the better course if it contradicts his intent.
Source pages p. 824

Commentary
This passage, from al-Zarqa's 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm', discusses the authority of verbal custom (al-'urf al-lafzi) in Hanafi jurisprudence. The author first establishes that discretionary punishments (ta'zir) are determined by custom, then focuses on the principle that a speaker's words are interpreted according to his custom, even if they differ from their literal linguistic meanings. The key maxim here is 'al-Haqiqah tutraku bi dalalat al-'adah' (literal meaning is abandoned due to customary indication). This passage provides the jurisprudential foundation: because emergent custom has transferred words to new conventional meanings, the speaker is bound only by those customary meanings. The general principle is that all verbal dispositions—oaths, contracts, endowments, divorces, etc.—are interpreted according to the custom of the speaker. The author then presents two applied examples from Hanafi fiqh: (1) an oath not to place a foot in a house is interpreted as an oath not to enter the house; entering riding constitutes a breach, while merely placing the foot in without entering does not. (2) The term 'nazir' in a waqf deed is interpreted according to the custom of the testator's time, and the phrase 'prescribed share' (al-faridah al-shar'iyyah) in a family endowment is interpreted to mean the inheritance shares (male: female = 2:1), overriding the general recommendation of equality in gifts because the testator's intent, expressed through his custom, governs.

Key Terms
العرف اللفظي (al-'urf al-lafzi): Verbal custom: the conventional meaning of a word established by the usage of a community, as opposed to its original linguistic meaning.
الحقيقة تترك بدلالة العادة (al-haqiqah tutraku bi dalalat al-'adah): A legal maxim meaning that the literal (linguistic) meaning is abandoned in favor of the meaning indicated by custom.
العرف (al-'urf): Custom: recurrent practices or conventional meanings that have become established in a community and are considered legally binding under certain conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person swears not to place his foot in someone's house, and then enters the house riding a horse (so his foot does not touch the ground), does he break his oath?
A) No, because he did not place his foot on the ground.
B) Yes, because custom interprets 'placing foot' as 'entering'.
C) Only if he dismounts and places his foot.
D) Only if he enters barefoot.

Key Takeaway
The customary meaning governs verbal dispositions, overriding literal linguistic definitions.

Customary Meaning Overrides Literal Meaning: The Principle and Its Applications
Principle and five custom-based rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi qawida that gives priority to customary meaning and apply it to five concrete cases in oaths, endowments, sales, and defects.

Core Idea
Custom gives words their legal bite, not the dictionary.

Words are not static; their meanings shift over time and by context. In Hanafi fiqh, when a word's customary meaning differs from its original literal meaning, the customary meaning governs legal interpretation. This lesson presents the key qawida and five applications from the works of Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, showing how custom shapes rulings on oaths, endowments, sales, and liability for defects.

Guided Path
1
State the Qawida
Memorize the principle: 'العبرة للمعنى العرفي حين التكلم' – Consideration is given to the customary meaning at the time of speaking. Understand that this rule operates wherever language is used in legal contexts (contracts, oaths, wills, etc.).
The qawida is a wusta (intermediate) maxim, applicable across many fiqh chapters.
2
Analyze the Oath Example
Read the first furu': an oath not to 'place one's foot' in a house. Identify the literal meaning (physical placement of foot) vs. the customary meaning (entering the house). Apply the qawida: the oath is interpreted as not entering. Then verify the subsidiary rulings: riding in counts (customary meaning of entry), while mere foot insertion without entry does not.
This shows how custom overrides literal meaning to produce a legally sound result.
3
Work Through the Endowment Examples
Examine the second furu': the term 'nāẓir' in an endowment deed. Recognize that its meaning changes with time – it can mean manager or supervisor depending on custom. Similarly, the phrase 'sharʿī prescribed shares' is interpreted by custom as the inheritance ratio (2:1) not equal division. Understand that the endower's intention is determined by his customary usage, not by ideal fiqh.
These examples illustrate that custom can even resolve ambiguity in endowment stipulations.
4
Explore the Sales Examples
Review the third and fourth furuat: sale of fruits using 'ḍamīn' or partnership shares using 'muqāṣara' are valid because custom gives these words a sales meaning, even though their Sharīʿa meanings are different. Also, a seller's disclaimer of defects using phrases like 'ḥāḍir ḥalāl' is effective by custom – the buyer cannot return the item for defects.
Custom creates new contract types and disclaimers that are legally recognized.
5
Note the Incomplete Divorce Example
The passage begins a fifth furu' about divorce with new expressions, but it is cut off. Reflect on how the same qawida would apply: if people customarily use certain words for divorce, those words become effective even if not the classical formula.
This extension shows the principle's breadth in family law.

Section (69): Authority of Custom
So the meaning of a word in the original language may be divorce, then in custom it becomes scolding with no indication of divorce, or vice versa. And the meaning of an utterance in the original language may be a binding contract, then in custom it becomes a non-binding promise, and its meaning may be sale and exchange, then it becomes a gift, and so on. The consideration is always for the customary meaning at the time of speaking, to establish rulings and obligations in accordance with it and its implications.
4/69 - On this basis, the jurists established many rulings, including: if a person swears that he will not place his foot in so-and-so's house, the oath is interpreted to mean entering the house, because that is the customary meaning, not just placing the foot, which is the literal meaning. So if he enters it riding without his foot touching its ground, he breaks his oath legally and must give expiation, and if he stretches his leg from outside and places it inside without entering, he does not break the oath. (Reference: al-Durr al-Mukhtar 3/84)
2 - If the endower stipulates in his endowment deed the appointment of a 'nāẓir' over his endowment, and the meaning of nāẓir in the custom of his time was the mutawallī who manages the endowment in terms of collection, maintenance, and spending, it is interpreted as mutawallī according to the old custom; and if its meaning was the supervisor overseeing the mutawallī, it is directed to that as per our modern custom. Likewise, if one endows wealth on his descendants and stipulates that the proceeds be distributed among them according to the 'sharʿī prescribed shares' (al-farīḍa al-sharʿiyya), the preponderant view of our jurists is that the male is given the share of two females, because this is the customary meaning among the people of the word 'al-farīḍa al-sharʿiyya' by which the endower's intention is interpreted, since what is meant by it is the known differentiation between male and female in inheritance; so this expression is interpreted according to its customary meaning, even though it is legally better for a person to equalize in gift-giving between male and female children, because the consideration is the endower's intention in distributing the proceeds of his endowment. And his words must be interpreted according to his language and custom that express his respected intention, not according to the better course if it contradicts his intention.

Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom
.(145
And the ruling of a will is like the ruling of an endowment in this regard (Reference: Rasā'il Ibn ʿĀbidīn 2)
Likewise, if the endower stipulates giving specified dinars or dirhams monthly or yearly, and the dinar or dirham in the custom of his time had a specific weight, then its weight changed, then consideration is given to the weight that was in his time.
3 - The sale of fruits on their trees is concluded with the words 'al-ḍamīn' and 'al-ḍamān' in our Syrian lands, because it is the customary wording there today among the owners of orchards and vineyards. The seller says 'ḍammantuka' and the buyer says 'ḍamintu', even though 'al-ḍamīn' and 'al-ḍamān' in the language of the Law mean commitment and obligation to pay for damages and the like. Similarly, the sale of one of the two partners in a jointly owned animal of his share to the other partner is concluded with the wording 'al-muqāṣara' because of their familiarity with this wording in this sale. The seller says: 'qāṣartuka ʿalā ḥiṣṣatī bikadhā' and the buyer partner accepts. (Reference: Radd al-Muḥtār beginning of Buyūʿ 4/9)
4
4 - It is valid for the seller to stipulate in the sales contract a general disclaimer of liability for defects, and then the buyer has no right to recourse against him for anything no matter what defects appear in the sold item. Therefore, if someone sells something today and expresses this disclaimer of liability for defects by some customary expressions, such as if he sells something as 'ḥāḍir ḥalāl' or 'kawm turāb' or 'ḥarrāq ʿalā al-zannād' or similar expressions that common people today have become accustomed to in this context, then the buyer may not return the defective item whether the defect is apparent or hidden, because people have become familiar with these expressions as stipulating the seller's disclaimer of liability for all defects. (Reference: al-Majalla / 343 / and its commentary by al-Atāsī, and also see Radd al-Muḥtār 8/90)
5 - If people become accustomed to effecting divorce with new words or expressions that become widespread...
Source pages p. 825

Commentary
This passage presents the principle that in legal interpretation, customary meaning ('urf) takes precedence over literal linguistic meaning when determining legal effects. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, a leading contemporary Hanafi jurist, explains that words can shift meanings: a divorce word in custom may become mere scolding, and a binding contract word may become a non-binding promise. The rule is that consideration is always given to the customary meaning at the time of utterance. Five illustrative rulings from Hanafi fiqh are given. First, an oath not to place one's foot in a house is interpreted as not entering, so riding in without foot touching ground causes expiation, but merely inserting the foot from outside does not. Second, in endowments, the term 'nāẓir' is interpreted according to the custom of the time; and 'sharʿī prescribed shares' for distribution among descendants is interpreted as the inheritance ratio (2:1 male to female) because that is the customary understanding, even though equal giving is preferred. Third, sales of fruits or partnership shares are valid using customary expressions like 'ḍamīn' and 'muqāṣara' even if these have different Sharīʿa meanings. Fourth, a seller may disclaim liability for defects using customary phrases like 'ḥāḍir ḥalāl', and the buyer cannot return the item for defects. Fifth (incomplete), new expressions for divorce become effective once customary. These examples show the robust role of custom in shaping legal rulings.

Key Terms
العرف (al-ʿurf): Customary usage that determines the meaning of words and actions for legal purposes.
الحقيقة اللغوية (al-ḥaqīqa al-lughawiyya): The literal, original meaning of a word in the Arabic language.
الناظر (al-nāẓir): A person appointed to oversee an endowment; meaning varies with custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi qawida, what determines the legal meaning of a word when literal and customary meanings conflict?
A) The literal meaning is always followed.
B) The customary meaning at the time of speaking is followed.
C) The meaning is determined by the judge's preference.
D) All possible meanings are considered invalid.

Key Takeaway
Customary meaning overrides literal meaning in legal interpretation, as per the Hanafi qawida: العبرة للمعنى العرفي حين التكلم.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate
8 min
Authority of Custom: The Maxim al-Ādah Muḥakkamah
Explaining the legal authority of customary practice with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-ādah muḥakkamah' to interpret oaths, agency contracts, and spousal maintenance, and identify when customary practice overrides literal linguistic meanings.

Core Idea
Custom is authoritative: it restricts absolute words and directs legal rulings to customary practice.

In this passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm, we explore the foundational maxim 'al-ādah muḥakkamah' — custom is authoritative. The text establishes custom as a recognized legal source, quoting classical Hanafi authorities, and then demonstrates its application through concrete examples from oaths, agency, and family law. By working through these examples, you will understand how practical custom (urf al-af'āl al-ādiyyah) can restrict general expressions and determine obligations within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its supporting quotations
Locate the maxim 'al-ādah muḥakkamah' in the passage and note the two quotations from al-Sarakhsi and al-Biri that establish custom as equivalent to textual proof.
The maxim is one of the five major legal maxims of Islamic law.
2
Distinguish the two types of practical custom
The passage divides practical custom into 'custom of ordinary actions' (urf al-af'āl al-ādiyyah) and 'custom of transactions' (urf al-mu'āmalāt). Focus on the first type for this lesson.
The second type is mentioned but not elaborated here.
3
Analyze the first fur': oath regarding meat
Work through the example: a person from a sheep-eating community swears not to eat meat. The ruling is that he does not break his oath by eating beef or fish, because custom restricts the word 'meat' to the customary type.
The legal cause (illah) is that custom restricts the absolute meaning of the word.
4
Analyze the second fur': agency
Examine the agency example: a principal authorizes an agent to purchase meat or clothing. The agent's authority is limited to the customary type; purchasing a non-customary type does not bind the principal.
This protects the principal's implicit reliance on custom.
5
Analyze the third fur': spousal maintenance
Review the maintenance example: the amount of nafaqah is determined by the couple's customary lifestyle and financial status, not a fixed amount.
This shows custom's role in determining obligations where the law is silent on specifics.

فالعرف عندئذ يعتبر مرجعاً ومنبعاً للأحكام، ودليلاً شرعياً عليها لا دليل سواه من النصوص التشريعية الأساسية .

وقال البيري في شرح الأشباه والنظائر لابن نجيم «نقلاً المشرع»: «الثابت بالعرف ثابت بدليل شرعي».

(1) محكمة بتشديد الكاف المفتوحة، اسم مفعول من التحكيم.
Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. Then custom is considered a reference and source for rulings, and a legal proof for them with no other proof besides it from the primary legislative texts. Imam al-Sarakhsi said in al-Mabsut: 'What is established by custom is like what is established by text.' And al-Biri said in his commentary on al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir of Ibn Nujaym, quoting al-Musharri': 'What is established by custom is established by a legal proof.' (From Ibn Abidin's Rasa'il, 2/115). And in this meaning came article 45 of the Majalla with the maxim: 'Specification by custom is like specification by text.' We have already stated that practical custom includes two types: custom of ordinary vital actions, and custom of civil transactions (see: F 3/68). We will present below a number of rulings that Islamic fiqh built on considering practical custom in its two types, by which it becomes clear what our jurists considered as authority for directing rulings and imposing obligations within legal limits. -A- Custom of Ordinary Actions: 7/69 - Ordinary actions - even if they are personal vital actions and not of the type of transactions and civil legal relations - when people become accustomed to them and their life habits run on them, they acquire authority and influence in directing the rulings of civil transactions connected to those actions towards a direction consistent with customary objectives. Among the examples of the authority of custom of ordinary actions and its influence on the rulings of dispositions and contracts in Islamic fiqh are the following issues: 1 - If it was the custom of a people to eat sheep meat, and one of them swore that he would not eat meat, his oath is directed to the meat customarily eaten among them, not others. So if he eats beef, camel meat, or fish, he does not break his oath and no expiation is due, even though it is also meat linguistically. Similarly, if one swears not to ride or not to ride an animal, he does not break his oath by riding any animal whatsoever, nor by riding on a human even though humans are a type of animal; rather he breaks his oath only if he rides what is customarily ridden among beasts (see: Radd al-Muhtar, Book of Oaths, 3/90-91). The implication is that if the swearer is a city dweller in modern times, he does not break his oath by riding a camel in the city, but he breaks it if he rides it while traveling, just as an Indian breaks his oath by riding an elephant while an Arab does not. The legal cause (illa) in this and similar cases is that practical custom restricts the absolute meaning of the word and directs the oath to the customary action, just like verbal custom, because it is established in the legal maxims that 'custom is authoritative' (Article 36 of the Majalla), i.e., it has in the view of the Shari'ah a governing authority to which rulings of dispositions are subject, so those rulings are established according to what custom and usage dictate (see: Radd al-Muhtar 3/86 and 90-91). 2 - If a person appoints an agent to purchase meat, bread, or clothing, for example, the agency contract is restricted to the type of meat or bread customarily eaten and the clothing customarily worn. So if the agent purchases another type that is not customary, the purchase does not bind the principal; rather the agent is liable for it (see: Ibn Abidin's Rasa'il 2/115). 3 - The wife's maintenance is obligatory upon her husband in the Hanafi ijtihad according to the customary amount for their likes and according to their financial status: if they are middle class, then maintenance of the middle class; if wealthy, maintenance of the wealthy; if poor, maintenance of the poor (see: al-Ahkam al-Shar'iyyah fi al-Ahwal al-Shakhsiyyah by Qadri Pasha al-Misri). (1) 'Muhakkamah' with emphasis on the open kaf, a passive participle from 'tahkim'.
Source pages p. 828

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm is an exposition on the theory of custom (al-urf) in Islamic jurisprudence, specifically within the Hanafi school. The author first establishes the principle that custom is a recognized source of law, quoting classical Hanafi authorities: al-Sarakhsi's statement in al-Mabsut that 'what is established by custom is like what is established by a text' (i.e., a textual proof), and al-Biri's comment that it is established by a legal proof. He then references the Ottoman Majalla's article 45, which enshrines the maxim 'Specification by custom is like specification by text.' The passage then focuses on the first category of practical custom: 'custom of ordinary actions' (urf al-af'al al-adiyyah). This refers to habitual, everyday behaviors that people consistently follow. Although these are not transactional in themselves, they have a powerful impact on legal rulings because they contextualize and specify the meanings of words used in oaths, contracts, and other dispositions. The author provides three clear Hanafi examples: 

1. Oaths: If a person swears not to eat meat, the oath is interpreted according to the type of meat customarily eaten in that community. Thus, an oath not to eat meat is not broken by eating fish or camel in a sheep-eating community. Similarly, a vow not to ride an animal is interpreted according to what is normally ridden. The key point is that custom overrides the literal linguistic meaning and directs the oath to the customary practice. This is because, as the maxim states, 'custom is authoritative' (al-adah muhakkamah).

2. Agency (wakalah): When a principal authorizes an agent to purchase a specific commodity (e.g., meat or clothing), the authority is limited to the customary type. If the agent buys a different type, the principal is not bound; the agent bears responsibility. This protects the principal's implicit reliance on custom.

3. Spousal maintenance (nafaqah): In the Hanafi school, the amount of maintenance a husband must provide to his wife is determined by the couple's customary standard of living and their financial status (middle, rich, or poor). The shari requirement is not a fixed amount but one that follows custom.

The underlying juristic principle is that custom restricts the absolute meaning of words and directs intentions, just as verbal custom does. The author references the Hanafi jurist Ibn Abidin's Radd al-Muhtar for these rulings. This passage is a classic demonstration of how the Hanafi school integrates custom as a supplemental source of law when texts are silent or general.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Custom; a recurring practice that is accepted by people of sound nature and is consistent with reason and shari'ah.
العادة محكمة (al-ādah muḥakkamah): The legal maxim meaning 'custom is authoritative'; it is one of the five major maxims of Islamic law.
عرف الأفعال العادية (urf al-af'āl al-ādiyyah): Custom of ordinary actions; habitual behaviors that people consistently follow in daily life.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rulings in the passage, if a person from a community where sheep meat is customary swears 'I will not eat meat,' and then eats beef, what is the ruling?
A) He breaks his oath and must pay expiation because beef is linguistically meat.
B) He does not break his oath because the oath is directed only to the customary meat (sheep).
C) He breaks his oath only if he intended to include all meat when swearing.
D) He does not break his oath but should still pay expiation to be safe.

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) is a binding source in Hanafi fiqh; it can specify general expressions and determine the scope of oaths, agency, and maintenance obligations.

The Maxim 'Designation by Custom is Like Designation by Text' – Applications in Liability
Explains the maxim with three fiqh examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'التعيين بالعرف كالتعيين بالنص' to determine liability in cases of fire, borrowing, and deposit using Hanafi fiqh rulings.

Core Idea
Custom defines the limits of permissible use; exceeding custom triggers liability.

This lesson unpacks the first major maxim from the passage: 'Designation by custom is like designation by text.' We will work through three concrete fiqh examples—lighting a fire, borrowing an item, and depositing goods—to see how custom determines whether an action is transgressory and thus liable. These examples demonstrate the practical authority of custom in defining obligations and rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its meaning
State the maxim 'التعيين بالعرف كالتعيين بالنص' and understand that it means custom has the same legal effect as an explicit textual stipulation in defining what is permissible.
The maxim is classified as a wusta (intermediate) qawida in the Hanafi school.
2
Analyze the fire example
Consider the scenario: landowner lights a fire; sparks damage neighbors. Determine when he is liable based on custom. If he lit in a customary manner, no liability; if he exceeded custom (e.g., lit during wind), he is liable.
This aligns with the modern concept of abuse of right.
3
Analyze the borrowing example
A borrower must use the borrowed item in a customary manner. If he exceeds that (e.g., uses a tool in an unusual way), he becomes a transgressor and is liable for damage even though borrowing is normally a trust.
Custom transforms the amanah into a daman when exceeded.
4
Analyze the deposit example
A depositee may delegate custody to household members if that is customary. If he entrusts to an unsuitable person (e.g., a valuable necklace to a servant), he is negligent and liable. Custom determines the standard of care.
The condition is that the delegate must be someone who would customarily keep such an item.
5
Note the overarching condition
All these applications are valid only when custom does not contradict a clear text of Quran or Sunnah. Custom is a supplementary source, not an override.
Refer to the istithna' in the qawida panel.

Source Text

الفصل (٦٩) سلطان العرف
في منفعة أن يتجاوز في استيفاء حقه إلى حد يضر بغيره فلو فعل كان ضامناً للضرر.
ومقياس التجاوز وعدمه إنما هو العرف والعادة.
فلو أوقد صاحب الأرض أو مستأجرها ناراً في الأرض فتطاير منها ما أحرق شيئاً لجيرانه من بيادر أو غيرها؛ فإن كان أوقدها بصورة معتادة عرفاً فهو غير ضامن وإن تجاوز المعتاد أو أوقدها في هبوب الريح كان ضامناً (ر) الدرر شرح الغرر آخر الإجارة (٢٤٠/٢) (١)
٧ - وكذلك ليس للمستعير أن يجاوز المعتاد عرفاً في كيفية استعمال العارية. فإن فعل كان ضامناً إن هلكت أو تضررت، رغم أن العارية في الأصل تعتبر أمانة في يده غير مضمونة. وذلك لأنه بتجاوزه المعتاد في استعمالها يصبح في العرف متعدياً.
۸ - إن مقتضى عقد الإيداع أن يحفظ الوديع الوديعة بنفسه في حرز مثلها عرفاً، وأن لا يودعها عند غيره لأن مبنى الإيداع على الثقة الشخصية بالوديع نفسه .
على أن الفقهاء سوّغوا للوديع على سبيل الاستثناء أن يحفظ الوديعة بواسطة من في عياله من زوجة أو ولد كبير أو أجير خاص غير مياوم، لأن الوديع الاستعانة لا يستغني عن ، ولا يعتبر بذلك مقصراً في الحفظ عرفاً، فلا يضمن بهم، الوديعة إذا تلفت أو سرقت عندهم، لكن بشرط أن لا يحفظها إلا عند من يحفظ مثلها عند مثله عادة وعرفاً، فيحفظ مثلاً فرس الوديعة عند سائسه أو خادمه، ويحفظ عقد الجوهر عند زوجته . فلو حفظ العقد النفيس عند الخادم كان مفرطاً مقصراً عرفاً فيضمنه إن ضاع أو تلف (ر ): المجلة / ۷۸۰ - ۷۸۲ وشروحها).
(1) وهذا يتلاقى ويتفق مع ما استقر عليه التفكير القانوني اليوم في النظرية الحقوقية الحديثة المسماة: نظرية التعسف في استعمال الحق : abus de droit فالشريعة الإسلامية قد سبقت إلى تقرير موضوع هذه النظرية في شتى فروع الأحكام التي يعرفها المطلعون .

الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف
٨/٦٩ - هذه أمثلة يسيرة في شتى الأحكام الكثيرة التي تزخر بها الفصول الفقهية من كل باب، يتجلى بها مالعرف الأفعال العادية في الأكل والشرب واللبس والركب والحفظ والانتفاع إلخ ... كمية وكيفية من سلطان يقيد العقود، ويحدّد حدود الالتزامات، وينشىء الضمانات في العلائق المدنية
ب - عرف المعاملات المدنية :
٦٩ / ٩ - عندما يكون عرف الناس منصباً على توزيع الحقوق والالتزامات بين طرفين في بعض المعاملات المدنية دون أن يصادم تصاً تشريعياً آمراً يكون من الطبيعي، بل من البديهي احترام سلطان هذا العرف في إثبات الحقوق وانتفائها، لأنه عندئد يكون في قوة العبارة المنشئة للحق، وصراحة الشروط الجائزة، إذ يعتبر أن المتعاقدين يتركان التصريح بما جرى عليه التعارف اعتماداً منهما على العرف.
بل قد جاءت النصوص التشريعية من السنة النبوية ذاتها في بعض فروع الأحكام معللة بالعرف كما سنرى في الفرع الثالث الآتي :
وعلى هذا وضعت القاعدة السالفة البيان: التعيين بالعرف كالتعيين بالنص والقاعدة الأخرى القائلة : المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً كما قرّر الفقهاء كثيراً من الأحكام المبنية على العرف والتعامل، وأحالوا أيضاً على عرف المعاملات في كثير من المواطن التفصيلية التي يخضع تفصيل جزئيات الحقوق فيها لحكم هذا العرف.
٦٩ / ١٠ - فمن أمثلة ذلك المسائل الآتية :
۱ - قرّر الفقهاء أنه يجوز للصديق وهو في بيت صديقه أن يأكل مما يجد أمامه، وأن يستعمل بعض الأدوات للشرب ونحوه بلا إذن صاحب البيت، لأنّ ذلك مباح له عرفاً. فلو انكسرت الآنية أثناء استعماله المعتاد، أو تلفت بآفة سماوية لا يكون ضامناً لها شرعاً كما يضمن الغاصب، لأنه لم يعتبر متعدياً.
Chapter (69): The Authority of Custom. ... In the sense that one should not exceed in exercising one's right to the extent of harming others. If he does so, he is liable for the harm. And the measure of exceeding or not is only custom and habit. If the owner of land or its lessee lights a fire on the land and sparks from it burn something of the neighbors' such as grain stacks or other things: if he lit it in a manner customary by custom, he is not liable; but if he exceeded the customary or lit it when the wind was blowing, he is liable (reference: al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar, end of Ijarah, 2/240). 7 - Similarly, the borrower may not exceed the customary manner of using the borrowed item. If he does so, he is liable if it is destroyed or damaged, even though borrowing is originally a trust (amanah) in his hand, not guaranteed. This is because by exceeding the customary usage he becomes a transgressor by custom. 8 - The requirement of the contract of deposit is that the depositee keep the deposit himself in a place befitting its like by custom, and not deposit it with someone else, because the basis of deposit is personal trust in the depositee himself. However, the jurists permitted the depositee, by way of exception, to keep the deposit through those in his household such as his wife, an adult son, or a specific non-daily employee, because the depositee cannot dispense with assistance, and he is not considered negligent in keeping by custom, so he is not liable for the deposit if it is lost or stolen with them, but on condition that he only entrusts it to someone who would normally keep such an item by custom. For example, he keeps a horse deposit with his groom or servant, and keeps a jewelry necklace with his wife. If he keeps the valuable necklace with a servant, he is negligent by custom and is liable if it is lost or damaged (reference: Majallah articles 780-782 and their commentaries). (1) This aligns with the modern legal theory known as 'abuse of right' (abus de droit); Islamic law preceded in establishing this theory in various branches of law known to scholars. Section 9: Theory of Custom. 8/69 - These are a few examples among many rulings in fiqh chapters, showing the authority of custom over ordinary actions in eating, drinking, dressing, riding, keeping, using, etc., in quantity and quality; binding contracts, defining limits of obligations, and establishing liabilities in civil relations. B - Custom in Civil Transactions: 69/9 - When people's custom concerns the distribution of rights and obligations between two parties in some civil transactions without conflicting with a mandatory legislative text, it is natural—indeed obvious—to respect the authority of this custom in establishing or negating rights, because it then has the force of the expression that creates the right and the explicitness of permissible conditions. It is considered that the contracting parties leave unsaid what is customary, relying on custom. Moreover, legislative texts from the Prophetic Sunnah itself in some branches of rulings are reasoned by custom, as we will see in the third branch below. Upon this, the previously mentioned maxim was formulated: 'Designation by custom is like designation by text', and the other maxim: 'What is known by custom is like what is stipulated by condition.' Just as jurists established many rulings based on custom and practice, and also referred to the custom of transactions in many detailed instances where the details of rights are subject to this custom. 69/10 - Examples of that include the following: 1 - The jurists ruled that a friend, while in a friend's house, may eat what he finds before him and use some utensils for drinking and the like without the owner's permission, because that is permissible for him by custom. So if the utensil breaks during his customary use, or is destroyed by a heavenly affliction, he is not liable by Shari'ah as a usurper would be, because he is not considered a transgressor.
Source pages p. 831

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the authority of custom (sultan al-urf) in Islamic law, specifically in the Hanafi tradition. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by stating the general principle that one must not exceed the limits of one's rights so as to harm others, and the measure of excess is determined by custom. He then provides several practical applications from different chapters of fiqh to illustrate how custom determines liability and permissibility.

First, the example of lighting a fire on one's land: if the fire is lit in a customary manner and causes damage to neighbors, the owner is not liable because he acted within the bounds of custom. But if he exceeded custom (e.g., lit a fire during windy conditions), he becomes liable. This aligns with the modern legal concept of abuse of right.

Second, the borrower of an item must use it in a customary manner; exceeding that makes him liable for loss or damage, even though borrowing is normally a trust (amanah) not subject to liability. Custom defines the boundaries of permissible use.

Third, the depositee (mudi') is generally required to keep the deposit personally, but custom allows delegation to household members (wife, adult son, specific employee) because such assistance is customary and not considered negligence. However, if he entrusts the deposit to someone inappropriate by custom (e.g., a valuable necklace to a servant), he becomes liable for loss.

The author then transitions to the second part of the chapter (theory of custom), emphasizing that custom governs even ordinary actions like eating, drinking, clothing, riding, and storing. In civil transactions, custom has the force of explicit stipulation: if people customarily understand a contract to include certain terms without stating them, those terms are binding. The maxims "التعيين بالعرف كالتعيين بالنص" (Designation by custom is like designation by text) and "المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً" (What is known by custom is like what is stipulated by condition) are cited as foundational.

Finally, the author gives one more example: a guest in a friend's house may eat and use utensils without explicit permission because custom allows it. If a utensil breaks during normal use, the guest is not liable, since he was not transgressing by custom. This again shows custom as a determinant of liability.

Throughout, the author stresses that custom is considered only when it does not conflict with a decisive legal text. These examples are all Hanafi rulings based on the principle that custom ('urf) is a legitimate source of law in areas not covered by explicit revelation.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Custom or habitual practice that is widespread among people and determines the boundaries of rights and obligations.
سلطان العرف (sultan al-urf): The binding force of custom as a legal determinant that can create rights, obligations, and liabilities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, a depositee who keeps a valuable necklace with his wife is not liable if it is lost because:
A) The wife is a trusted family member and customarily keeps such items.
B) The depositee is never liable for deposits.
C) The necklace was not explicitly mentioned in the deposit contract.
D) The wife is considered the owner by custom.

Key Takeaway
Custom defines the limits of permissible use; acting within custom negates liability, while exceeding it triggers liability.

Custom as a Source of Obligations in Civil Transactions – The Authority of Custom (Sultan al-Urf)
Explains custom as equivalent to explicit stipulation in civil transactions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how custom in civil transactions creates binding obligations equivalent to explicit stipulations, using the guest example and the maxim 'المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً'.

Core Idea
What is known by custom has the same legal force as an explicit condition in contracts.

This lesson explores the second part of the passage, where the author expands on the authority of custom beyond specific examples to a general principle: in civil transactions, custom is treated as if the parties had explicitly agreed to it. We focus on the guest example and the two maxims that encapsulate this idea.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second maxim
State the maxim 'المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً' (What is known by custom is like what is stipulated by condition) and understand its meaning.
This maxim is parallel to the first and also originates from the Hanafi school.
2
Analyze the guest example
Consider the scenario: a friend in a friend's house eats food and uses utensils without explicit permission. According to Hanafi fiqh, this is permissible by custom, and if the utensil breaks during normal use, the guest is not liable.
The guest is considered non-transgressor because custom allows such use.
3
Generalize to other transactions
Understand that in any civil transaction, if there is a widespread custom regarding rights and obligations, it is binding on the parties as if they had stipulated it. The author says this is natural and obvious.
Examples: determining reasonable care, scope of permission, etc.
4
Connect to the Sunnah
Note that the author mentions that even Prophetic Sunnah sometimes bases rulings on custom. Custom is not merely a secondary source but can be the rationale behind revealed texts.
This supports the strength of custom in Hanafi usul.
5
Apply the condition of no conflict with nass
As with the first maxim, this maxim applies only when the custom does not contradict a mandatory text. The author emphasizes that custom is respected when it does not conflict with a decisive legislative text.
This ensures primacy of revelation.
