Chapter (69): The Authority of Custom. ... In the sense that one should not exceed in exercising one's right to the extent of harming others. If he does so, he is liable for the harm. And the measure of exceeding or not is only custom and habit. If the owner of land or its lessee lights a fire on the land and sparks from it burn something of the neighbors' such as grain stacks or other things: if he lit it in a manner customary by custom, he is not liable; but if he exceeded the customary or lit it when the wind was blowing, he is liable (reference: al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar, end of Ijarah, 2/240). 7 - Similarly, the borrower may not exceed the customary manner of using the borrowed item. If he does so, he is liable if it is destroyed or damaged, even though borrowing is originally a trust (amanah) in his hand, not guaranteed. This is because by exceeding the customary usage he becomes a transgressor by custom. 8 - The requirement of the contract of deposit is that the depositee keep the deposit himself in a place befitting its like by custom, and not deposit it with someone else, because the basis of deposit is personal trust in the depositee himself. However, the jurists permitted the depositee, by way of exception, to keep the deposit through those in his household such as his wife, an adult son, or a specific non-daily employee, because the depositee cannot dispense with assistance, and he is not considered negligent in keeping by custom, so he is not liable for the deposit if it is lost or stolen with them, but on condition that he only entrusts it to someone who would normally keep such an item by custom. For example, he keeps a horse deposit with his groom or servant, and keeps a jewelry necklace with his wife. If he keeps the valuable necklace with a servant, he is negligent by custom and is liable if it is lost or damaged (reference: Majallah articles 780-782 and their commentaries). (1) This aligns with the modern legal theory known as 'abuse of right' (abus de droit); Islamic law preceded in establishing this theory in various branches of law known to scholars. Section 9: Theory of Custom. 8/69 - These are a few examples among many rulings in fiqh chapters, showing the authority of custom over ordinary actions in eating, drinking, dressing, riding, keeping, using, etc., in quantity and quality; binding contracts, defining limits of obligations, and establishing liabilities in civil relations. B - Custom in Civil Transactions: 69/9 - When people's custom concerns the distribution of rights and obligations between two parties in some civil transactions without conflicting with a mandatory legislative text, it is natural—indeed obvious—to respect the authority of this custom in establishing or negating rights, because it then has the force of the expression that creates the right and the explicitness of permissible conditions. It is considered that the contracting parties leave unsaid what is customary, relying on custom. Moreover, legislative texts from the Prophetic Sunnah itself in some branches of rulings are reasoned by custom, as we will see in the third branch below. Upon this, the previously mentioned maxim was formulated: 'Designation by custom is like designation by text', and the other maxim: 'What is known by custom is like what is stipulated by condition.' Just as jurists established many rulings based on custom and practice, and also referred to the custom of transactions in many detailed instances where the details of rights are subject to this custom. 69/10 - Examples of that include the following: 1 - The jurists ruled that a friend, while in a friend's house, may eat what he finds before him and use some utensils for drinking and the like without the owner's permission, because that is permissible for him by custom. So if the utensil breaks during his customary use, or is destroyed by a heavenly affliction, he is not liable by Shari'ah as a usurper would be, because he is not considered a transgressor.
Source pages p. 831

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the authority of custom (sultan al-urf) in Islamic law, specifically in the Hanafi tradition. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by stating the general principle that one must not exceed the limits of one's rights so as to harm others, and the measure of excess is determined by custom. He then provides several practical applications from different chapters of fiqh to illustrate how custom determines liability and permissibility.

First, the example of lighting a fire on one's land: if the fire is lit in a customary manner and causes damage to neighbors, the owner is not liable because he acted within the bounds of custom. But if he exceeded custom (e.g., lit a fire during windy conditions), he becomes liable. This aligns with the modern legal concept of abuse of right.

Second, the borrower of an item must use it in a customary manner; exceeding that makes him liable for loss or damage, even though borrowing is normally a trust (amanah) not subject to liability. Custom defines the boundaries of permissible use.

Third, the depositee (mudi') is generally required to keep the deposit personally, but custom allows delegation to household members (wife, adult son, specific employee) because such assistance is customary and not considered negligence. However, if he entrusts the deposit to someone inappropriate by custom (e.g., a valuable necklace to a servant), he becomes liable for loss.

The author then transitions to the second part of the chapter (theory of custom), emphasizing that custom governs even ordinary actions like eating, drinking, clothing, riding, and storing. In civil transactions, custom has the force of explicit stipulation: if people customarily understand a contract to include certain terms without stating them, those terms are binding. The maxims "التعيين بالعرف كالتعيين بالنص" (Designation by custom is like designation by text) and "المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً" (What is known by custom is like what is stipulated by condition) are cited as foundational.

Finally, the author gives one more example: a guest in a friend's house may eat and use utensils without explicit permission because custom allows it. If a utensil breaks during normal use, the guest is not liable, since he was not transgressing by custom. This again shows custom as a determinant of liability.

Throughout, the author stresses that custom is considered only when it does not conflict with a decisive legal text. These examples are all Hanafi rulings based on the principle that custom ('urf) is a legitimate source of law in areas not covered by explicit revelation.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Custom or habitual practice that is widespread among people and determines the boundaries of rights and obligations.
سلطان العرف (sultan al-urf): The binding force of custom as a legal determinant that can create rights, obligations, and liabilities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, a guest who breaks a cup while using it in the host's home is not liable because:
A) The host is responsible for damage caused by guests.
B) The guest acted within customary use and is not a transgressor.
C) The cup was already broken.
D) The guest had asked permission.

Key Takeaway
Custom in civil transactions has the force of an explicit condition, granting rights and imposing obligations as if the parties had agreed to it.

Authority of Custom in Civil Transactions: Two Foundational Maxims
Explaining the authority of custom with maxims and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi maxims regarding custom and apply them to at least two of the given furuat cases.

Core Idea
Custom specifies like text; what is customary is like a stipulated condition.

In Hanafi mu'amalat, custom plays a crucial role in determining rights and obligations when parties are silent. This lesson explores two central maxims — 'Specification by custom is like specification by text' and 'What is well-known by custom is like a stipulated condition' — and illustrates them with four practical cases from classical fiqh. Understanding these principles equips a mufti to resolve contractual disputes and everyday transactions in accordance with established practice, as long as custom does not contradict an imperative text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two maxims and their meaning
From the passage, extract the Arabic texts of the two maxims and write their English equivalents. Understand that the first maxim gives custom the force of a textual specification, while the second treats customary practice as an implied contractual term.
The maxims are: 'التعيين بالعرف كالتعيين بالنص' and 'المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً'.
2
Note the scope and limitation of custom
Custom is authoritative only when it does not conflict with an imperative legislative text (نص تشريعي آمر). This is a key principle of Hanafi usul: custom operates in the absence of contrary textual evidence.
The passage explicitly states 'دون أن يصادم نصاً تشريعياً آمراً'.
3
Examine the first two furuat: friend's house and fallen fruit
Analyze how custom permits the use of property without explicit permission and negates liability. Compare the rulings with those on usurpation (ghasb) to see the effect of custom on legal responsibility.
Note that in both cases, the permissibility is based on 'urf, not on explicit consent.
4
Examine the third fur'ah: virgin's silence in marriage
Explain why silence is considered consent by custom. The hadith and its justification (shyness of virgins) are cited. This shows custom interpreting an act (silence) as a legal expression of will.
This is an example of custom giving legal meaning to a non-verbal act.
5
Examine the fourth fur'ah: mu'atah contracts
Understand that mutual exchange without words is valid because custom treats delivery and payment as equivalent to offer and acceptance. This is a major principle in Hanafi sales law.
Mu'atah (مُعاطاة) is widely practiced in everyday transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف
ب - عرف المعاملات المدنية :
عندما يكون عرف الناس منصباً على توزيع الحقوق والالتزامات بين طرفين في بعض المعاملات المدنية دون أن يصادم نصاً تشريعياً آمراً يكون من الطبيعي، بل من البديهي احترام سلطان هذا العرف في إثبات الحقوق وانتفائها، لأنه عندئذ يكون في قوة العبارة المنشئة للحق، وصراحة الشروط الجائزة، إذ يعتبر أن المتعاقدين يتركان التصريح بما جرى عليه التعارف اعتماداً منهما على العرف.
بل قد جاءت النصوص التشريعية من السنة النبوية ذاتها في بعض فروع الأحكام معللة بالعرف كما سنرى في الفرع الثالث الآتي :
وعلى هذا وضعت القاعدة السالفة البيان: التعيين بالعرف كالتعيين بالنص والقاعدة الأخرى القائلة : المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً كما قرّر الفقهاء كثيراً من الأحكام المبنية على العرف والتعامل، وأحالوا أيضاً على عرف المعاملات في كثير من المواطن التفصيلية التي يخضع تفصيل جزئيات الحقوق فيها لحكم هذا العرف.
فمن أمثلة ذلك المسائل الآتية :
۱ - قرّر الفقهاء أنه يجوز للصديق وهو في بيت صديقه أن يأكل مما يجد أمامه، وأن يستعمل بعض الأدوات للشرب ونحوه بلا إذن صاحب البيت، لأنّ ذلك مباح له عرفاً. فلو انكسرت الآنية أثناء استعماله المعتاد، أو تلفت بآفة سماوية لا يكون ضامناً لها شرعاً كما يضمن الغاصب، لأنه لم يعتبر متعدياً.
وكذلك قالوا: يجوز للإنسان أن يتناول من الثمار الساقطة المتروكة تحت شجرها في أراضي البساتين بلا إذن صاحبها إذا كانت من الأنواع التي يتسارع إليها الفساد لو بقيت لأن العرف على إباحة ذلك. فلا يكون ضامناً لما أكل، إلا أن ينهاه صاحبها عن التناول منها أو يكون صاحبها قائماً بتتبعها ،وجمعها فلا بد عندئذ من الإذن.
فهنا قد ورد العرف على إباحة المال واعتبر نافياً للضمان.
۲ - ثبت في السنة بشأن الفتاة البكر البالغة إذا استأذنها وليها في عقد نكاحها أنَّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : إذنها صُماتها أي إن سكوتها عن الجواب دون رفض أو تفويض : يعتبر منها إذناً وتوكيلاً فيسري عليها التزويج المبني عليه .
وقد عُلل ذلك بدلالة العرف، إذ يغلب على الفتيات الأبكار في هذا المقام الخجل من إبداء الرغبة عادة بينما لا يخجلن من إظهار الرفض كما تقدم في بحث القياس (ر: ف ۷/۳ سابعاً).
وموضوع العرف هنا ليس عملاً حيوياً عادياً بل معاملة مدنية هي الإذن بالتزويج. فقد حقق النص التشريعي عقد الوكالة بالسكوت بناء على هذا العرف المدني. - ونظير ذلك عقود التعاطي التي أقرّ الفقهاء فيها انعقاد المعاوضات المالية بالقبض والدفع دون إيجاب وقبول باللفظ؛ كما لو سأل راغب الشراء صاحب البضاعة عن سعرها فبينه له، أو وجد سعرها مكتوباً عليها، فدفعه له وأخذها ومضى وكلاهما ساكت؛ فإن البيع ينعقد بينهما شرعاً. ومستند الفقهاء في ذلك هو العرف العام الذي يعتبر القبض بعد معرفة الثمن في قوة التعبير عن الإرادة بالإيجاب والقبول في عادة الناس.
Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom
B - Custom of Civil Transactions:
When people's custom is directed toward distributing rights and obligations between two parties in some civil transactions without conflicting with an imperative legislative text, it is natural, even obvious, to respect the authority of this custom in establishing or negating rights, because then it is as strong as the declaratory expression that creates the right and as explicit as permissible conditions, since it is considered that the contracting parties leave out explicit mention of what has become customary, relying on custom.
Indeed, legislative texts from the Prophetic Sunnah itself have come in some branches of rulings, justified by custom, as we will see in the third following section.
Upon this was laid down the previously stated maxim: 'Specification by custom is like specification by text,' and the other maxim: 'What is well-known by custom is like what is stipulated as a condition.' As the jurists established many rulings based on custom and transactions, and they also referred to the custom of transactions in many detailed places where the detail of partial rights is subject to the ruling of this custom.
Examples include the following issues:
1- The jurists decided that it is permissible for a friend in his friend's house to eat what he finds before him and to use some utensils for drinking and the like without the permission of the house owner, because that is permissible for him by custom. If the utensil breaks during its normal use, or is destroyed by a celestial calamity, he is not liable for it legally as a usurper is liable, because he is not considered a transgressor.
Similarly, they said: It is permissible for a person to take fallen fruits left under their trees in orchard lands without the owner's permission, if they are of the types that quickly decay if left, because custom permits that. So he is not liable for what he ate, unless the owner forbids him from taking them or the owner is following and collecting them; then permission is necessary.
Here, custom has permitted consumption of property and is considered negating liability.
2- It is established in the Sunnah regarding a mature virgin girl: when her guardian asks her permission for her marriage contract, the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Her consent is her silence.' That is, her silence without refusal or delegation is considered her permission and authorization, so the marriage based on it is effective.
This was justified by the indication of custom, because virgins are usually shy in this situation about expressing desire, while they are not shy about showing refusal, as previously discussed in the section on analogy (see section 7/3, seventh).
The subject of custom here is not a habitual physical act but a civil transaction, namely permission for marriage. The legislative text established the contract of agency by silence based on this civil custom. Analogous to this are contracts of mu'atah (mutual giving) which the jurists recognized as valid financial exchanges by taking and giving without verbal offer and acceptance; for example, if a prospective buyer asks the seller about the price and he tells him, or he finds the price written on it, so he gives the price and takes it and leaves, both silent; the sale is legally concluded between them. The basis of the jurists on this is the general custom that considers taking after knowing the price as strong as the expression of will by offer and acceptance in people's custom.
Source pages p. 832

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm discusses the authority of custom ('urf) in civil transactions. The author begins by establishing the general principle: when custom operates in distributing rights and obligations between parties without contradicting an imperative textual ruling, it carries the same weight as explicit contractual language. This is because parties implicitly rely on customary practices rather than spelling out every detail. The passage then introduces two foundational maxims: (1) 'Specification by custom is like specification by text' (al-ta'yin bi al-'urf ka al-ta'yin bi al-nass), meaning that what is determined by custom has the same legal effect as if it were explicitly stated in a text; and (2) 'What is well-known by custom is like what is stipulated as a condition' (al-ma'ruf 'urfan ka al-mashrut sharṭan), meaning that customary practice functions as an implied term in contracts. These maxims are among the most important in the Hanafi school for resolving contractual disputes and determining rights.

The author then provides four illustrative examples from Hanafi fiqh. The first example concerns a guest in a friend's home: custom permits him to eat available food and use utensils without explicit permission. If he accidentally breaks a utensil during normal use or it is destroyed by an act of God, he is not liable, because he is not a transgressor (ghāṣib). This is because custom deems such use permissible, and liability is negated. The second example involves fallen fruit in orchards: if it is of a type that spoils quickly and the owner is not actively collecting it, custom allows taking it without permission and without liability. However, if the owner forbids it or is gathering it, permission is required. The third example relates to the well-known hadith that a virgin's silence indicates consent to marriage. The author notes that this ruling is based on custom: virgins are typically shy to express consent but not to refuse. Thus, silence is interpreted as permission, and the marriage contract is valid. This is a clear instance where custom gives meaning to a legal act. The fourth and final example concerns contracts of mu'āṭāh (barter or sale without verbal offer and acceptance). In commercial practice, transactions often occur by handing over goods and payment without uttering formal words. Hanafi jurists recognize such contracts as valid based on general custom (al-'urf al-'āmm), which treats the exchange as equivalent to explicit offer and acceptance.

The passage emphasizes that custom is a legitimate source of law in the Hanafi school, provided it does not contravene a definitive text. It functions as an implied expression of will and as a means of interpreting contracts and determining liability. The commentary from a Hanafi perspective should note that these rulings are built on the principle that custom is authoritative in the absence of contrary evidence, and they reflect the school's pragmatism in accommodating people's practices.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Customary practice among people in transactions and daily life that does not contradict a definitive text.
التعيين بالعرف (al-ta'yin bi al-'urf): The determination of rights, obligations, or specifications by customary practice rather than explicit stipulation.
المعروف عرفاً (al-ma'ruf 'urfan): What is widely recognized and practiced by people in a particular context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi examples in the passage, if a friend uses a utensil in his friend's house without permission and it accidentally breaks during normal use, is he liable?
A) Yes, because he did not have permission.
B) Yes, because he is a usurper (ghasib).
C) No, because custom permits such use and he is not a transgressor.
D) No, but only if the owner explicitly allowed it beforehand.

Key Takeaway
Custom is a weighty source in Hanafi transactions, equal to explicit text or condition, but it yields to an imperative legislative text.

Custom as a Permissive Factor: The Case of Fallen Fruits
Presents a furu' (branched case) applying the qawida that custom is authoritative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida 'al-urf muhakkam' to identify when custom permits taking property without liability, and to identify the exceptions that override it.

Core Idea
Custom permits taking fallen fruits if they spoil quickly and owner does not object, but not if owner prohibits or collects.

Custom ('urf) is a recognised source of law in the Hanafi school, especially in transactions (muamalat). This passage presents a concrete application: fallen fruits in orchards. The ruling shows that custom can imply the owner's consent and negate liability, but it is not absolute. We will dissect the conditions and exceptions, linking them to the general maxim that custom is authoritative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the custom
The case is fallen fruits left under trees in orchards. The custom is that people take these fruits without asking, because they spoil quickly. Identify that the custom relates to the type of fruit (quick-spoiling).
The passage specifies 'min al-anwa' allati yatasara'u ilayha al-fasad'.
2
Determine the default ruling without custom
Without custom, taking another's property without permission is prohibited (haram) and incurs liability (daman). Note that custom overrides this default.
The default is based on the prohibition of consuming others' wealth unjustly.
3
Apply the qawida: custom as permissive
The custom here indicates the owner's implicit consent. Therefore, the act becomes permissible (ja'iz) and liability is waived (daman negated). This is an application of the maxim 'al-urf muhakkam' (custom is authoritative).
The text says: 'al-urf 'ala ibahat dhalik' and 'i'tubira nafiyan li al-daman'.
4
Identify the exceptions
The custom is overridden if the owner explicitly prohibits the person, or if the owner is actively following and collecting the fruits. In these cases, permission is required and taking without it incurs liability.
The exceptions show that custom does not override the owner's explicit will.
5
Derive the mufta bihi ruling
The operative ruling (mufta bihi) is: taking is permissible without liability only when the owner is not present or collecting, and the fruit type customarily spoils quickly. Otherwise, liability applies.
This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك قالوا: يجوز للإنسان أن يتناول من الثمار الساقطة المتروكة تحت شجرها في أراضي البساتين بلا إذن صاحبها إذا كانت من الأنواع التي يتسارع إليها الفساد لو بقيت لأن العرف على إباحة ذلك. فلا يكون ضامناً لما أكل، إلا أن ينهاه صاحبها عن التناول منها أو يكون صاحبها قائماً بتتبعها، وجمعها فلا بد عندئذ من الإذن. فهنا قد ورد العرف على إباحة المال واعتبر نافياً للضمان.
And likewise they said: It is permissible for a person to take fallen fruits left under their trees in orchard lands without the owner's permission if they are of the types that quickly spoil if left, because the custom is that this is permissible. So he is not liable for what he ate, unless the owner prohibits him from taking them or the owner is actively collecting them; then permission is necessary. So here custom has permitted property and is considered a negation of liability.
Source pages p. 833

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling based on custom (urf). The issue is fallen fruits in orchards. The Hanafi ruling is that if the fruits are of a type that spoils quickly if left, custom allows taking them without permission from the owner. Consequently, the taker is not liable for compensation (damān) for what he consumes. However, the permission is conditional: if the owner explicitly forbids or is actively gathering the fruits, then taking without permission is not allowed and liability applies. This is a clear example of how custom can make permissible what would otherwise be prohibited (taking another's property) and can negate liability. The legal reasoning (ta'lil) is that custom indicates the owner's implicit consent. This ruling is recorded in the Hanafi school; it is an application of the general principle that custom is authoritative (al-‘ādah muhakkamah).

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Customary practice that is prevalent among people and not opposed to Shariah.
ضمان (daman): Liability for compensation, usually monetary, for property that is consumed or damaged without right.
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission required to take another's property; may be explicit or implied by custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does custom NOT permit taking fallen fruits?
A) When the fruits are not yet fully ripe
B) When the fruits are of a type that spoils quickly
C) When the owner prohibits taking or is actively collecting the fruits
D) When the fruits are in a public garden

Key Takeaway
Custom permits taking quickly spoiling fallen fruits without liability, but only if the owner does not prohibit or actively collect—illustrating that custom is authoritative but subordinate to the owner's explicit will.

Custom as a Source of Legal Consent: Silence and Conduct
Explains how custom validates silence as consent in marriage and conduct as offer/acceptance in sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that custom can interpret silence and conduct as valid expressions of consent in marriage and commercial transactions.

Core Idea
Custom gives legal weight to silence and actions, making them equivalent to verbal consent.

In Hanafi fiqh, custom ('urf) plays a crucial role in interpreting human behavior. This lesson explores two classic applications: the silence of a virgin in marriage and the conduct of parties in commercial exchanges. In both cases, custom transforms a non-verbal act into a binding legal expression. Understanding this principle is essential for modern muftis dealing with new forms of consent and contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal cases
The passage presents two distinct scenarios: (1) a virgin's silence when her guardian asks her permission for marriage, and (2) a buyer and seller concluding a sale by simply handing over goods and money without words. Note that both are rooted in custom.
The first case is based on a hadith; the second is a juristic extension.
2
Extract the common legal element
In both cases, the determining factor is custom ('urf). For the virgin, custom dictates that silence indicates consent because virgins are shy to express acceptance but not refusal. For the sale, custom treats the physical exchange as equivalent to offer and acceptance.
The passage explicitly states 'dilalat al-'urf' for the first and 'al-'urf al-'amm' for the second.
3
Understand the juristic reasoning (ta'lil)
The ruling is justified by the principle that custom can assign legal meaning to silence and conduct. The hadith itself is seen as confirming this custom, not creating a new rule. This is an example of how custom operates within the Hanafi school.
The argument type is ta'lil: custom is the effective cause ('illa).
4
Recognize the limits and conditions
Not all silence is consent; it must be supported by the prevailing custom of that context. Similarly, for contracts by conduct, the price must be known to the parties. The passage highlights that custom is not absolute but operates within boundaries.
The hadith implies that explicit refusal overrides silence. For sales, knowledge of price is essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲ - ثبت في السنة بشأن الفتاة البكر البالغة إذا استأذنها وليها في عقد نكاحها أنَّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : إذنها صُماتها» (١) أي إن سكوتها عن الجواب دون رفض أو تفويض : يعتبر منها إذناً وتوكيلاً فيسري عليها التزويج المبني عليه . وقد عُلل ذلك بدلالة العرف، إذ يغلب على الفتيات الأبكار في هذا المقام الخجل من إبداء الرغبة عادة بينما لا يخجلن من إظهار الرفض كما تقدم في بحث القياس (ر: ف ۷/۳ سابعاً). وموضوع العرف هنا ليس عملاً حيوياً عادياً بل معاملة مدنية هي الإذن بالتزويج. فقد حقق النص التشريعي عقد الوكالة بالسكوت بناء على هذا العرف المدني. - ونظير ذلك عقود التعاطي التي أقرّ الفقهاء فيها انعقاد المعاوضات المالية بالقبض والدفع دون إيجاب وقبول باللفظ؛ كما لو سأل راغب الشراء صاحب البضاعة عن سعرها فبينه له، أو وجد سعرها مكتوباً عليها، فدفعه له وأخذها ومضى وكلاهما ساكت؛ فإن البيع ينعقد بينهما شرعاً. ومستند الفقهاء في ذلك هو العرف العام الذي يعتبر القبض بعد معرفة الثمن في قوة التعبير عن الإرادة بالإيجاب والقبول في عادة الناس. (۱) حديث: البكر رضاها صمتها أخرجه البخاري (الفتح ١٩١/٩ - ط السلفية) وفي :رواية اسكاتها إذنها وفي لفظ له قال: إذنها صُماتها».
2. It is established in the Sunnah concerning the mature virgin girl, if her guardian seeks her permission for her marriage, that the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Her permission is her silence' meaning that her silence without refusal or delegation is considered from her as permission and agency, so the marriage based on it is valid. And that was explained by the indication of custom, since it is common for virgin girls in this situation to be shy about expressing desire while they are not shy about showing refusal, as previously discussed. And the subject of custom here is not a vital act but a civil transaction which is permission for marriage. So the legislative text validated the contract of agency by silence based on this civil custom. - And similar to that is contracts by conduct (uqud al-ta'ati), which the jurists have validated: the conclusion of financial exchanges by taking and giving without verbal offer and acceptance, such as if a buyer asks the seller about the price and he tells him, or finds the price written on it, then gives him the money and takes it and leaves, both silent; the sale is concluded between them legally. And the basis of the jurists in that is the general custom which considers delivery after knowledge of the price as equivalent to expression of intent by offer and acceptance in people's custom. (1) Hadith: 'The virgin's consent is her silence' reported by Bukhari (Fath 9/191) and in another narration 'her silence is her permission'.
Source pages p. 833

Commentary
This passage discusses the role of custom in interpreting silence as consent, specifically in two contexts. First, the marriage of a virgin: the hadith states that her silence is considered her permission. The Hanafi school explains this by custom: virgins are typically shy to express interest but not to refuse, so silence indicates consent. This is an application of custom to validate a legal act (agency in marriage). Second, contracts by conduct (uqud al-ta'ati) where the sale is concluded by handing over the goods and money without verbal offer and acceptance. The jurists rely on the general custom that such conduct indicates mutual consent. Both examples show how custom can substitute for explicit verbal expression. This is a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: custom is given weight in determining the meaning of actions and silence. Note that the hadith is from Sahih Bukhari, and the Hanafi school uses it as evidence for the ruling.

Key Terms
عقود التعاطي (uqud al-ta'ati): Contracts concluded by mutual conduct without verbal expression of offer and acceptance.
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission, which can be given by silence in certain contexts according to custom.
العرف (al-'urf): Custom; the prevalent practice in a society that can give legal meaning to actions and silence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the Hanafi ruling that a virgin's silence in marriage constitutes consent?
A) Explicit command in the Quran
B) The hadith and general custom that virgins are shy to express acceptance but not refusal
C) Consensus of all four schools
D) Analogy with sales contracts

Key Takeaway
Custom can transform silence and conduct into legally binding expressions of consent, as seen in the virgin's marriage consent and contracts by conduct.

Custom Governs Ancillary Obligations
Explaining the rule that custom governs ancillary obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi qawida that custom governs ancillary obligations in commutative contracts and apply it to at least three examples.

Core Idea
Custom determines who bears incidental costs in contracts when parties are silent.

In Hanafi fiqh, when two parties enter a contract but do not specify who pays for ancillary services like brokerage, weighing, or transport, the law does not leave it undetermined. This lesson explores the primary rule: custom (urf) is the first reference to allocate these obligations. The jurists prioritize custom over any default rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of ancillary obligations
Recognize that ancillary obligations are costs secondary to the main contract, such as broker fees, weighing costs, and transport.
They are not the primary subject of the contract but are necessary for its completion.
2
State the primary rule
The rule: custom governs ancillary obligations when parties are silent. Quote the qawida in Arabic and English.
3
Examine the furuat (applications)
Review the examples given: broker's fee, document writing, removal from warehouse, weighing, cutting fruits. Understand how custom applies to each.
These are illustrative; the rule applies to any ancillary cost.
4
Note the condition of silence
The rule applies only when the parties have not explicitly agreed on who bears the cost. If they have agreed, the agreement prevails.
5
Understand priority over default
Custom is considered first; the default rule applies only when no custom exists. This hierarchy is crucial for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف
٤ - إِنَّ الالتزامات التبعية الناشئة بعقد المعاوضة كأجرة السمسار وكتابة الصك، وأجرة إخراج البضاعة المبيعة من مستودعها، وأجرة كيلها أو وزنها لأجل تسليمها وكلفة وزن الثمن أو نقله إذا كان مما يحتاج إلى كلفة، وأجرة قطع الثمار المبيعة على شجرها إلخ ... كل ذلك إن سكت المتعاقدان فيه عن بيان ما يلتزم به كل منهما فقد اعتبر الفقهاء العرف أولاً في إلزام كل منهما بما تعورف أنه يلزمه .
فإن لم يكن عرف فقد نصبوا لها ضابطاً يقوم على أساس أن ما يتوقف عليه تسليم الثمن فعلى المشتري وما يتوقف عليه تسليم المبيع فعلى البائع مع بعض تفصيل معروف في محله (ر: المجلة / ۲۸۸
.(۲۹۲
فقد اعتبروا العرف في توزيع هذه الالتزامات على الطرفين مقدماً على الضابط الفقهي في توزيعها .
ومثل ذلك ما جرى العرف ببيعه محمولاً على حساب البائع إلى بيت المشتري، فإن حمولته عندئذ يلزم بها البائع عملاً بالعرف وإلا فالأصل أن تكون حمولة المبيع على المشتري.
Chapter Nine: The Theory of Custom
4. Indeed, the ancillary obligations arising from a commutative contract, such as the broker's fee, the writing of the document, the fee for removing the sold goods from their warehouse, the fee for measuring or weighing them for delivery, the cost of weighing the price or transporting it if it requires cost, and the fee for cutting the fruits sold on their trees, etc. All of this, if the two contracting parties remain silent about specifying what each is obliged to, the jurists have considered custom first in obligating each with what is customarily incumbent upon him. If there is no custom, they have established a default rule based on the principle that whatever is necessary for delivery of the price is upon the buyer, and whatever is necessary for delivery of the sold item is upon the seller, with some known detail in its place (see: Majallah 288-292). Thus, they have considered custom in distributing these obligations between the two parties as prior to the jurisprudential default rule. And similar is what is customarily sold with transport on the seller's account to the buyer's house; then its transport is incumbent upon the seller by operation of custom, otherwise the default is that transport of the sold item is upon the buyer.
Source pages p. 834

Commentary
This passage presents a rule from Hanafi fiqh regarding the role of custom in determining incidental obligations in commutative contracts. The passage type is a qawida with furuat (a general legal rule with applications). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that when contracting parties do not specify who bears ancillary costs like broker fees, weighing fees, or transport costs, the jurists first refer to custom to allocate these obligations. If no custom exists, a default rule applies: costs necessary for delivery of the price are on the buyer, and costs for delivery of the sold item are on the seller. The rule prioritizes custom over the default rule. An example is given: if custom is that transport to the buyer's house is on the seller, then it is enforced, otherwise the default rule applies.

Key Terms
عرف (urf): Custom or common practice of the people in transactions
التزامات تبعية (iltizamat taba'iyyah): Incidental obligations that are subsidiary to the main contractual duties
ضابط (dabit): A default legal rule applied when custom is absent

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sale of televisions, the contract does not mention who pays for delivery. Local custom is that the seller delivers free of charge. Who bears the delivery cost?
A) Buyer
B) Seller
C) Both equally
D) Not determinable

Key Takeaway
Custom, not a default rule, determines who bears incidental costs in contracts when parties have not agreed.

Default Rule When Custom Is Absent
Explaining the default rule for ancillary obligations when custom is absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi default rule for allocating ancillary obligations when no custom exists and apply it to scenarios.

Core Idea
When custom is absent, costs for delivering the price are on the buyer; for delivering the item, on the seller.

In the previous lesson, we learned that custom is primary. But what if there is no established custom? The Hanafi jurists have set a precise default rule: costs necessary for delivering the price are on the buyer; costs for delivering the sold item are on the seller. This lesson unpacks that default rule and its applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify condition: no known custom
The default rule applies only when no custom exists. First, confirm that there is no relevant custom.
2
State the default rule
The rule: 'Whatever is necessary for delivery of the price is upon the buyer; whatever is necessary for delivery of the sold item is upon the seller.' Quote in Arabic and English.
This is from the Majallah (288-292).
3
Apply to examples
Classify each cost: is it necessary for delivering the price (e.g., weighing money) or the item (e.g., weighing goods)?
The same cost may be classified differently depending on the context.
4
Note the priority of custom
The default rule is subsidiary; custom overrides it. The passage emphasizes that custom is prior to the default rule.
5
Recognize the source
The default rule is derived from the Majallah and is a standard part of Hanafi fatwa methodology.

Custom Determines Included Accessories in Sale
Explaining a legal maxim (qawida) on custom and sale accessories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida 'كل ما جرى عرف الناس على تبعيته للمبيع يدخل في البيع بلا ذكر' to determine which accessories are included in a sale without mention, and identify exceptions where custom does not treat an item as an accessory.

Core Idea
Custom, not physical attachment, determines what is included in a sale without mention.

When you buy a house, do you also get the key? In Hanafi law, the answer depends on custom. The jurists formulated a concise rule: anything that people customarily consider part of the sold item is included in the sale without needing to be mentioned. This lesson unpacks that rule and its applications through classical and modern examples, showing how a mufti must consult local custom when deciding such cases.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Read the Arabic qawida: 'كل ما جرى عرف الناس على تبعيته للمبيع يدخل في البيع بلا ذكر'. Understand that it applies to sales of movable and immovable property, and the key condition is 'urf (custom).
2
Identify the condition: custom (al-'urf)
The rule hinges on what people customarily consider as an accessory. If custom varies by time or place, the ruling changes. Note that this is consistent with the major Hanafi maxim 'العادة محكمة' (custom is authoritative).
3
Distinguish between included and excluded items
Work through the examples: camel's halter (included), house key (included), bicycle pump and tools (included). Contrast with donkey's lead rope and bicycle lamp (excluded due to lack of custom). Ask: what makes the difference? The presence or absence of custom.
4
Apply the rule to new scenarios via qiyas
Use analogy to decide new cases. For instance, if custom treats bicycle pump as included, what about a car's spare tire? The answer depends on prevailing custom in the local market. The mufti must ascertain this custom before issuing a fatwa.
5
Recognize exceptions (istithna'at)
The rule is not absolute. Exceptions occur when custom does not treat an item as an accessory. The source mentions donkey's lead rope and bicycle lamp as examples. Always check for contrary custom that might block inclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥ - في باب شمول المبيع قرر الفقهاء من جملة قواعده أن كل ما جری عرف الناس على تبعيته للمبيع يدخل في البيع بلا ذكر، ويستحقه المشتري بلا ثمن إضافي :
بخلاف
ففي بيع البعير يدخل خطامه الموجود عليه وفي بيع الفرس يدخل رسنها كذلك، لتعارف الناس على هذا في الدواب التي تحتاج إلى مقود . بيع الحمار والغنمة فإنه لا يدخل فيه مقودهما إلا إذا ذكر في العقد لعدم ،العرف ولكن تدخل قلادة الحمار (ر: المجلة ومرآتها وشرح الباز وعلي حيدر، م/٢٣٢).
وفي بيع الدار يدخل مفتاحها، وفي بيع الدراجة المسماة بالإفرنجية (بيـسـيـكـلـيـت) يدخل منفاخها والمفاتيح الأساسية لفك أجزائها، وحقيبة أدواتها التي تعلق عليها للعرف في ذلك، ولكن لا يدخل مصباحها إلا بالذكر لعدم العرف .
وفي بيع السيارة اليوم كذلك يحكم العرف فيما يتبعها بلا ذكر أو لا بعها (١).
5. On the topic of inclusiveness of the sold item, the jurists have established among their rules that everything that people customarily consider as an accessory to the sold item is included in the sale without mention, and the buyer is entitled to it without additional price. Exception: In the sale of a camel, its halter that is on it is included; in the sale of a horse, its lead rope is similarly included, due to people's custom regarding animals that need a lead. In the sale of a donkey and a sheep, their lead ropes are not included unless mentioned in the contract, due to lack of custom; but the donkey's necklace is included. (See: Majallah and its mirrors, and Sharh al-Baz and Ali Haydar, Article 232). In the sale of a house, its key is included. In the sale of a bicycle (called in French 'bicyclette'), its pump and the essential wrenches for disassembling its parts, and its tool bag that is attached to it, are included due to custom in that; but its lamp is not included unless mentioned, due to lack of custom. In the sale of a car today, similarly custom governs what is included without mention or not.
Source pages p. 834

Commentary
This passage is a qawida with furuat explaining the rule that custom determines which accessories of a sold item are included in the sale without explicit mention. The author lists examples: a camel's halter, a horse's lead rope, a house's key, and a bicycle's pump and tools are included because custom treats them as part of the item. Conversely, a donkey's lead rope and a bicycle's lamp are not included because custom does not attach them. This rule is applied to modern items like cars, where custom will decide. The footnote (i) will be covered in the next lesson.

Key Terms
شمول المبيع (shumul al-mabi): The inclusiveness of the sold item; what is considered part of the sale
تبعية (taba'iyyah): Being an accessory or appurtenance customarily attached to the main object

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the sale of a bicycle, which of the following is NOT included by custom without mention?
A) The pump
B) The essential wrenches
C) The lamp
D) The tool bag

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, custom determines which accessories are included in a sale without explicit mention.

Easement Rights and Custom in Property Sales
Footnote on easement rights and custom in property sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida that easement rights are not included in a sale unless mentioned or by custom, and identify when custom overrides the default rule.

Core Idea
Easement rights are not automatically included in a sale unless specified, but local custom can make them part of the property.

This lesson examines a specific Hanafi legal maxim governing easement rights in real property sales. The default rule is that rights like water, pathway, and drainage are not included unless explicitly mentioned. However, a fatwa by Ibn Abidin shows that local custom can alter this. We will analyze the qawida and its furuat, and see how a mufti applies it in practice.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
State the Hanafi legal maxim: 'Easement rights are not included in the sale of real property unless mentioned or by custom.'
This is the sughra (minor) maxim.
2
Identify its scope
Determine which fiqh chapters this maxim governs. It applies to sales of real property (houses, land, etc.).
Scope includes buyu' (sales) and property law.
3
Work through the furuat
Apply the maxim to two cases: (1) sale of a house in Syria with canal water rights, (2) sale of agricultural land with a private river water right. Determine each ruling.
The first is included by custom; the second is not.
4
Identify the istithna
Find the exception: Syrian custom regarding canal water rights overrides the default rule. The governing maxim is 'al-'adah muhakkamah' (custom is authoritative).
This istithna shows how custom can modify the default.
5
Note the related maxim
The maxim 'al-'adah muhakkamah' complements this qawida by providing the basis for custom-based inclusion.
The relationship is complementary; custom acts as a secondary source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱) وفي حقوق العقار الارتفاقية كالشرب والممر والمسيل يذكر الفقهاء أنها لا تدخل في بيع الدار ونحوها إلا إذا ذكرت في العقد أو ذكر أن الدار بيعت بحقوقها ومرافقها جملة، ونحو ذلك من الألفاظ العامة التي تتناولها (ر ): المجلة / ٢٣٥ وشروحها والدرر ١٤٩/٢).
ولكن العلامة ابن عابدين أفتى بدخول حق الشرب من الأقنية والأنهار في الديار الشامية، وإن لم يذكر في بيع الدار ما يشعر بدخوله، لأن عرف هذه البلاد على ذلك (ر) مجموعة رسائل ابن عابدين (١٣٦/٢ .
أقول: وينطبق أيضاً هذا في مدينة دمشق اليوم على ما يخص عقاراتها من مياه الفيجة التي تشترى لها وتمد إليها بالأنابيب، فإن العرف على دخولها بلا ذكر. لكن في الأراضي الزراعية ذات حقوق الشرب من الأنهار الخاصة لا يدخل الشرب في الأرض إلا بذكره أو ذكر الحقوق والمرافق لأنهم متعارفون على التصرف بشرب الأراضي بيعاً وشراءاً منفرداً عن الأرض.
(1) Regarding real property easement rights such as the right of water, pathway, and drainage, the jurists mention that they are not included in the sale of a house and the like unless they are mentioned in the contract or it is stated that the house is sold with its rights and appurtenances collectively, or similar general expressions that encompass them (see: Majallah 235 and its commentaries, and al-Durar 2/149). But Allamah Ibn Abidin gave a fatwa that the right of water from canals and rivers is included in the Syrian lands, even if nothing indicating its inclusion is mentioned in the sale of the house, because the custom of these lands is upon that (see: Majmu'at Rasa'il Ibn Abidin 2/136). I say: This also applies today in the city of Damascus regarding the fixtures of its properties from the Fijah water that is purchased for them and supplied to them by pipes, for custom is upon their inclusion without mention. However, in agricultural lands that have water rights from private rivers, the water right is not included in the land unless mentioned or the rights and appurtenances are mentioned, because they are accustomed to dealing with the water rights of lands separately by sale and purchase apart from the land.
Source pages p. 835

Commentary
This footnote expands on the previous rule regarding accessories. The general rule for easement rights (such as water, pathway, drainage) is that they are not included in the sale of a house unless mentioned. However, Ibn Abidin issued a fatwa that in Syria, the right of water from canals and rivers is included by custom even if not mentioned, due to the prevalent custom. The author (al-Zarqa) adds that this applies to the Fijah water system in Damascus today. But for agricultural lands with private river water rights, custom treats these rights as separate property, so they are not included without mention.

Key Terms
حقوق الارتفاق (huquq al-irtifaq): Easement rights: rights of water, pathway, drainage over another's property

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the footnote, in the sale of a house in Damascus today, is the right to Fijah water included without mention?
A) Yes, because custom includes it
B) No, because it must be explicitly mentioned
C) Only if the buyer requests it
D) Only for agricultural land

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi qawida excludes easement rights by default, but local custom can include them, as established by Ibn Abidin's fatwa on Syrian houses.

6 min
Custom Determines Currency Type in Vague Contracts
Explaining rule that custom determines the prevailing currency when type is unspecified

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'custom determines currency type' to resolve ambiguity in sale contracts, and identify when the exception applies leading to a fasid contract.

Core Idea
Custom clarifies ambiguous currency type unless types are equally common — then ambiguity invalidates the sale.

In Hanafi sales law, the price must be known for a valid contract. But when the contract mentions only a generic currency (e.g., 'dinars'), custom steps in to determine which specific type is intended. This lesson examines the qawida from al-Majallah and its furuat, exploring both the rule and its exception.

Guided Path
1
Understand the requirement
Recognize that a known price (ma'lumiyyat al-thaman) is a condition for sale validity. This includes specifying type and amount.
The price can be known by description or by being physically present.
2
Learn the qawida
When the contract mentions a generic currency without specifying type, custom (al-urf) refers it to the most common type (al-arwaj) in that locality.
This is a subsidiary qawida (sughra) derived from 'al-ada muhakkamah'.
3
Apply to typical scenarios
If one type is predominant, the contract is valid and refers to that type. Both parties are bound.
4
Recognize the exception
If two or more types are equally common but have different values, custom cannot resolve the ambiguity. The contract becomes fasid (corrupt) and may be rescinded.
This prevents dispute since each party would claim the favorable type.
5
Evaluate practical outcome
A fasid sale is not automatically void; either party has the option to rescind. If they continue, the price must be renegotiated or a fair value determined.
This protects both parties from exploitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦ - مما يشترط لصحة البيع شرعاً معلومية الثمن، أي تحديده في العقد نوعاً وقدراً، أو أن يكون معيَّناً بالذات كما لو كان حاضراً مشاراً إليه . ولكن صرّح الفقهاء أنه إذا وقع البيع بمبلغ من الدنانير أو الدراهم مثلاً دون أن يبين العاقد نوعها، وكان في البلد منها أنواع مختلفة في القيمة والرواج، فإنَّ العقد يصرف بحكم العرف إلى النوع الأروج، أي الأكثر تعاملاً وتداولاً بين الناس في بلد العقد، ويكون كل من العاقدين ملزماً بذلك.
فإذا كانت أنواعها متساوية في الرواج مع اختلاف القيم فعندئذ يفسد البيع ويستحق الفسخ (المجلة ٢٤٠ وشروحها، وذلك لتأديته إلى نزاع مشكل : فإنَّ البائع عندئذ يطلب الأعلى قيمة والمشتري يتمسك بالأدنى، ولا يوجد عرف يرجع إليه لتساويهما في الرواج.
6. Among the conditions for the validity of a sale legally is that the price be known, i.e., specified in the contract as to type and amount, or be physically present and pointed to. But the jurists have stated that if the sale is made for an amount of dinars or dirhams, for example, without the contracting party specifying their type, and in the country there are various types differing in value and circulation, then the contract is directed by virtue of custom to the most current type, i.e., the one most commonly transacted and circulated among the people in the country of the contract, and each of the contracting parties is bound by that. If the types are equal in circulation but differ in value, then the sale becomes corrupt and subject to rescission (Majallah 240 and its commentaries, because it leads to problematic dispute: the seller then demands the higher value and the buyer insists on the lower, and there is no custom to refer to due to their equality in circulation.
Source pages p. 835

Commentary
This passage presents a rule regarding the determination of currency type when the contract does not specify. Since the price must be known, if the parties mention a generic currency (e.g., 'dinars') and there are multiple types with different values and circulation, custom dictates that the most commonly used type is intended. If all types have equal circulation but different values, the contract is invalid (fasid) and can be rescinded because no custom can resolve the dispute. This shows how custom serves to clarify ambiguity in contracts.

Key Terms
معلمية الثمن (ma'lumiyyat al-thaman): The requirement that the price be known in type and amount
الأروج (al-arwaj): The most common or widely circulated type of currency

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Majallah rule, when does mentioning a generic currency in a sale contract make the contract fasid?
A) When the currency is not specified at all
B) When there are multiple types and one is predominant
C) When two or more types are equally common but have different values
D) When the parties disagree after the sale

Key Takeaway
Custom determines the intended currency type in vague contracts, but only when one type predominates; equality of circulation leads to a fasid sale.

8 min
The Authority of Custom (al-‘Ādah Muḥakkamah) in Contracts
A qawida-with-furuat presentation demonstrating al-‘ādah muḥakkamah across sales, rentals, gifts, and easements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim al-‘ādah muḥakkamah to determine implicit terms in sales, rentals, and gifts, and identify when custom cannot resolve a dispute.

Core Idea
Custom fills contractual gaps when parties are silent, but cannot override explicit stipulations.

The Hanafi school gives significant weight to custom (al-‘urf) in interpreting contracts. This lesson explores the application of the maxim al-‘ādah muḥakkamah across multiple fiqh chapters, from the sale of a bicycle to wedding gifts. You will learn how custom operates as a default rule and where its authority ceases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contractual gap
Read each scenario and determine what term is missing in the contract. For example, in the bicycle sale, the contract does not specify which accessories are included.
The gap must be an unstated term, not a violation of a required condition.
2
Examine the prevailing custom
For each gap, ask: Is there a consistent, widespread practice among people in that region regarding this term? Consider the source's examples: pump included vs. lamp excluded.
Custom must be general (not individual) and consistent.
3
Apply the maxim: custom is authoritative
If a custom exists, it governs the gap as an implicit condition. Both parties are bound accordingly. For instance, rent payment terms follow local custom if not stipulated.
The maxim applies even if the custom seems arbitrary.
4
Note exceptions and conflicts
Where multiple customs conflict (e.g., equal circulation but different value) or where a custom does not exist, the maxim fails. The sale becomes defective or detailed fiqh rules apply.
Also, custom cannot override an explicit contractual condition.
5
Distinguish when custom does not apply
In agricultural lands with private river water rights, the relevant custom treats water as separate, so it is not included without mention. This shows custom is interpreted narrowly.
Always verify the specific custom of the community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(بیسیکلیت) يدخل منفاخها والمفاتيح الأساسية لفك أجزائها، وحقيبة أدواتها التي تعلق عليها للعرف في ذلك، ولكن لا يدخل مصباحها إلا بالذكر لعدم العرف .
وفي بيع السيارة اليوم كذلك يحكم العرف فيما يتبعها بلا ذكر أو لا بعها (١).
٦ ـ مما يشترط لصحة البيع شرعاً معلومية الثمن، أي تحديده في العقد نوعاً وقدراً، أو أن يكون معيَّناً بالذات كما لو كان حاضراً مشاراً إليه . ولكن صرّح الفقهاء أنه إذا وقع البيع بمبلغ من الدنانير أو الدراهم مثلاً دون أن يبين العاقد نوعها، وكان في البلد منها أنواع مختلفة في القيمة والرواج، فإنَّ العقد يصرف بحكم العرف إلى النوع الأروج، أي الأكثر تعاملاً وتداولاً بين الناس في بلد العقد، ويكون كل من العاقدين ملزماً بذلك.
فإذا كانت أنواعها متساوية في الرواج مع اختلاف القيم فعندئذ يفسد البيع ويستحق الفسخ (المجلة ٢٤٠ وشروحها، وذلك لتأديته إلى نزاع مشكل : فإنَّ البائع عندئذ يطلب الأعلى قيمة والمشتري يتمسك بالأدنى، ولا يوجد عرف يرجع إليه لتساويهما في الرواج.
(۱) وفي حقوق العقار الارتفاقية كالشرب والممر والمسيل يذكر الفقهاء أنها لا تدخل في بيع الدار ونحوها إلا إذا ذكرت في العقد أو ذكر أن الدار بيعت بحقوقها ومرافقها جملة، ونحو ذلك من الألفاظ العامة التي تتناولها (ر ): المجلة / ٢٣٥ وشروحها والدرر ١٤٩/٢).

أقول: وينطبق أيضاً هذا في مدينة دمشق اليوم على ما يخص عقاراتها من مياه الفيجة التي تشترى لها وتمد إليها بالأنابيب، فإن العرف على دخولها بلا ذكر. لكن في الأراضي الزراعية ذات حقوق الشرب من الأنهار الخاصة لا يدخل الشرب في الأرض إلا بذكره أو ذكر الحقوق والمرافق لأنهم متعارفون على التصرف بشرب الأراضي بيعاً وشراءاً منفرداً عن الأرض.

عن
Section (69): The Authority of Custom. (Bicycle) Its pump, basic tools for disassembling its parts, and the tool bag that hangs on it are included by custom, but its lamp is not included unless mentioned due to lack of custom. In the sale of a car today, custom similarly rules regarding what accompanies it without mention or not. 6. Among the conditions for a valid sale legally is that the price be known, i.e., its type and amount be specified in the contract, or that it be determined by reference as if it were present and pointed to. But the jurists stated that if a sale is made for an amount of dinars or dirhams without the contracting party specifying their type, and there are different types in the country varying in value and circulation, then the contract is interpreted by custom to refer to the most common type, i.e., the most current in circulation among people in the country of the contract, and both parties are bound by that. If the types are equal in circulation but differ in value, then the sale is defective and subject to rescission (al-Majallah 240 and its commentaries, because it leads to problematic dispute: then the seller demands the highest value and the buyer insists on the lowest, and there is no custom to refer to since they are equal in circulation. (1) Regarding easement rights of real estate such as waterway, pathway, and drain, the jurists mention that they are not included in the sale of a house and the like unless mentioned in the contract or it is mentioned that the house is sold with all its rights and facilities, and similar general expressions that encompass them (see: al-Majallah 235 and its commentaries, and al-Durr 2/149). But Allamah Ibn Abidin gave a fatwa that the right of water from canals and rivers is included in the Syrian lands, even if nothing is mentioned in the sale of the house that indicates its inclusion, because the custom of these lands is that (see: Majmu'at Rasa'il Ibn Abidin 2/136). I say: This also applies today in the city of Damascus to the water of al-Fijah that is purchased for its properties and supplied to them by pipes, for custom includes it without mention. But in agricultural lands with water rights from private rivers, the water right is not included in the land unless it or the rights and facilities are mentioned, because people customarily deal with water rights of lands separately from the land. The manner of paying the rent is subject to the condition of the contracting parties. If they did not stipulate, then the manner of payment is subject to custom regarding prompt payment, deferment, or installment. If there is no known custom in that, then regarding the manner of paying rent for real estate, persons, or things and animals, there is a detailed fiqh explanation given in the book of Ijarah in fiqh compilations and in Articles 466-483; so reference to custom when there is no contractual condition is prioritized in fiqh. From al-Majallah. If a father equips his daughter with what he has for her marriage, then they differ regarding whether what he gave her was on loan (so he has the right to reclaim it) or it was a gift (so it is hers); similarly, if the daughter dies in this state, and there is a dispute between the father and the husband over the husband's inheritance share, the settled opinion of the jurists in this context is to apply custom, considering the people's habit and the father's status and lack thereof. So if the continuous or prevalent custom is that such a father usually gives such equipment as a gift, then the daughter's word is taken if she is alive, or the husband's if she is deceased. If the current customs are that such a father does not give such equipment as a gift, then the father's word is taken that it is a loan. See: the book "al-Qawl al-Hasan fi Jawab: al-Qawl li Man?" in the chapter on Hibah, and Rasa'il Ibn Abidin 2/143, quoting from the motherbooks of the Hanafi school.
Source pages p. 835

Commentary
This passage is a qawida-with-furuat presentation. The underlying maxim is al-‘ādatu muḥakkamah (custom is authoritative). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, applies this maxim to several concrete cases across different chapters of fiqh. First, in the sale of a bicycle, custom determines which accessories are included: the pump, tools, and tool bag are included by custom, but the lamp is not because it is not customary. This extends to modern car sales. Second, in the sale of currency, if the type of dinars or dirhams is not specified, custom refers to the most commonly circulating type. If two types are equally common but differ in value, the sale becomes defective because there is no custom to resolve the dispute. Third, in the sale of real estate, easement rights (water, pathway, drain) are generally not included unless explicitly mentioned. However, Ibn Abidin gave a fatwa that in Syrian lands, the right to water from canals and rivers is included by virtue of local custom. Al-Zarqa adds that this applies today in Damascus to the piped water supply, but not to agricultural lands with private river water rights, because people there trade water separately. Fourth, in rental contracts, if the parties do not specify payment terms (lump sum, deferred, or installments), custom determines the mode of payment. If no custom exists, the detailed rules in fiqh books apply, but custom takes precedence. Fifth, in disputes between a father and daughter (or her husband after her death) over whether wedding gifts were loans or gifts, the decisive factor is custom: if the custom of the father's social circle is to give such items as gifts, the daughter/husband's claim prevails; if custom is that such fathers do not give them as gifts, the father's claim (loan) prevails. The passage shows that custom operates as a default rule to fill gaps in contracts and prevent disputes, but it cannot override an explicit stipulation. The Hanafi school consistently upholds custom in these contexts, as reflected in the Majallah and Ibn Abidin's works.

Key Terms
العادة محكمة (al-‘ādah muḥakkamah): The legal maxim 'custom is authoritative', used to fill gaps in contracts where no explicit term exists.
العرف (al-‘urf): Custom that is prevalent and consistent among people, used to determine implicit terms in contracts.
الأروج (al-arwaj): The most common and widely circulated type (of currency) in a region.
حق الشرب (haqq al-shurb): A right of easement for water, particularly for irrigation or household use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented, if a man sells a bicycle and does not mention the accessories, which item is NOT included by custom?
A) The pump
B) The basic tools
C) The lamp
D) The tool bag

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim al-‘ādah muḥakkamah applies to fill gaps in contracts, but only when no custom conflicts and when the parties have not stipulated otherwise.

Custom Determines Payment Method in Lease
Applies custom to determine payment schedule in lease

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim al-'adah muhakkamah to determine the method of rent payment in an ijarah contract when no stipulation exists.

Core Idea
Custom determines payment method in lease only when the parties have no stipulation.

This passage from the Majallah illustrates how the Hanafi legal maxim 'Custom is authoritative' ('al-'adah muhakkamah') operates in leasing. When parties to a lease contract do not specify the payment schedule, custom steps in to fill the gap. Understanding this hierarchy—first stipulation, then custom, then default rules—is essential for issuing correct fatwas in financial transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the governing legal maxim
Recognize that the passage applies the Hanafi maxim 'al-'adah muhakkamah' (custom is authoritative). This is a major (kubra) qa'ida governing all mu'amalat.
2
Understand the hierarchy of sources
Note the priority: (1) the contracting parties' express stipulation, (2) if absent, prevailing custom, (3) if no known custom, the detailed fiqh rules in the Majallah (articles 466-483).
Custom does not override an explicit stipulation; it only fills silence.
3
Identify the scope of custom
Custom determines three possibilities for rent payment: immediate (al-ta'jil), deferred (al-ta'jil), or in installments (al-tanjim). The custom of the locality or trade governs.
4
Apply to a new case
Given a lease with no payment clause, look to the local custom (e.g., monthly advance payment). If multiple customs exist, the one most beneficial to both parties or the prevalent one is followed.
5
Note the exception
Custom is not authoritative if it contradicts a clear text (nass) or a fundamental principle of Shari'ah. In this context, custom cannot be used to justify riba or gharar.
This is why the passage adds a fallback to detailed fiqh rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن كيفية دفع أجرة المأجور يتبع فيها شرط العاقدين. فإن لم يشترطا فإنَّ كيفية الدفع عندئذ تخضع للعرف في التعجيل أو التأجيل أو التنجيم، أي التقسيط. فإن لم يكن في ذلك عرف معروف ففي كيفية دفع الأجرة في إجارة العقار، أو الأشخاص، أو الأشياء والحيوان يوجد تفصيل فقهي مبين في كتاب الإجارة من المدونات الفقهية وفي المواد / ٤٦٦ - ٤٨٣/ فالنظر في ذلك إلى العرف عند عدم الشرط العقدي، هو المقدَّم فقهاً . من المجلة.
Indeed, the manner of paying the rent of the leased object is subject to the stipulation of the contracting parties. If they do not stipulate, then the method of payment at that time is subject to custom: immediate payment, deferred payment, or installment payment. If there is no known custom in that regard, then regarding the method of paying rent in the lease of real estate, persons, or chattels and animals, there is a detailed fiqh exposition found in the book of Ijarah in fiqh compilations and in articles 466-483. So the consideration in that case is custom when there is no contractual stipulation; that is the preferred view in fiqh. From the Majallah.
Source pages p. 836

Commentary
This passage presents a rule (rasm) from the theory of custom: the method of paying rent in a lease contract is determined first by the parties' stipulation. If they are silent, then the prevailing custom governs. If there is no custom, then the detailed fiqh rules in the Majallah (articles 466-483) apply. The passage reflects the Hanafi principle that custom holds authority in matters not explicitly regulated by contract or revealed texts.

Key Terms
المأجور (al-ma'jur): The object or service that is leased, i.e., the subject matter of the lease contract.
التنجيم (al-tanjim): Paying in installments, i.e., in portions at specified times.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The lessor's preference
B) The prevailing custom
C) The lessee's ability
D) The judge's discretion

Key Takeaway
In leasing, when parties are silent on payment method, custom fills the gap—this is a direct application of the Hanafi maxim 'al-'adah muhakkamah'.

Custom as the Arbiter in Father-Daughter Disputes over Wedding Gifts
Uses custom to adjudicate ownership of wedding gifts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-'aadatu muhakkamah' to resolve a financial dispute between a father and daughter over wedding gifts when evidence is lacking.

Core Idea
Custom determines ownership of wedding gifts: if custom treats them as gifts, the daughter keeps them; if as loans, the father can reclaim them.

When a father provides wedding gifts (jihaz) for his daughter, a dispute may arise after the marriage whether the items were given as a loan (ariyah) or as ownership (tamlik). The Hanafi jurists have settled that the resolution depends on the prevailing custom (urf). This lesson explores how the maxim 'custom is authoritative' provides a clear criterion for such disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Dispute and Parties
Recognize the conflict: father claims loan (right to reclaim), daughter/husband claims ownership. Note the lack of explicit proof.
2
Recognize the Lack of Evidence
No witnesses or documents exist. The standard principle qawl liman (who speaks?) cannot be applied by default; custom fills the gap.
3
Determine the Prevailing Custom
Investigate the common practice in that society: do fathers of similar status give such items as gifts or loans? The custom must be consistent and widespread.
The father's social standing (wajahah) is a factor in assessing likelihood.
4
Apply the Custom to Decide Whose Word is Accepted
If custom indicates ownership, the daughter's (or husband's) claim is accepted; if loan, the father's claim prevails. This implements the maxim 'custom is authoritative'.
5
Confirm the Ruling by the Maxim
The principle 'العادة محكمة' (custom is authoritative) is a major legal maxim (qa'ida kubra) that governs this and similar financial disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو جهز الأب ابنته ماله من في زواجها، ثم اختلف وإياها اختلف وإياها في أن ما أخرجه لها كان على سبيل العارية فيحق له استرداده، أو كان على سبيل التمليك فهو لها ؛ وكذلك لو ماتت البنت والحالة هذه، فوقع الاختلاف بین الأب والزوج من أجل نصيبه الإرثي، فالذي استقر عليه رأي الفقهاء في هذا المقام هو تحكيم العرف بالنظر إلى عادة الناس وحال الأب من حيث الوجاهة وعدمها : في ذلك - فإذا كان العرف مستمراً أو غالباً أنَّ مثل هذا الأب إنما يخرج مثل هذا الجهاز عادة على سبيل التمليك فالقول للبنت لو كانت حية، أو للزوج لو متوفاة. - أما إذا كان في العادات الجارية أن مثل هذا الأب لا يخرج مثل هذا الجهاز تمليكاً فالقول للأب في أنه عارية ر: كتاب «القول الحسن في جواب : القول لمن؟» (۱) في باب الهبة، ورسائل ابن عابدين، ١٤٣/٢ نقلاً عن أمهات كتب المذهب الحنفي.
If the father outfits his daughter with his property for her marriage, then they disagree with each other over whether what he gave her was on the basis of a loan (so he has the right to recover it) or on the basis of ownership (so it is hers). Similarly, if the daughter dies in that state and a dispute occurs between the father and the husband regarding the husband's inheritance share, then the settled opinion of the jurists in this matter is to apply custom, looking at the people's habit and the father's status in terms of respectability or lack thereof. So if the ongoing or predominant custom is that such a father usually gives such an outfit as ownership, then the daughter's word (if alive) or the husband's word (if deceased) is taken. But if the prevailing custom is that such a father does not give such an outfit as ownership, then the father's word (that it is a loan) is taken. Refer to the book 'Al-Qawl al-Hasan fi Jawab: al-Qawlu liman?' in the chapter of Hibah, and Ibn Abideen's Rasa'il, 2/143, quoting from the primary Hanafi books.
Source pages p. 836

Commentary
This passage exemplifies the principle of custom in financial disputes between father and daughter regarding marriage gifts (jihaz). The jurists establish a rule: the nature of the gift (loan or ownership) is determined by the prevalent custom. If the custom is that fathers gift such items permanently, the daughter's or husband's claim is preferred. If custom indicates loan, the father's claim prevails. This is a manifestation of the maxim 'custom is authoritative' in resolving competing claims.

Key Terms
الجهاز (al-jihaz): The outfit or items provided by a father to his daughter for marriage, including furniture, clothing, etc.
القول لمن (al-qawlu liman): The principle of whose word is accepted in judicial disputes when evidence is lacking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) By the daughter's preference
C) By the prevailing custom
D) By the value of the gifts

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) is authoritative in resolving disputes over wedding gifts when evidence is lacking, overriding default presumptions.
