Custom and Formalities for Written Instruments
Applies custom to formal requirements for legal documents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'Custom is authoritative' to determine the validity of a written document based on its conformity to customary formalities.

Core Idea
A written document is legally binding only if it follows the customary formalities of its time and place.

The Hanafi school treats written documents as equivalent to verbal statements for legal transactions, but only if they meet certain conditions rooted in custom. This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa explains the key terms mustabinah and marsumah, showing how the requirements evolve with changing practices. As a mufti, you must assess whether a document follows the prevailing urf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Recognize that written documents must be 'mustabinah' (on a tangible surface) and 'marsumah' (written according to customary formalities). These are the pillars of validity.
Both conditions must be satisfied; missing either invalidates the document.
2
Analyze 'mustabinah'
Understand that the writing must appear on something physical like paper, not on air, water, or a wall. This ensures permanence and clarity.
Transactions attempted via impermanent writing are void.
3
Analyze 'marsumah'
Understand that the document must follow the customary form of its time: traditionally paper, address, and seal/signature. The author notes that customs change; today a signature alone may suffice.
The standard is dynamic – what is 'marsumah' depends on current urf.
4
Apply to a contemporary scenario
Take a modern deed with only a signature at the bottom. According to contemporary custom, this is considered marsumah and thus legally binding. The author explicitly confirms this.
The mufti must know the prevailing custom in his society.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في قضية الرسائل والصكوك نص الفقهاء على أنها لكي تعتبر وتكون كالنطق باللسان يجب أن تكون مكتوبة وفقاً للعرف والعادة، بأن تكون مستبينة مرسومة. ومعنى كونها مستبينة أن تكون على شيء تظهر عليه، فلا عبرة للكتابة بالأصبع مثلاً على الهواء أو الماء أو الجدار، ولا ينعقد بها تصرف. ومعنى كونها مرسومة أن تكون محرّرة وفقاً للمراسم والتقاليد المعتادة - ففي الرسالة المكتوبة يجب اليوم أن تكون على قرطاس، ومعنونة، ومصدرة ببيان المرسل والمرسل إليه كقولهم من فلان بن فلان إلى فلان بن فلان، ومختومة بخاتم المرسل أو موقعة بتوقيعه (ر ) المجلة / ٦٩ وشرحها للأتاسي، ورد المحتار ٤٢٨/٢). فالكتابة اليوم على عظم أو جلد أو ورقة شجرة لا عبرة لما فيها من أو شراء أو طلاق أو غير ذلك. وكذلك إذا كان الكتاب خطاب بإيجاب بيع على قرطاس ولكنه لم يكون معنوناً، لأن كل ذلك يعتبر غير مرسوم. أقول : في عرفنا اليوم لا حاجة إلى تصدير الكتاب بذكر المرسل. يكفي تصديره باسم المرسل إليه ، ثم توقيع المرسل في ذيل الكتاب. ومن العرف الزمني اليوم الاكتفاء في تحرير الصكوك بتوقيع ذي العلاقة عليها فقط دون خطاب في أولها ودون بيان أن الموقع يقر ويعترف بمضمونها. فمجرد توقيعه على صك يتضمن عقداً بينه وبين غيره، أو من تصرفات الإرادة المنفردة كطلاق أو وصية أو وقف، يعتبر إقراراً خطياً من الموقع بمضمون الصك.
Regarding the issue of letters and deeds, the jurists have stated that for them to be considered and to be like verbal utterance, they must be written in accordance with custom and habit, such that they are clear (mustabinah) and formal (marsumah). The meaning of 'clear' is that they be on something on which they appear; thus, writing with a finger on air, water, or a wall is not considered, and no transaction is concluded by it. The meaning of 'formal' is that they be drafted according to conventional formalities and traditions. So in a written letter nowadays, it must be on paper, addressed, and prefaced with a statement of the sender and recipient, e.g., 'From so-and-so son of so-and-so to so-and-so son of so-and-so', and stamped with the sender's seal or signed with his signature (see Majallah article 69 and its commentary by al-Atasi, and Radd al-Muhtar 2/428). Thus, writing on bone, leather, or tree bark is not considered; any sale, purchase, divorce, or other transaction in such writing is invalid. Similarly, if a letter is an offer of sale on paper but is not addressed, that is considered informal. I say: In our custom today, there is no need to preface the letter with the sender's name; it suffices to begin with the recipient's name, then the sender's signature at the end. And from contemporary custom, it is sufficient in drafting deeds to have only the signature of the relevant party at the bottom, without an opening address and without stating that the signatory acknowledges and admits its contents. Thus, merely his signature on a deed containing a contract between him and another, or a unilateral disposition such as divorce, will, or endowment, is considered a written acknowledgment by the signatory of the deed's contents.
Source pages p. 837

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the rule that written documents are legally equivalent to verbal statements only if they comply with customary formalities. It defines two key conditions: the writing must be on a physical medium (mustabinah) and follow customary formalities (marsumah). The author then gives historical and contemporary examples: traditional requirements like paper, address, and seal; and modern customs where signature alone suffices. This reflects the Hanafi principle that custom evolves and legal formalities adapt accordingly.

Key Terms
مستبينة (mustabinah): Writing that appears on a tangible surface, as opposed to writing on air, water, etc.
مرسومة (marsumah): Writing that follows customary formalities and conventions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following would be considered marsumah in contemporary custom?
A) A letter written on a bone with no address
B) A deed that includes only the signature of the relevant party at the bottom
C) A contract written on a wall
D) An offer written on water

Key Takeaway
A written document is legally equivalent to a verbal statement only if it meets the customary formalities of its time and place.

Validity of Written Documents: Customary Conditions
Conditions for written documents to be legally effective according to custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'al-ʿādah muḥakkamah' to determine the legal validity of a written document by checking the two conditions of visibility (mustabīnah) and formality (marsūmah), and explain how changing customs affect the required format.

Core Idea
A written document is valid only if it is visible and follows custom; form matters as much as content.

In Hanafi fiqh, written documents are generally accepted as evidence, but not every scribble counts. The jurists laid down two conditions rooted in custom: the writing must be on a surface that displays it (mustabīnah) and it must be drafted according to the customary formalities of the time (marsūmah). This lesson unpacks these conditions and shows how custom evolves, affecting legal practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Read the passage and extract the two conditions (mustabīnah and marsūmah) that make a written document equivalent to oral speech. Understand the literal meaning and the legal implication.
These conditions are based on the maxim al-ʿādah muḥakkamah.
2
Distinguish between visible and formal
Analyse the sub-condition 'mustabīnah' (visible) – the writing must be on a tangible medium that shows it, not air, water, etc. Then analyse 'marsūmah' – it must follow the formalities customary at the time.
The passage gives historical examples: paper, addressing, signature.
3
Trace the evolution of custom
Note how the author (al-Zarqa) updates the earlier fiqh by saying that today, the sender's name in the heading is not needed; the recipient name and signature suffice. Also, a bare signature on a promissory note constitutes guarantee or assignment according to commercial custom.
This shows the dynamic nature of customary conditions.
4
Apply the conditions to the fuduli case
Work through the fatwa incident: an unauthorised person (fuduli) contracts a lease, writes a document signed by the two parties, then the owner signs. According to modern custom, the owner's signature is considered ratification (ijazah) because it is placed for documentation and expresses consent.
This demonstrates how custom determines legal effect.
5
Generalise using the qawida
Connect all the examples to the overarching maxim 'al-ʿādah muḥakkamah' and see how it operates across different chapters of fiqh (sales, leases, commercial instruments).
Identify the scope and any possible exceptions (istithna).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[رجوع إلى: المجلة / 69 وشرحها للأتاسي، ورد المحتار 428/2]. فالكتابة اليوم على عظم أو جلد أو ورقة شجرة لا عبرة لما فيها من بيع أو شراء أو طلاق أو غير ذلك. وكذلك إذا كان الكتاب خطاب بإيجاب بيع على قرطاس ولكنه لم يكن معنوناً، لأن كل ذلك يعتبر غير مرسوم. أقول : في عرفنا اليوم لا حاجة إلى تصدير الكتاب بذكر المرسل، يكفي تصديره باسم المرسل إليه ، ثم توقيع المرسل في ذيل الكتاب. ومن العرف الزمني اليوم الاكتفاء في تحرير الصكوك بتوقيع ذي العلاقة عليها فقط دون خطاب في أولها ودون بيان أن الموقع يقر ويعترف بمضمونها. فمجرد توقيعه على صك يتضمن عقداً بينه وبين غيره، أو من تصرفات الإرادة المنفردة كطلاق أو وصية أو وقف، يعتبر إقراراً خطياً من الموقع بمضمون الصك. حتى إن من العرف التجاري الذي يؤيده قانون التجارة أن مجرد توقيع الشخص الثالث على سند الأمر المسمى (كمبيالة) دون ذكر أية عبارة، يعتبر كفالة منه؛ وأن المستفيد من سند الأمر إذا وقع في ظهره توقيعاً مجرداً على بياض وسلمه لشخص كان ذلك التوقيع حوالة منه إلى المستلم، أو إلى من يختاره المستلم. فكل ذلك أعراف معتبرة تثبت لها أحكامها شرعاً. هذا، ومما يجدر التنبيه إليه من العرف الزمني في أمر الصكوك أن التوقيع على صك من شخص ذي علاقة ولو كان هو غير العاقد يعتبر رضا بالعقد في عرف الناس اليوم، لأن وضع التوقيع إنما يقصد به غرض توثيقي، وإلا كان عبثاً، فهو يوضع عادة للتعبير عن الرضا والموافقة. وعلى هذا لو عقد فضولي إجارة على مال شخص، وكتب بالعقد صك وقعه العاقدان، ثم اطلع عليه المالك فوقعه باسمه أيضاً. فتوقيع المالك يعتبر بحسب العرف إجازة لعقد الفضولي، فينفذ على المالك. [هذه حادثة الفتوى من وقائع العصر، وقد سئلت عنها فأجبت بهذا الجواب].
9. On the issue of letters and documents, the jurists have stated that for them to be considered and equivalent to oral speech, they must be written in accordance with custom and habit, i.e., they must be visible and formal. The meaning of 'visible' is that they be on something that shows them, so writing with a finger on air, water, or a wall is disregarded, and no transaction is concluded by it. The meaning of 'formal' is that it be drafted according to the customary formalities. Thus, a written letter today must be on paper, addressed, and headed with a statement of the sender and recipient, e.g., 'From so-and-so so-and-so to so-and-so so-and-so', and sealed with the sender's seal or signed with his signature. [Refer to al-Majalla / 69 and its commentary by al-Atasi, and Radd al-Muhtar 2/428]. So today, writing on bone, skin, or tree leaf is disregarded for any sale, purchase, divorce, or other matter. Similarly, if the letter is a message offering a sale on paper but is not addressed, that is also considered informal. I say: In our custom today, there is no need to head the letter with mentioning the sender; it suffices to head it with the name of the recipient, then the sender's signature at the end of the letter. Additionally, from contemporary custom, in drafting documents it suffices to have the signature of the concerned party alone, without any address at the beginning and without a statement that the signatory acknowledges and confesses its content. Thus, merely his signature on a document that includes a contract between him and another, or a unilateral disposition such as divorce, will, or endowment, is considered a written acknowledgment by the signatory of the document's content. Even from commercial custom, which is supported by commercial law, that simply the signature of a third party on a promissory note called a 'bill of exchange' without stating any phrase is considered a guarantee from him; and that the beneficiary of a promissory note, if he puts on its back a bare signature on a blank space and hands it to a person, that signature is considered an assignment from him to the recipient, or to whomever the recipient chooses. All these are valid customs that have their legal effects established in Islamic law. This, and it is worth noting from contemporary custom regarding documents, that the signature on a document by a concerned party, even if he is not the contracting party, is considered consent to the contract in people's custom today, because the purpose of placing the signature is merely documentary; otherwise it would be frivolous. So it is placed customarily to express consent and approval. On this basis, if an unauthorized person (fuduli) enters into a lease contract on someone's property, and writes up a document signed by the two contracting parties, then the owner sees it and signs his name on it as well, then the owner's signature, according to custom, is considered ratification of the unauthorized contract, so it becomes binding on the owner. [This is a fatwa incident from contemporary facts; I was asked about it and gave this response.]
Source pages p. 837

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive Hanafi ruling on the validity of written documents. It establishes two key conditions based on custom: the writing must be visible (مستبينة) – meaning on a surface that displays it, not ephemeral – and it must be formal (مرسومة) – meaning it follows the customary format and formalities. The author gives examples from earlier fiqh: a letter must be on paper, addressed, and signed/sealed. Then he notes that customs change; today, the sender's name is not required on the heading; the recipient's name and the sender's signature suffice. He also extends this to commercial instruments like promissory notes (sukuk al-amr, i.e., bills of exchange), where a bare signature on the back constitutes a guarantee or an assignment according to commercial custom. This aligns with the Hanafi principle that customary practice determines the legal effect of signatures. Finally, he addresses the modern practice of a property owner signing a contract made by a fuduli (unauthorised agent); that signature is now considered tacit ratification (ijazah) due to custom, binding the owner. This shows the dynamic nature of fiqh: rulings adapt to evolving customs as long as they do not contradict established principles.

Key Terms
مستبينة (mustabīnah): Visible; written on a surface that displays the writing.
مرسومة (marsūmah): Formal; drafted according to customary formalities.
فضولي (fuḍūlī): A person who acts on behalf of another without authorization.
إجازة (ijāzah): Ratification or approval of a contract made by an unauthorized person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi jurists, what two conditions must a written document fulfill to have legal effect?
A) It must be witnessed and sealed.
B) It must be visible (على شيء تظهر عليه) and formal (وفقاً للمراسم المعتادة).
C) It must be written on paper and signed.
D) It must be registered with the government.

Key Takeaway
Written documents must be both visible and follow customary formalities to be legally effective in Hanafi fiqh.

Custom-Based Rulings Change with Custom
Explains the principle that custom-based rulings evolve with custom and its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'La yunkaru taghayyur al-ahkam bi-taghayyur al-azman' to modern transaction scenarios by identifying which rulings are custom-based and explaining how they change.

Core Idea
Rulings based on custom change as custom changes, but fixed Shariah rulings remain unaltered.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi legal principle that rulings derived from custom evolve with the custom itself. This is captured in the famous maxim 'It is not denied that rulings change with the change of times' (Majalla 39). We will examine practical examples from commercial transactions and understand the scope and limits of this changeability.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its legal basis
Write down the maxim 'La yunkaru taghayyur al-ahkam bi-taghayyur al-azman' and understand that it is derived from the principle that the ruling revolves with its cause ('illa). Custom is one such cause.
The maxim is a wusta (intermediate) legal maxim governing mu'amalat.
2
Identify the scope: custom-based rulings only
Recognize that this maxim applies only to rulings that are founded on custom ('urf). Fixed ibadat and established Shariah rulings (like the obligation of prayer) are not subject to change by custom.
Al-Zarqa emphasizes this distinction: rulings based on custom change, but primary rulings do not.
3
Apply the maxim to the given furuat (examples)
Work through each example from the passage: (1) third-party signature on a bill of exchange as guarantee, (2) blank endorsement as hawala, (3) owner's signature on a fuduli's contract as ratification. Explain how each is considered valid by current commercial custom.
These examples show custom defining legal meanings of actions.
4
Distinguish between general and specific custom
Note that specific custom (e.g., local practice) applies only within its sphere, while general custom has broader effect. The maxim holds for both, but specific custom is territorial or group-limited.
This is the dabt (restriction) of the maxim: 'Specific custom does not exceed its place.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف
حتى إن من العرف التجاري الذي يؤيده قانون التجارة أن مجرد الشخص الثالث على سند الأمر المسمى (كمبيالة) دون ذكر أية عبارة، يعتبر كفالة منه ؛ وأن المستفيد من سند الأمر إذا وقع في ظهره توقيعاً مجرداً على بياض وسلمه لشخص كان ذلك التوقيع حوالة منه إلى المستلم، أو إلى من يختاره المستلم .
فكل ذلك أعراف معتبرة تثبت لها أحكامها شرعاً .

المالك يعتبر بحسب العرف إجازة لعقد الفضولي، فينفذ على المالك .
هذه صور متنوعة أوردناها على سبيل التمثيل. وقد من مختلف النواحي الفقهية التي تغلغل فيها تأثير العرف المدني . في المعاملات، وهي غيض من فيض. ومنها يعرف ما أقره النظر الفقهي للعرف في المعاملات أيضاً من حاكمية وسلطان.
نتيجة :
الأحكام المبنية على العرف تتبدل بتبدله:
- ومن الواضح بعدما تقدم أن ما جميع بني من الأحكام على العرف يتبدل بتبدل العرف ويدور معه كيفما استدار . ذلك لأن من المقرر فقهاً أن الحكم يدور مع العلة .
وعن هذا وضعت القاعدة الفقهية القائلة: «لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان (ر) المجلة/ ٣٩ وشروحها). ولا فرق في العرف الذي تخضع له الأحكام بين العرف العام والعرف الخاص، غير أنَّ العرف الخاص بمكان أو بفئة من الناس يقتصر اعتباره وسلطانه على المكان أو الفئة اللذين استقرَّ العرف فيهما، ولا يتعداهما. فقد يكون العرف اللفظي أو العملي في موضوع ما ، كمعنى كلمة الفريضة الشرعية، وكتأجيل قدر من المهر أو تقسيط أجرة العقار، مختلفاً مکان بین وآخر، فيجري على أهل كل مكان حكم عرفهم الخاص. وسيأتي لذلك مزيد بیان
Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. Even from commercial custom supported by commercial law, the mere signature of a third party on a bill of exchange (called a promissory note) without mentioning any phrase is considered a guarantee from him; and that the beneficiary of a bill of exchange, if he signs on its back with a bare signature on a blank space and hands it to a person, that signature is a transfer (hawala) from him to the recipient, or to whomever the recipient chooses. So all these are considered valid customs, and their rulings are established legally. Moreover, it is worth noting from contemporary custom regarding documents that the signature on a document by a related person, even if he is not the contracting party, is considered consent to the contract in people's custom today, because the purpose of placing a signature is documentary, otherwise it would be futile. So it is usually placed to express consent and approval. Based on this, if a fuduli (unauthorized agent) contracts a lease on someone's property and writes a document signed by the two contractors, then the owner sees it and signs it with his name as well, the owner's signature is considered by custom as ratification of the fuduli's contract, so it becomes binding on the owner. These are various examples we have presented by way of illustration, from different jurisprudential aspects in which the influence of civil custom has penetrated in transactions, and it is a drop from an ocean. From them one knows the authority and dominance that jurisprudential consideration has established for custom in transactions as well. Result: Rulings based on custom change with its change. It is clear from what has preceded that all rulings built upon custom change with the change of custom and revolve with it wherever it revolves. That is because it is established jurisprudentially that the ruling revolves with the cause. And from this the legal maxim was laid down: 'It is not denied that rulings change with the change of times' (Majalla Art. 39 and its commentaries). There is no difference in the custom that governs rulings between general custom and specific custom, except that specific custom of a place or a group of people is limited in its validity and authority to the place or group in which it is established, and does not extend beyond them. So verbal or practical custom in a certain matter, like the meaning of the word 'fard' (obligatory) or like deferring part of the dowry or installments of rent, may differ from one place to another, so the ruling of their specific custom applies to the people of each place. A further explanation will come.
Source pages p. 838

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi discusses the theory of custom ('urf) in Hanafi jurisprudence. It is a legal philosophy and qawida passage that demonstrates how custom operates as a source of law, especially in transactions. The author begins by giving practical examples from commercial law: in trade customs, a third party's signature on a bill of exchange is a guarantee; a blank endorsement on the back of a bill is a transfer (hawala). These are recognized as valid customs that produce legal effects. He then highlights a contemporary custom: a person's signature on a document is considered consent, even if that person was not the original contracting party. This is because the signature has a documentary purpose, indicating approval. Using this, he gives a ruling: If a fuduli (unauthorized agent) leases someone's property and both contracting parties sign, and then the owner also signs, that signature is, by custom, a ratification (ijaza) of the fuduli's contract, making it binding on the owner. This is a furu' (application) of the principle. The author then states the core result: rulings built on custom change as custom changes, because the ruling revolves with its cause ('illa). This leads to the well-known maxim: 'It is not denied that rulings change with the change of times' (Majalla Art. 39). He then clarifies that both general and specific customs are valid, but specific custom (e.g., of a locality or profession) applies only within that sphere. Examples include the meaning of 'fard' or the deferment of dowry or rent installments varying by place. Thus, this passage establishes the dynamic nature of custom-based rulings and their scope, a key concept in Hanafi usul al-iftaa.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): A practice or custom that people follow and accept, whether verbal or practical, that has become established in their dealings.
الفضولي (al-fuduli): An unauthorized agent who acts on behalf of another without permission, resulting in a suspended contract pending ratification.
الحكم (al-hukm): A legal ruling derived from a source, including those based on custom that can change with changing circumstances.
القاعدة الفقهية (al-qa'ida al-fiqhiyya): A legal maxim that encapsulates a general principle of fiqh, used to derive rulings in similar cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if an owner signs a lease contract made by a fuduli, what is the legal effect of that signature by custom?
A) The owner is bound as a co-signer.
B) The owner is considered a guarantor for the lease.
C) The signature is considered ratification (ijaza) making the contract binding on the owner.
D) The signature has no effect because the owner was not a party.

Key Takeaway
Custom-based rulings are dynamic and change with evolving customs, but fixed Shariah rulings remain unchanged; this maxim is essential for muftis to responsibly address new transactions.

The Condition of Predominance in Custom
Explaining the requirement that custom be predominant for legal consideration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for considering custom as a legal basis and identify when a custom loses authority due to equal practice and abandonment.

Core Idea
For custom to bind it must be predominant in practice; a custom equally followed and abandoned is no evidence.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa explains a foundational rule in Hanafi legal theory: custom (urf) can be a source of law only if it is consistently practiced by its community. The rule distinguishes between a custom that is predominant (ghalib) and one that is shared equally (mushtarak). Understanding this condition is essential for muftis when they assess custom in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal condition
Recognize that for custom to be authoritative, it must be 'muṭṭarid' (consistent) or 'ghālib' (predominant) – meaning the community practices it more often than not. This is a condition (shart) for legal consideration.
2
Recognize the counter-case: shared custom
If the custom is practiced and abandoned equally, it is 'urf mushtarak'. The text states it is not considered in transactions and not valid as a basis for determining rights and duties.
The reason given is that abandonment on similar or more frequent occasions invalidates the indication of intent.
3
Apply the condition to general and specific customs
Both general customs (widespread in all countries) and specific customs (limited to a region or trade) must be predominant in their respective spheres. Generality alone does not exempt from the condition of predominance.
This prevents misunderstanding that a widespread custom automatically has authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف
فاشتراط الاطراد أو الغلبة في العرف معناه اشتراط الأغلبية العملية فيه لأجل اعتباره حاكماً في الحوادث (۱)
فإنَّ العادة تسمى عرفاً متى اعتادها أكثر القوم ولو كانوا لا يجرون عليها إلا في أقل أعمالهم. وإذا تساوى عملهم بها وعدمه سميت عرفاً مشتركاً، وهو لا يعتبر في معاملات الناس ولا يصلح مستنداً ودليلاً للرجوع إليه في تحديد الحقوق والواجبات المطلقة، لأن عمل القوم به أحياناً إذا صلح دليلاً على قصدهم إلى تحكيمه فتركهم له أحياناً مماثلة أو أكثر ينقض هذه الدلالة .
ومن ثم كان من شرائط اعتبار العرف بنوعيه اللفظي والعملي، أن يكون مُطَّرداً أو غالباً بهذا المعنى.
والاطراد أو الغلبة لا يستلزمان أن يكون العرف عاماً، فإنَّ عموم العرف غير اطراده :
-
فالعرف العام هو الذي يكون منتشراً في جميع البلاد.
- والعرف الخاص هو الذي يتعارف في بلد أو بلدان معينة دون سواها، أو بين أهل حرفة أو صناعة مخصوصة دون سواهم، كما تقدم.
فكل من العرف العام والخاص يشترط لاعتباره وتحكيمه في المعاملات المطلقة أن يكون في محيطه مطرداً أو غالباً على أعمال أهله، لأنَّ العرف وإن كان عاماً، قد يكون غالباً في أعمال الناس بالمعنى المشروح أو لا يكون.
النصاب
(1) أما الأغلبية العددية بمعنى أن يكون العرف جارياً بين جميع القوم أو أكثرهم فهي ليست من قبيل الشرائط، بل هي ركن في تكوين العرف لا يتحقق معناه دونها؛ وهي العددي الذي تقدم بيانه لتحقق معنى العرف لأنّ العادة الفردية لا تصبح عرفاً إلا إذا اعتادها أكثر القوم في بيئتها، إذ العرف هو عادة الجمهور (ر: ف ١٠/٦٧ - ١١) ...
٨٩٨
Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. So the requirement of consistency or predominance in custom means the requirement of practical majority for it to be considered authoritative in incidents (1). For habit is called 'custom' when the majority of people practice it, even if they only follow it in a minority of their actions. If their practice of it and abandonment of it are equal, it is called 'shared custom', and it is not considered in people's transactions nor is it valid as a basis and evidence for reference in determining absolute rights and duties, because their occasional practice of it, if it serves as evidence of their intention to adopt it, then their abandonment of it on similar or more frequent occasions invalidates this indication. Hence, among the conditions for considering custom, both verbal and practical, is that it be consistent or predominant in this sense. Consistency or predominance does not entail that the custom be general, because generality of custom is different from its consistency: - General custom is that which is widespread in all countries. - Specific custom is that which is customary in a particular country or countries and not others, or among the people of a specific trade or industry and not others, as previously mentioned. Both general and specific custom require, for their consideration and application in absolute transactions, that they be consistent or predominant in their sphere over the actions of its people, because custom, even if general, may be predominant in people's actions in the explained sense or not. [Footnote]
Source pages p. 841

Commentary
This passage discusses a key condition for custom (ʿurf) to be legally recognized: it must be 'predominant' (ghālib) or 'consistent' (muṭṭarid). The author explains that 'predominance' here refers to a practical majority—meaning the custom is followed more often than not by those in its sphere. If people follow it only half the time, it becomes 'shared custom' (ʿurf mushtarak), which is not authoritative because their inconsistency undermines the indication of their intent. The text further distinguishes between generality of custom (ʿumūm) and predominance (iṭṭirād): a custom can be general (widespread) but not predominant if people do not consistently act upon it. Both general and specific customs must be predominant in their respective spheres. The footnote clarifies that the numerical majority is not a 'condition' (sharṭ) but a 'pillar' (rukn) of custom—without it, no custom exists at all, since an individual habit is not custom.

Key Terms
اطراد (iṭṭirād): Consistency in practice; the custom is followed regularly without significant interruption.
غلبة (ghalabah): Predominance; the custom is practiced more often than not by the relevant community.
عرف مشترك (ʿurf mushtarak): A custom that is equally practiced and abandoned; not authoritative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the status of a 'shared custom' (عرف مشترك)?
A) It is authoritative in absolute transactions.
B) It is not authoritative because practice and abandonment are equal.
C) It is authoritative only for general customs.
D) It is considered a pillar (rukn) of custom.

Key Takeaway
Custom is only authoritative when it is predominant or consistent in practice; a custom equally practiced and abandoned has no legal weight.

Distinguishing Generality from Predominance
Distinguishing between generality and predominance in custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the generality of custom (umum) and its predominance (ittidad), and explain why a custom can be general yet not predominant.

Core Idea
A custom may be widespread but not predominant—generality is not enough; it must be consistently acted upon.

The author emphasizes that custom's authority depends on its regularity, not its geographic or social reach. A general custom (uruf amm) that is not predominantly followed in practice is still not authoritative. This clarification prevents confusion between the two concepts and guides muftis in accurately assessing the binding force of custom.

Guided Path
1
Define general and specific custom
General custom (ʿurf ʿāmm) is widespread across all countries. Specific custom (ʿurf khāṣṣ) is limited to a certain area, trade, or industry. Both are subject to the same condition.
2
Recognize that predominance is a separate condition
The text explicitly states: 'generality of custom is different from its consistency (ittidad)'. A custom can be known everywhere (general) but not consistently practiced by the people.
Example: In all countries, people know the custom of tipping, but in some areas it is only done occasionally, so it is not predominant.
3
Understand the implication for muftis
When evaluating custom, a mufti must verify both the scope (general/specific) and the predominance within that scope. A general custom that is not predominant is invalid; a specific custom that is predominant is valid.
This prevents overreliance on surface-level familiarity without checking actual practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. So the requirement of consistency or predominance in custom means the requirement of practical majority for it to be considered authoritative in incidents (1). For habit is called 'custom' when the majority of people practice it, even if they only follow it in a minority of their actions. If their practice of it and abandonment of it are equal, it is called 'shared custom', and it is not considered in people's transactions nor is it valid as a basis and evidence for reference in determining absolute rights and duties, because their occasional practice of it, if it serves as evidence of their intention to adopt it, then their abandonment of it on similar or more frequent occasions invalidates this indication. غلبة (ghalabah): Predominance; the custom is practiced more often than not by the relevant community.
عموم العرف (ʿumūm al-ʿurf): Generality of custom; the custom is prevalent across a wide area.

Key Takeaway

Only Pre-Existing Custom Governs Transactions
Explaining the temporal condition for custom: only the custom existing at the time of the act is relevant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that only custom existing at the time of the transaction governs its interpretation and identify when a newly arising custom (al-ʿurf al-ṭāriʾ) cannot retroactively alter past acts.

Core Idea
Only the custom concurrent with the act governs it; later custom cannot rewrite the past.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's Madkhal details the second condition for custom to be legally operative: temporal concurrence. Custom only applies prospectively. Whether verbal or practical, the custom that existed when the transaction was created is the sole determinant of its meaning and legal effect. A later change in custom cannot retroactively reinterpret past declarations or contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the custom's time
Determine when the transaction was performed. The custom concurrent with that time is the only relevant one.
For verbal custom, the meaning of words at the time of utterance governs.
2
Distinguish verbal vs. practical custom
The rule applies to both types. For verbal custom, interpretation hinges on the custom at the time of speaking.
3
Apply to a case
Given a scenario, decide whether the old or new custom governs. Use the waqf example from the text as a model.
The phrase 'على الفريضة الشرعية' in an old endowment retains its original meaning of male double share even if custom later shifts to equality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (۷۰) شرائط اعتبار العرف
۳/۷۰ - الشريطة الثانية أن يكون العرف المراد تحكيمه في التصرفات قائماً عند إنشائها : العرف إنما يعمل فيما يوجد بعده لا فيما مضى قبله.
فالعرف الحاكم في أمر من الأمور بين الناس يجب أن يكون موجوداً عند وجود هذا الأمر ليصح حمله عليه. وهذا احتراز عن العرف الحادث، فإنه لا عبرة له بالنسبة إلى الماضي ولا يحكم فيه .
وهذه الشريطة أيضاً شاملة للعرف اللفظي والعملي:
أ - ففي العرف اللفظي رأينا أن كلام المتصرف بالإرادة المنفردة كالوقف والحلف والنذر والوصيَّة والطلاق وكذا كلام العاقد في العقود، إنما يحمل على معانيه العرفية دون معانيه اللغوية في أصل اللغة ولكن هذا العرف اللفظي الذي تحمل عليه معاني الكلام إنما هو العرف القائم حين صدور هذه التصرفات من أصحابها، لأنه هو الذي يعين مراد المتكلم. فإذا تغير العرف بعد ذلك في مفاهيم تلك الألفاظ والتراكيب الواردة في الوقفيات والوصايا وسائر الصكوك فلا عبرة للعرف الحادث في تفسير التصرفات القولية الواقعة في ظل العرف القديم، وإنما الذي يحمل على العرف اللفظي الجديد هو ما يصدر بعده من تصرفات.
فلفظ على الفريضة الشرعية مثلاً في كلام الواقفين والموصين معناه العرفي إلى اليوم أن يكون للذكر من غلة الوقف أو مال الوصية ضعف ما للأنثى. فلو تبدل العرف في هذا التعبير فأصبح معناه التساوي في الحصص بين الذكور والإناث لا يؤثر هذا التبدل في أحكام الوقفيات والوصايا السابقة الجارية على مقتضى العرف اللفظي الأول.
يقول العلامة ابن نجيم في الأشباه والنظائر الفن الأول ۱۳۳/۱):
العرف الذي تحمل عليه الألفاظ إنما هو المقارن السابق دون المتأخر. ولذلك قالوا: لا عبرة بالعرف الطارىء» ومثل ذلك يقال في نصوص الشارع أيضاً.
۸۹۹
Chapter (70) Conditions for Considering Custom. 3/70 - The second condition: that the custom intended to be applied in transactions must be existing at the time of their creation: Custom only operates on what occurs after it, not on what preceded it. So the custom governing a matter among people must exist at the time of that matter to be validly applied to it. This is a precaution against newly arising custom, which has no relevance to the past and does not govern it. This condition also includes verbal and practical custom: A - In verbal custom, we saw that the speech of the person acting alone, such as waqf, oath, vow, will, divorce, and likewise the speech of the contracting party in contracts, is interpreted according to its customary meanings rather than its linguistic meanings in the original language. But this verbal custom upon which the meanings of speech are interpreted is only the custom existing at the time these acts were performed by their owners, because it is what determines the speaker's intent. If the custom later changes in the meanings of those words and phrases occurring in endowments, wills, and other documents, the new custom has no effect in interpreting the verbal acts that occurred under the old custom; rather, the new verbal custom applies only to acts that occur after it. For example, the phrase 'according to the legal share' in the speech of endowers and testators has a customary meaning to this day that the male receives from the endowment income or will property twice the female's share. If the custom were to change in this expression so that its meaning became equal shares between males and females, this change does not affect the rulings of previous endowments and wills that proceeded according to the first verbal custom. The scholar Ibn Nujaym says in al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir (Art 1, 133/1): 'The custom upon which words are interpreted is only the preceding concurrent one, not the subsequent one. Therefore, they said: no heed is paid to newly arising custom.' And the same is said regarding the texts of the Lawgiver as well.
Source pages p. 842

Commentary
This passage establishes the second condition for custom to be legally operative: it must exist at the time of the act or transaction. Custom only applies prospectively, not retroactively. The author distinguishes between verbal and practical custom. For verbal custom, the meanings of words in declarations like waqf, wills, oaths, and contracts are determined by the custom prevailing at the time of utterance. A subsequent change in custom does not alter the interpretation of past acts. The example given is the phrase 'على الفريضة الشرعية' in endowments, which traditionally meant the male takes twice the female's share. Even if modern custom shifts to equal shares, the old endowments remain governed by the original custom. The author quotes Ibn Nujaym from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir confirming this principle: only the concurrent preceding custom is considered, not the later one. This also applies to interpreting the revealed texts (nusus al-shari'), where the custom at the time of revelation governs understanding, but that is a separate discussion.

Key Terms
العرف المقارن السابق (al-ʿurf al-muqārin al-sābiq): The custom that exists at the time of the act/transaction, concurrent with it.
العرف الطارئ (al-ʿurf al-ṭāriʾ): A custom that arises after the act; it has no bearing on past acts.
العرف الحادث (al-ʿurf al-ḥādith): Newly arising custom, synonymous with العرف الطارئ.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the text, what is the ruling on a waqf document that uses the phrase 'على الفريضة الشرعية' if the custom later changes to mean equal shares?
A) The waqf is reinterpreted according to the new custom.
B) The waqf remains governed by the old custom of double share for males.
C) The waqf becomes invalid due to ambiguity.
D) The waqf is adjusted to equal shares only if the endower is alive.

Key Takeaway
Only the custom concurrent with a transaction governs its interpretation; later custom has no effect on past acts.

Conflict of Custom with a Specific Text: The Six Ribawi Items
Internal Hanafi dispute on whether custom overrides a specific text regarding ribawi measures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to ANALYSE the Hanafi dispute on whether custom can override a specific legislative text in determining the measurement standard for ribawi items, and EVALUATE the tarjih between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A specific prophetic text prescribes weight for gold and silver, and volume for wheat, barley, dates, and salt — but custom may or may not override it.

This passage from Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's work addresses a critical jurisprudential question: when a specific text (nass) from the Prophet (peace be upon him) prescribes a particular measure for ribawi items, can later changes in custom alter that measure? The Hanafi school is internally divided between the majority view of Abu Hanifa and the dissenting opinion of his disciple Abu Yusuf. Understanding this dispute illuminates the interaction between text and custom in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
What is the default principle for equality in ribawi transactions? Custom determines whether weight or volume is used.
The general rule is that equality is by customary measure.
2
Locate the specific text
What did the Prophet specify for the six items? Gold and silver by weight; wheat, barley, dates, salt by volume.
The text is a specific ruling for these six items.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position
The specific text is fixed and overrides custom permanently.
Majority view: text is absolute and unchanging.
4
State Abu Yusuf's position
The text was based on prevalent custom; if custom changes, the ruling changes accordingly.
Minority view: text is conditioned on custom.
5
Analyse the evidence and tarjih
Compare the proofs: Abu Hanifa relies on the text as a fixed ruling; Abu Yusuf argues it is reasoned by custom. The author (al-Zarqa) favours Abu Yusuf's view as stronger, but the majority follows Abu Hanifa.
Tarjih involves weighing the strength of the specific text vs the rationale behind it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (۷۱) اصطدام العرف بنص تشريعي خاص
بيع مقدار منها بأكثر أو أقل منه إذا كانا من جنس واحد، لأن الفضل عندئذ يكون ربا محرّماً، فلا تجوز مبادلة القمح بالقمح مثلاً، ولا الذهب بالذهب، إلا إذا تساوى البدلان.
فإذا اختلف الجنسان كبيع الذهب بالفضة أو القمح بالشعير، جاز التفاضل ولكن تمتنع النسيئة، أي تأجيل أحد البدلين، فيجب التقابض في مجلس العقد (۱).
وهذا من قبيل سد الذرائع، كيلا يتخذ جواز التفاضل عند اختلاف الجنسين ذريعة ووسيلة إلى ربا النسيئة فيستقرض الشخص ذهباً مثلاً إلى أجل ثم يوفي فضة أكثر منه بقدر الربا المراد.
فهذا الحكم كما ترى يقوم على أساس وجوب تساوي الكميات المبادلة في الجنس الواحد فما هو مقياس هذا التساوي، فقد يتساوى المقداران وزناً ويتفاوتان كيلاً، وبالعكس؟
ومن المتفق عليه أنَّ التساوي يُعتبر شرعاً بالمقياس العرفي في كل صنف، فما كان وزنياً ،عرفاً كالزيت والسمن يجب تساوي الكميتين فيه بالوزن وما كان كيلياً عرفاً يجب التساوي فيه بالكيل.
وإذا تبدل العرف في مقياس شيء فأصبح كيلياً مثلاً بعد أن كان وزنياً، أو أصبح وزنياً بعد أن كان كيلياً تبدل المقياس الربوي فيه تبعاً للعرف .
غير أنه قد ورد النص عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في ستة أصناف من الأموال الربوية (ومنها القمح) أنه يجب التساوي في بعضها بالوزن، وفي بعضها بالكيل (۲)
(۱) يلحظ هنا أن هذه الأحكام الربوية أصبحت غير معمول بها في ظل القوانين الزمنية السائدة في بلادنا وكثير من البلاد العربية والإسلامية فيتبايع الناس اليوم بالأموال الربوية كيفما شاؤوا بمقتضى الأحكام القانونية .
(۲) هي الذهب والفضة والحنطة والشعير والملح والتمر.
فذهب جمهور المجتهدين ومنهم أبو حنيفة إلى أنه يعتبر في خصوص هذه الأشياء المقياس الذي ورد النص به أبداً ولا عبرة لتبدل العرف فيه .. ففي مبادلة القمح مثلاً يجب التساوي كيلاً لا وزناً ولو تعارف الناس بيعه بالوزن كما في زماننا اليوم (۱) ، لأنَّ هذا نصّ خاص بالمقياس الربوي هذه الأصناف فلا يعتبر العرف على خلافه .
ولكن ذهب أبو يوسف صاحب أبي حنيفة إلى أن المقياس المعتبر حتى في تلك الأشياء الستة هو المقياس العرفي، وأنه يتبدل بتبدل العرف، كما في سائر الأموال الربوية الأخرى التي لم يرد نص خاص بشأن مقياسها ويقول : إنَّ النص في مقياس تلك الأصناف الستة معلل بالعرف، فإنما وَرَدَ النص بلزوم التساوي فيها كيلاً أو وزناً لأن هذا مقياسها المتعارف في عهد النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام . فلو كان العرف فيها على مقياس آخر لورد النص معتبراً فيها ذلك المقياس الآخر (ر ) الهداية وشروحها ١٥٧/٦).
ورأي أبي يوسف هذا كما ترى أقوى حجة، وأقوم محجة، رغم انفراده به .
(۱) ليس معنى ذلك أن يمتنع شرعاً على هذا الرأي بيع هذه الأصناف بغير المقياس المنصوص إذا قوبلت بغير جنسها كالنقود مثلاً، بل للناس أن يتعارفوا في بيعها بخلاف جنسها أي مقياس أرادوا أو بلا مقياس أي جزافاً، ولكن معناه أنه إذا شيء منها بجنسه كقمح بقمح، أو استقرض منها شيء، فإنه يجب عندئذ في الوفاء التساوي بالمقياس الذي ورد به النص.
Chapter (71): Conflict of Custom with a Specific Legislative Text. Selling a quantity of it for more or less if they are of the same genus, because the excess then would be prohibited riba. So it is not permissible to exchange wheat for wheat, for example, nor gold for gold, unless the two counter-values are equal. If the two genera differ, like selling gold for silver or wheat for barley, disparity is allowed but postponement (nasi'ah) is prohibited, i.e., deferring one of the two counter-values, so hand-to-hand exchange is required in the contract session (1). This is a form of blocking the means (sadd al-dhara'i'), so that the permissibility of disparity when the genera differ is not taken as a means to riba al-nasi'ah, whereby a person would borrow gold, for example, on credit and then repay more silver in the amount of the desired riba. This ruling, as you see, is based on the obligation of equality of quantities exchanged in the same genus. So what is the measure of this equality? For the two quantities may be equal in weight but differ in volume, or vice versa. It is agreed upon that equality is considered legally by the customary measure in each type. So whatever is weighty by custom, like oil and ghee, equality of the two quantities is required by weight; and whatever is volumetric by custom, equality is required by volume. And if custom changes in the measure of something, so it becomes volumetric, for example, after it was weighty, or becomes weighty after it was volumetric, the ribawi measure changes according to custom. However, a text came from the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding six types of ribawi wealth (including wheat) that equality is required in some by weight and in some by volume (2). (1) Note here that these ribawi rulings are no longer practiced under the temporal laws prevailing in our country and many Arab and Islamic countries; people today trade in ribawi wealth however they wish according to legal provisions. (2) They are: gold, silver, wheat, barley, salt, and dates. The majority of mujtahids, including Abu Hanifa, held that for these specific items, the measure prescribed by the text is always considered and there is no regard for change of custom therein. So in exchanging wheat, for example, equality is required by volume, not by weight, even if people customarily sell it by weight as in our time today (1), because this is a specific text regarding the ribawi measure for these items, so custom contrary to it is not considered. But Abu Yusuf, the companion of Abu Hanifa, held that the considered measure even for those six items is the customary measure, and it changes with change of custom, as in all other ribawi wealth for which no specific text came regarding their measure. He says: The text regarding the measure of those six items is reasoned by custom; the text came requiring equality in them by volume or weight only because this was their customary measure in the era of the Prophet (peace be upon him). So if custom had been otherwise, the text would have come considering that other measure (see al-Hidayah and its commentaries 6/157). And this opinion of Abu Yusuf, as you see, is stronger in evidence and more correct in path, despite his being alone in it. (1) This does not mean that according to this opinion selling these items by a measure other than the prescribed one is forbidden if they are exchanged for a different genus, like currency, for example; rather people may customarily sell them for a different genus by any measure they wish or without measure i.e., lump sum. But it means that if something is sold for its own genus, like wheat for wheat, or something is borrowed of it, then at repayment equality is required by the measure prescribed by the text.
Source pages p. 852

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of a conflict between custom and a specific legislative text in Hanafi jurisprudence. The issue concerns the measurement standard for ribawi (interest-ridden) items in barter transactions. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, first establishes the general rule: equality in weight or volume is determined by custom. If customs change, the measure changes accordingly. However, there is a special text from the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding six specific items (gold, silver, wheat, barley, salt, dates) that prescribes specific measures (weight for gold and silver, volume for the others). The majority of mujtahids, including Imam Abu Hanifa, hold that this text is fixed and custom cannot override it. Thus, in exchanging wheat for wheat, equality must be by volume even if people normally sell by weight. Imam Abu Yusuf, however, argues that the text itself was based on the custom of the Prophet's time; if custom changes, the ruling changes accordingly. Al-Zarqa indicates that Abu Yusuf's opinion is stronger in evidence, despite being a minority within the Hanafi school. The passage includes a footnote clarifying that the ruling only applies when the items are exchanged for the same genus; if exchanged for a different genus, custom may be followed. The author also notes that these ribawi rules are no longer practiced under modern positive laws in many Muslim countries. This is a classic example of how custom interacts with textual rulings, and it demonstrates the internal diversity within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Common practice or custom that is considered in legal rulings, especially in determining measurement standards for ribawi items.
النص التشريعي الخاص (al-nass al-tashri'i al-khass): A specific legislative text from the Quran or Sunnah that provides a particular ruling, here the hadith specifying measures for six ribawi items.
المقياس الربوي (al-miqyas al-ribawi): The measurement standard (weight or volume) used to determine equality in ribawi transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of the majority of Hanafi scholars regarding the measurement standard for the six ribawi items?
A) It changes with custom.
B) It is fixed by the text.
C) It is determined by the local marketplace.
D) It is only applicable in the Arabian Peninsula.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on whether custom can override a specific text on ribawi measures, with the majority following Abu Hanifa's fixed view, but a powerful minority (Abu Yusuf) arguing the text was custom-based and thus changeable.

General vs. Specific Custom in Specifying General Texts
Analyzing the authority of custom in specifying general texts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida 'al-'urf al-'amm yukhassis al-nass al-'amm' to distinguish when a custom may specify a general text and when it may not, and evaluate the Maliki position.

Core Idea
Only general custom, not specific, can specify a general text; doubt does not remove certainty.

In this passage, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa explains a critical principle in the Hanafi theory of custom: when a general text (nass 'amm) conflicts with a customary practice, the custom's scope determines its legal authority. General custom (al-'urf al-'amm) can specify the text, as seen in the istisna contract. However, specific custom (al-'urf al-khas) cannot, because its absence elsewhere creates doubt about the legislator's intent. This distinction is essential for muftis encountering local customs that appear to contradict clear texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Text and Its Ruling
Locate the general prohibition (e.g., 'do not sell what you do not possess') and its scope.
The text is a general amr or nahi that covers many cases.
2
Assess the Opposing Custom: General or Specific?
Determine whether the custom is widespread (general) or confined to a place/group (specific). The passage gives istisna as a general custom, and local merchant customs as specific.
General = practiced by all people in all lands; specific = limited to a region or trade.
3
Apply the Hanafi Qawida
If the custom is general, it specifies the general text (the text is understood as excluding the customary practice). If specific, it does not specify; the general text remains in force.
The maxim: 'al-'urf al-'amm yukhassis al-nass al-'amm' and its dabt: 'al-'urf al-khas la yukhassis al-nass al-'amm'.
4
Examine the Fur': Istisna Contract
Istisna falls under the general prohibition of selling non-existent items but is validated by universal custom. This illustrates the rule.
The Hanafi school relies on consensus (ijma) and custom to validate istisna.
5
Contrast the Maliki Position
Note that the Maliki school (according to verified view) allows custom to specify a general text regardless of generality, but the Hanafi school insists on generality.
This difference affects fatwa in jurisdictions where Maliki opinion is followed.

Source Text

فهذا النص عام في منع كل أنواع البيع التي لا يكون المبيع فيها موجوداً في ملك البائع، سوى السلم الذي استثناه لما فيه من مصلحة باستسلاف الثمن للاستعانة على الإنتاج.
فعقد الاستصناع يشمله بالمنع عموم النص المانع، وإن لم يكن وارداً فيه خصيصاً.
ولكن الاستصناع عقد قد تعارفه جميع الناس في كل البلاد لاحتياجهم إلى طريقته ولاسيما في الأحذية ونحوها مما فيه مقاييس وأوصاف يختلف فيها الشخص عن غيره

الفصل (۷۲) العرف المقارن لنص عام يعارضه
لأنه إذا كان عرف بعض البلاد أو الناس يقتضي تخصيص النص، فإن عدم هذا العرف لدى بقية الأماكن أو الناس لا يقتضيه فلا يثبت التخصيص بالشك (١).
(۱)
وقد رأينا آنفاً أن تخصيص النص التشريعي العام هو بيان لمراد الشارع منه لا تعديل طارىء عليه ( ر ) ف (٣/٧٢ الحاشية، والعرف الخاص قد يكون بين فئة ضيئلة من الناس أو في مكان صغير، فلا يصلح دليلاً على أن الشارع لم يرد من نصه العام عموم معناه .

Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom
This text is general in prohibiting all types of sale where the sold item is not present in the seller's ownership, except salam which was exempted due to the benefit of receiving the price in advance to aid production.
Thus the contract of istisna is included in the prohibition by the generality of the prohibiting text, even though it is not specifically mentioned therein.
But istisna is a contract that has become customary among all people in all countries due to their need for its method, especially in shoes and the like where measurements and descriptions differ from person to person.
Therefore, ijtihad affirmed the validity of the istisna contract due to the prevailing custom, and considered this custom as specifying the general prohibiting text, as if the text implicitly exempted istisna just as it explicitly exempted salam. And practice remained on the text in other types of sale of non-existent items. Consensus on the validity of istisna has been reported.
4/72 - b - Specific Opposing Custom: As for when the existing custom opposing the general text is a specific custom confined to a place or a group of people, such as the custom of merchants or craftsmen in some countries or some categories, the preponderant opinion in Hanafi ijtihad is that it does not have the strength and authority of general custom when opposing the text. So specific custom is not valid to specify the opposing general text, even if it existed at the time of the text's revelation.
Hadith of Hakim b. Hizam: Regarding the license in salam, the statement of al-Zayla'i in Nasb al-Raya indicates that it was not narrated with this wording. Rather, it is derived from general hadiths about salam. (1) It is noted here that the sale of non-existent items in general has become considered valid on condition that its existence is not impossible, according to paragraph 2 of Article 64 of the former Ottoman Code of Civil Procedure, and then according to the Syrian Civil Code.
916
Chapter 72: Custom Opposing a General Text
Because if the custom of some countries or people requires specification of the text, then the absence of this custom in other places or people does not require it, so specification is not established by doubt. (1)
(1) We have already seen that specification of a general legislative text is an explanation of the legislator's intent, not a subsequent amendment (see footnote 3/72). And specific custom may be among a small group of people or in a small place, so it is not valid as evidence that the legislator did not intend the general meaning of the general text.
5/72 - This is the precise statement of the Hanafi ijtihad on this point.
In the Maliki school, there is disagreement among its jurists regarding considering practical custom as specifying the generality of a text. But the verified jurists of the school hold that existing practical custom specifies the general text just as it restricts the absolute text, contrary to what al-Qarafi said in his Furouq (1/173, al-Farq 28) that practical custom is not suitable for specifying or restricting the text.
Similarly, they do not explicitly distinguish between whether the custom is general or specific. And they... (see footnote of Shams al-Din al-Dasuqi on al-Sharh al-Kabir 2/143). And among the branches of this principle with Imam Malik is that he says: if a woman is of noble status, she is not obliged to breastfeed her child if he accepts the breast of another, due to the customary benefit in that, as the custom among such women is to hire wet nurses for their children.
(1) Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir by Ibn Nujaym, end of the sixth principle, 1/134, and the treatise 'Nashr al-Urf' by Ibn Abidin, first chapter, 2/116 from his collection of treatises.
(2) The esteemed professor Ahmad Fahmi Abu Sunna has elaborated on the investigation of the Hanafi and Maliki schools and others on the issue of specifying general legislative text by practical custom in the fifth and seventh articles of his thesis 'al-Urf wa al-'Adah', pp. 91-94 and 124.
Source pages p. 858

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Am' discusses the theory of custom ('urf) in Islamic legal theory, specifically focusing on when custom opposes a general legislative text (nass 'amm). The author first illustrates with the example of the istisna contract (manufacturing contract). The general prophetic prohibition on selling what one does not possess includes istisna, which involves selling an item not yet in existence. However, due to universal customary practice (al-'urf al-'amm), the Hanafi school validates istisna, treating this custom as a mukhassis (specifier) of the general text. The specification is implicit, akin to the explicit exception of salam. This shows that general custom can specify a general text.

Then the author distinguishes specific custom (al-'urf al-khas) that is limited to a particular place or group. In the Hanafi school, such specific custom does not have the authority to specify a general text, even if it existed at the time of revelation. The reason is that if custom is not universal, its absence elsewhere creates doubt (shakk) about the legislator's intent, and specification by doubt is not allowed. The passage also references a hadith about Hakim b. Hizam regarding salam, clarifying that the license for salam is based on general hadiths, not a specific narration.

Later, the author compares the Maliki school, noting that the verified Maliki position holds that practical custom can specify a general text and restrict an absolute text, regardless of whether it is general or specific, contrary to al-Qarafi's view. An example from Imam Malik: a noble woman is not obliged to breastfeed her child if custom allows hiring a wet nurse, showing the influence of custom.

The passage is a detailed exposition of a key usul al-fiqh principle: custom can specify general texts, but the Hanafi school requires the custom to be general ('amm) to exert such influence.

Key Terms
العرف العام (al-'urf al-'amm): Custom that is widespread among all people or in most regions, not limited to a specific place or group.
العرف الخاص (al-'urf al-khas): Custom confined to a particular locality or a specific group, e.g., merchants in one city.
تخصيص النص العام (takhsis al-nass al-'amm): Limiting the scope of a general textual ruling by excluding some of its otherwise included cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in this passage, when can a custom (urf) specify a general textual (nass 'amm) prohibition?
A) Only if the custom existed at the time of revelation and is general.
B) Only if the custom is specific to a particular trade.
C) Only if the custom is mentioned explicitly in the text.
D) Only if the custom is adopted by the majority of jurists.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, only general custom (al-'urf al-'amm) can specify a general text; specific custom (al-'urf al-khas) lacks the authority due to the risk of doubt.

The Two Cases Where New Custom Overrides a General Text
Explains two conditions for a new custom to override a general text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two cases where a new custom overrides a general text and apply them to the example of conditions in sales, identifying the role of 'illa.

Core Idea
A new custom overrides a text only when the text was built on the old custom or when the text's 'illa is negated.

This lesson examines a critical principle in Hanafi iftaa: when a newly emerged custom contradicts the apparent meaning of a general text from the Qur'an or Sunnah. The Hanafi school does not allow arbitrary override; rather, two specific conditions must be met. Understanding these conditions is essential for fatwa issuance in changing circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Read the passage and list the two situations where a new custom can override a general text.
Focus on the author's explicit enumeration.
2
Understand the 'illa mechanism
In the second case, recognize that the ruling changes because the underlying reason ('illa) of the text is negated by the new custom.
The ruling revolves around its 'illa; when the 'illa disappears, the ruling disappears.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Note that Imam Abu Yusuf holds a unique view on the first case, which the author considers the strongest position.
The majority (jumhur) disagree with Abu Yusuf on this point.
4
Apply to the sales condition example
Connect the general principle to the specific Hanafi example: customary conditions in sales become valid despite the hadith's apparent prohibition.
The 'illa of preventing dispute is negated by custom.

الفصل (۷۳) العرف الحادث بعد نص عام يعارضه
يدلُّ دلالة واضحة على أنَّ العرف الحادث، ولو خالف ظاهر النص التشريعي، يُعتبر ويُحترم في حالتين :
١ - إذا كان النص التشريعي نفسه معللاً بالعُرف، أي مبنياً على عرف عملي قائم عند وروده، فعندئذ إذا تبدل ذلك العرف يتبدل تبعاً له حكم النص، ولو كان النص خاصاً بالموضوع وهذ الرأي انفرد به أبو يوسف أصحاب أبي حنيفة، وهو الرأي الأقوى مدركاً وحجة وإن كان الجمهور من على خلافه .
•
وهذه الحالة قد تقدَّم بيانها وأمثلتها في البحث عن اصطدام العرف بالنص الخاص فليرجع إليه (ف۷/۷۱ - ۸).
۲ - إذا كان النص التشريعي معللاً بعلة ينفيها العرف الحادث، سواء أكانت علة النص مصرحاً بها فيه أو مستنبطة استنباطاً بطريق الاجتهاد.
ففي مثل هذه الحال يعتبر العرف الحادث ويحترم وإن خالف النص لأنَّ هذه المخالفة تصبح ظاهرية غير حقيقية ما دامت علة النص تنتفي بوجود العرف، إذ من المقرّر في قواعد الأصول أن الحكم الشرعي يدور علته فيثبت عند ثبوتها، وينتفي بانتفائها .
٥/٧٣ - الشواهد الفقهية على هذا المبدأ:
وهناك شواهد فقهية عديدة على هذا المبدأ في اعتبار العرف الحادث نورد بعضها فيما يلي:
۷۳/٦ الشاهد الأول :
كالبيع،
يذكر فقهاء الحنفية في بحث الشرط المفسد في عقود المعاوضات أن من الشرط المفسد لعقد المعاوضة كل شرط تشترط فيه منفعة خارجة عن الحكم الأصلي للعقد وعما يلائمه كشرط المشتري على البائع طحن الحنطة المشتراة، أو حمل البضاعة على حسابه، أو إصلاح الآلة المبيعة إذا تعطلت عند المشتري، أو اشتراط البائع أن يستعمل المبيع مدة بعد البيع، فكل ذلك في أصل نظريتهم شروط مفسدة.
وقد أخذ الحنفية في ذلك بظاهر ما روي عن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام أنه قد نهى عن بيع وشرط كما تقدم بيانه في بحث سلطان الإرادة العقدية من نظرية العقود (ف ٧/٤١ و ٣/٤٢ - ٤) والهداية وشروحها ٧٦/٦ - ٧٨

١- الشرط الذي ورد بجوازه نص شرعي، كشرط الخيار (ر: ف ٣/ ۷ - المثال (۹).
۲ - الشرط الذي تعارف الناس اشتراطه .

أنه
ومن فروع هذا النظر تصحيحهم بيع الوفاء لجريان العرف به، رغم في الأصل يقوم على أساس اشتراط مفسد عندهم، وقد أفتوا أولاً بفساده قبل أن يفشو فيه العرف (ر) ف ٩/١٦ و ٨/٤٦).
۷/۷۳ - ومستند الفقهاء في ذلك هو النظر إلى علة الحديث النبوي الذي نص على منع الشرط في البيع، فقد اعتبروا أن الغرض التشريعي منه هو منع سبب المنازعة، لأنَّ هذه الشروط الزائدة على أصل عقد البيع يفضي تنفيذها وكيفيته إلى النزاع غالباً، فرأوا أن العرف إذا جرى على بعضها ينفي النزاع إذ يجعل الأمر معلوماً مألوفاً، فلا يكون العرف قاضياً
Chapter (73): The new custom after a general text that contradicts it. It clearly indicates that the new custom, even if it contradicts the apparent meaning of the legislative text, is considered and respected in two cases:
1- If the legislative text itself is qualified by custom, i.e., built upon an existing practical custom at the time of its occurrence. Then, if that custom changes, the ruling of the text changes accordingly, even if the text is specific to the subject. This opinion is unique to Abu Yusuf among the companions of Abu Hanifa, and it is the strongest opinion in terms of basis and proof, even though the majority are opposed to it.
•
This case has been explained earlier with its examples in the discussion of the collision of custom with a specific text, so refer to it (f 7/71-8).
2- If the legislative text is qualified by a reason (illa) that the new custom negates, whether the reason of the text is explicitly stated in it or derived through ijtihad.
In such a case, the new custom is considered and respected even if it contradicts the text, because this contradiction becomes apparent and not real as long as the reason of the text is negated by the existence of the custom. For it is established in the principles of usul that the legal ruling revolves around its reason; it is established when the reason is established, and negated when it is negated.
5/73 - Juristic examples of this principle:
There are many juristic examples of this principle in considering the new custom; we will present some of them below:
73/6 First example:
Like in sales.
The Hanafi jurists mention in the discussion of the invalidating condition (shart mufsid) in commutative contracts that among the invalidating conditions for a commutative contract is every condition in which a benefit is stipulated that is outside the original ruling of the contract and what is compatible with it, such as the buyer's condition on the seller to grind the purchased wheat, or to transport the goods at his expense, or to repair the sold machine if it breaks down with the buyer, or the seller's condition to use the sold item for a period after the sale. All of this, in their original theory, are invalidating conditions.
The Hanafis adopted in that the apparent meaning of what was narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he prohibited a sale and a condition, as previously explained in the discussion of the authority of contractual will in the theory of contracts (f 7/41 and 3/42-4) and al-Hidayah and its commentaries (76/6-78).
They made an exception from this text for two types of contractual conditions, judging them to be valid and binding if stipulated in the contract:
1- A condition for which a legal text permits it, such as the condition of option (khiyar) (see f 3/7, example 9).
2- A condition that people have customarily stipulated.
Thus, conditions that are considered invalidating by the Hanafis according to their interpretation of this text from the Sunnah, if some of them become customary, then by custom they become valid, binding, and legally deserving of respect, even if the custom regarding them is new.
And among the branches of this view is their validation of Bay' al-Wafa (sale with repurchase) due to the prevalence of custom, even though in origin it is based on an invalidating condition according to them, and they initially gave fatwa of its invalidity before the custom became widespread in it (see f 9/16 and 8/46).
7/73 - The basis of the jurists in that is looking at the reason ('illa) of the Prophetic hadith that prohibits the condition in sale. They considered that the legislative purpose of it is to prevent the cause of dispute, because the execution and modality of these additional conditions to the original sale contract usually lead to dispute. So they saw that if custom prevails regarding some of them, it negates the dispute by making the matter known and familiar, so that the custom is not abrogating...
Source pages p. 865

Commentary
This passage presents a key principle in Hanafi usul al-iftaa: the interaction between new custom ('urf hadith) and a general textual ruling (nass 'amm). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, states that a new custom can override a text in two specific situations. First, if the text itself was originally based on a pre-existing custom (e.g., a ruling that assumed a certain practice), then when that custom changes, the ruling changes too—even if the text is specific in wording. This view is attributed solely to Imam Abu Yusuf among the Hanafis, and al-Zarqa considers it the strongest position. Second, if the text has an underlying reason ('illa) that the new custom negates, then the ruling falls away because Hanafi usul holds that rulings revolve with their 'illa: they exist when the 'illa exists and vanish when it disappears.

The second case is then illustrated with a detailed example from Hanafi fiqh: conditions in sales (shurut mufsida). Originally, the Hanafis held that any condition in a sale contract that provides an extra benefit not inherent in the contract is invalidating, based on the hadith 'La bay' wa shart' (no sale and condition). However, they made an exception for two types of conditions: those explicitly permitted by another text (like the option of khiyar), and those that have become customary among people. The rationale is that the 'illa (reason) of the prohibition is to prevent disputes; if a condition becomes customary, it is well-known and unlikely to cause conflict, so the 'illa is negated, and the condition becomes valid. A concrete application is the validation of Bay' al-Wafa (a sale with a promise of repurchase) after it became widespread in custom, despite initially being considered invalid. This demonstrates how the Hanafi madhhab dynamically applies the principle that custom can override a text when the latter's 'illa is absent.

Key Terms
العرف الحادث (al-'urf al-hadith): A custom that emerges after the revelation of a legal text and contradicts its apparent meaning
النص التشريعي (al-nass al-tashri'i): A legislative text from Quran or Sunnah that provides a ruling
الشرط المفسد (al-shart al-mufsid): A condition that invalidates a contract according to original Hanafi theory

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for Hanafis validating customary conditions in sales?
A) The hadith is weak and not relied upon.
B) The custom negates the 'illa of dispute, which is the purpose of the prohibition.
C) The majority of scholars agree that all conditions are valid.
D) The Quran explicitly permits conditions in sales.

Key Takeaway
A new custom overrides a general text only when the text was built on that custom or when the custom negates the text's 'illa.

Custom Validating Conditions Through Specification by Cause
Explaining how custom can validate conditions prohibited by a general text through specification by cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida that a later custom which negates the cause of a textual ruling constitutes specification of that text by its cause, and identify the conditions under which a corrupting condition becomes valid through custom.

Core Idea
Custom can validate a corrupting condition if it removes the cause of the prohibition, not by abrogating the text but by specifying its application.

The Hanafis recognized that some contractual conditions, though generally prohibited because they lead to dispute, become valid and binding if they become customary. This lesson explores the legal reasoning behind this principle, focusing on the qawida that custom specifying by cause (takhsis bi al-illah) allows flexibility while preserving the authority of the prophetic text. We will examine the exceptions, the role of the cause (illah), and the distinction from abrogation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Text and Its Prohibition
The prophetic hadith forbids a sale with a condition. This is a general prohibition based on the cause (illah) of preventing dispute. Identify the two exceptions the Hanafis carved out: conditions permitted by a specific text and conditions that become customary.
The text is general (amm) but not absolute; its application depends on the presence of its cause.
2
Understand the Concept of Corrupting Condition (shart mufsid)
A corrupting condition is one that is not sanctioned by a specific text or custom and leads to uncertainty or dispute. Use the legal_definition_dissector to break down its components: hukm, mahall, sabab, shart, mani, athar.
The sabab (cause) is potential dispute; the shart (condition for validity) is custom or specific text.
3
Apply the Qawida: Custom that Negates the Cause
The qawida states: 'A custom that arises later which negates the cause of a textual ruling is considered a specification of that text by its cause.' This means that when custom makes a condition well-known and dispute-free, the cause of the prohibition disappears, and the text no longer applies to that condition.
This is not abrogation (naskh) but specification (takhsis) by cause – the text was always intended to apply only where its cause exists.
4
Distinguish from Specification by Custom and Identify Istithna
The passage clarifies that this is not takhsis of a general text by a later custom (which is invalid because the specifier must be contemporaneous), but rather takhsis by the cause. Also note the istithna: if there is a specific text (nass khass) prohibiting a particular condition by name, custom cannot override it.
This nuance preserves the integrity of the text while allowing practical adaptation.
5
Apply to Furuat: Bay' al-Wafa and Currency Differences
Work through the two examples given: (1) bay' al-wafa (sale with repurchase right) – initially invalid, later validated after custom spread; (2) differences in weight of dinar varieties – custom can determine exchange rates if no specific prohibition exists. For each, identify how custom removes the cause of dispute.
These illustrate the qawida in action and show that only specific custom (al-'urf al-khass) is needed, not universal custom.
