Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. If the sold item becomes defective with the buyer, or the seller stipulates that he use the sold item for a period after sale, all of that in their original theory are corrupting conditions. The Hanafis followed the apparent meaning of what was narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he forbade a sale with a condition, as previously explained in the discussion of the authority of contractual will from the theory of contracts (f 7/41 and 3/42-4) and al-Hidayah and its commentaries 6/76-78. They excepted from this text two types of contractual conditions, ruling them valid and binding if stipulated in the contract: 1- The condition that is permitted by a textual evidence, like the condition of option (see f 3/7 - example 9). 2- The condition that people customarily stipulate. So conditions that are considered corrupting by the Hanafis according to their interpretation of this prophetic text, if something of them becomes customary, it becomes by custom valid, binding, and legally obligatory to respect, even if the custom is new. Among the branches of this view is their validation of bay' al-wafa (sale with right of repurchase) due to the prevalence of custom in it, although in origin it is based on a corrupting condition according to them, and they initially gave fatwa of its invalidity before custom spread in it (see f 16/9 and 46/8). 7/73 - The jurists' basis for that is consideration of the cause of the prophetic hadith that stipulates the prohibition of conditions in sale. They considered that its legislative purpose is to prevent the cause of dispute, because these conditions additional to the original sale contract often lead to dispute in their execution and manner. They saw that if custom prevails regarding some of them, it removes dispute because it makes the matter known and familiar. Thus custom does not override the text but rather agrees with its purpose and spirit, even if the custom is new (1) (see al-'Inayah sharh al-Hidayah, chapter of corrupt sale, 6/76-77). They did not require for the validity of the customary condition that the custom be general; rather, specific custom suffices. For possible conditions are countless types, varying by time and place according to people's needs. Conditions customary in one country may not be customary elsewhere, so it is valid for each people to stipulate in their sales and other transactions what they customarily stipulate, unless there is a specific text that prohibits a particular condition. In that case, no custom contrary to it is considered, as is known from the previous discussion of the conflict of custom with a specific text. See also al-'Inayah and Fath al-Qadir in the same place, and Radd al-Muhtar (4/121-123). 8/73 - The second evidence: In the issue of the difference in weight of dinars, the verifying scholar Ibn 'Abidin in his treatise 'Nashr al-'Urf' decided that when there are multiple varieties of a single type of currency, and... (1) It becomes clear from this that considering a new custom that apparently contradicts the generality of a general legislative text is not a case of specifying the general text with a new custom, because among the conditions of specifying a general legislative text is that its specification evidence must be contemporaneous with its existence, as previously stated. Specification is an interpretation of the Lawgiver's intent from His text since its revelation. So it cannot be considered that the general text effective in its generality was specified from its revelation by a custom that would occur later and might not occur. Rather, considering the new custom that removes the cause of the text is a result of considering the text as specified by its cause. So it is specification by the cause, not by the new custom. The text prohibiting the condition in sale, after ijtihad determined the cause of prohibition in it, is considered from its revelation as specified and confined to cases where its cause is realized, excluding other cases where that cause is absent. Among these cases where the cause of this text is absent is when the condition becomes customary. So here the specification is in reality specification by the cause of the text; and considering the new custom, if it negates that cause, is merely an application of that previously-considered specification.
Source pages p. 866

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm discusses the Hanafi theory of custom ('urf) and its role in validating contractual conditions that would otherwise be considered corrupting (mufsidah). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by stating that certain conditions, such as the buyer's use of the sold item after purchase, are originally deemed corrupting according to the Hanafi school. This is based on the prophetic prohibition of a sale with a condition, as recorded in hadith. However, the Hanafis made two exceptions: conditions explicitly permitted by a specific text (e.g., the option of stipulation) and conditions that have become customary among people. The key point is that custom can transform a corrupting condition into a valid and binding one, even if the custom arises later. As an example, the passage cites bay' al-wafa (a sale with the right of repurchase), which was initially declared invalid but later validated due to custom. The rationale is that the underlying cause (illah) of the prohibition is the potential for dispute. When a condition becomes customary, it becomes well-known and familiar, thereby eliminating the cause of dispute. Thus, custom does not override the text but rather aligns with its purpose. The passage further clarifies that only a specific custom (al-'urf al-khass) is sufficient; it does not need to be universal. However, if there is a specific text prohibiting a particular condition, custom cannot override it. The footnote distinguishes between specification (takhsis) of a general text by a later custom (which is invalid) and specification by the cause (which is valid). The latter means that the text is understood from the beginning to apply only where its cause exists; when custom negates the cause, the text simply does not apply. This is a sophisticated usul al-fiqh argument that preserves the authority of the text while allowing for practical adaptation.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Custom; the prevailing practice among people in transactions, which can affect legal rulings in the absence of a specific text.
الشرط المفسد (al-shart al-mufsid): A condition that is considered corrupting because it leads to dispute or is contrary to the nature of the contract, but can become valid through custom.
علة النص (illat al-nass): The underlying reason or cause that justifies a textual ruling; here, the cause of prohibiting conditions in sales is preventing dispute.
تخصيص بالعلة (takhsis bi al-'illah): Specification of a general text by its cause, meaning the text applies only when the cause is present; if the cause is absent due to custom, the text does not apply.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi theory of custom discussed in this passage, what was the initial fatwa regarding bay' al-wafa (sale with right of repurchase) before it became customary?
A) It was considered valid because it is permitted by a specific text.
B) It was considered invalid because it contained a corrupting condition.
C) It was considered valid because of general custom.
D) It was considered invalid because it involved riba.

Key Takeaway
Custom can validate a corrupting condition if it eliminates the cause of dispute, through specification by cause, not abrogation.

8 min
The Later Custom (Al-Urf al-Hadith) and Its Legal Weight
Analysing when a later custom can override a general textual ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi condition that a later custom must negate the illa of a general text to be legally valid, and distinguish this from the view that general custom can specify any general text.

Core Idea
A later custom can only override a general text if it negates the underlying cause; otherwise it is abrogation, not specification.

This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm examines a delicate usul al-ifta issue: when a custom arises after a general legal text and contradicts it. Through the example of borrowing bread by count among neighbors, the Hanafi school demonstrates that custom can only be given effect if it removes the effective cause ('illa) of the text. Otherwise, honoring the custom would amount to naskh (abrogation), which custom cannot do. The passage also highlights a scholarly debate between Ibn Abidin and Ahmed Fahmi Abu Sunna over whether a general custom can specify a general text regardless of timing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim
State the core rule: a later custom (urf hadith) conflicting with a general text is respected only if it negates the illa of that text. But if the illa remains, the custom is disregarded because accepting it would be naskh.
The illa is the rationale behind the ruling; without it the ruling ceases to apply.
2
Apply the maxim to the bread example
Analyze how Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani allowed borrowing bread by count among neighbors despite bread being a weighed ribawi item. The illa of riba prohibition (profit-seeking through weight inequality) is absent in such neighborly transactions, so the custom is valid.
3
Contrast with prohibited customs
Examine the examples of unacceptable customs (excessive mourning, mixed dancing, customary riba). In these, the illa of prohibition (e.g., harm, corruption) remains or intensifies, so the custom has no legal weight.
4
Evaluate the scholarly dispute
Compare Ibn Abidin's view (from Nashr al-Urf) that a general custom can specify a general text regardless of timing, with Abu Sunna's rebuttal that this applies only to pre-existing custom (urf qadim). Determine the mufta bihi position.
Abu Sunna's view aligns with the broader Hanafi principle that later custom cannot abrogate a text without negating its illa.
5
Formulate the fatwa rule
Synthesize the rule: for a mufti, when a later custom conflicts with a general text, first identify the illa of the text. If the custom negates it, the custom prevails; if not, the text prevails and the custom must be rejected.
This rule is derived from the maxim 'al-hukm yaduru ma'a il-lati wujudan wa 'adaman'.

Chapter (73): The custom that arises after a general text and contradicts it. [...] This permission for borrowing bread by count among neighbors without regard to weight difference was relied upon by Imam Muhammad on the common custom, arguing that neighbors have become accustomed to disregarding the weight difference and considering only the count. [...] Thus this transaction is not fit to be taken as a path to prohibited usury, because the cause (illa) of the general texts prohibiting usury (which is profit-seeking) is not realized by this path, and the ruling rotates with its cause. There is no specific text prohibiting borrowing bread by count among neighbors by way of specification and designation, so that this custom would be conflicting with the explicit text. So the considered criterion is the cause of the general texts prohibiting usury, and this cause is not realized in this custom among neighbors where they do not consider the weight difference between loaves. [...] This is a set of established legal evidences that speak, along with their explanations transmitted from the jurists, that if the custom removes and negates the cause of the conflicting text, it is respected legally, even if it is a later custom. But if the cause of the conflicting text is not negated by the later custom, then this custom is disregarded in the face of the generality of the text, because considering the later custom would then be abrogation (naskh) of the text, and custom does not have this authority. [...] If people become accustomed to some legally objectionable customs [...] then their habituation does not remove the cause of their prohibition, because their prohibition was not based on their unfamiliarity but on the evil of their consequences. [...] (1) Research by the verifier Ibn Abidin on the ruling of conflict between custom and text in his treatise 'Nashr al-Urf'. He extracted from the sayings of jurists a criterion summarized as: If custom contradicts the text in every respect, i.e., the text is specific to the subject so that acting on custom necessitates leaving the text, then there is no doubt in rejecting the custom [...] But if it does not contradict it in every respect, as when the text is general and custom contradicts it in some of its applications, then custom is considered if it is a general custom, because general custom can specify the text. He did not distinguish in this criterion between the custom existing at the time of the text's revelation and that arising after. However, the esteemed professor Ahmad Fahmi Abu Sunna in his thesis 'Al-Urf wa al-Adah fi Ra'y al-Fuqaha' [...] refuted what Ibn Abidin verified on this issue convincingly and clarified that the statement of the author of 'Al-Tahrir' is about the old custom existing at the time of the legislative text's revelation, whereas the emergent custom cannot specify the general text, even if it is general custom.
Source pages p. 869

Commentary
This passage is a legal definition of a qawida (maxim) concerning the conflict between a later custom ('urf hadith) and a general text (nass amm) in Hanafi usul al-ifta. The author begins by presenting a case study: lending bread by count among neighbors, despite bread being a ribawi item that is weighed. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani allowed this based on prevalent custom, because the underlying cause (illa) of riba – profit-seeking through weight inequality – is absent in such neighborly transactions. The key principle extracted is: if a custom removes the cause of a general prohibition, it is respected even if it arises later. Conversely, if the custom does not negate the cause, it is invalid because giving it effect would amount to abrogation (naskh) of the text, which custom cannot do. Examples of rejected customs include excessive mourning, mixed dancing, or customary riba. The passage concludes with a dispute between Ibn Abidin (who argued that a general custom can specify a general text regardless of timing) and Ahmad Fahmi Abu Sunna (who restricted this to pre-existing custom only). The Hanafi position leans toward Abu Sunna's view that a later custom cannot override a general text unless it negates its cause.

Key Terms
العرف الحادث (al-urf al-hadith): العرف الذي ينشأ بعد ورود النص التشريعي العام
العلة (al-illa): الوصف المناسب الذي يدور معه الحكم وجوداً وعدماً
النص العام (al-nass al-amm): النص الذي يتناول جميع أفراد جنسه بلفظ شامل
النسخ (al-naskh): رفع الحكم الشرعي بدليل متأخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It must be practiced by the majority of Muslims.
B) It must negate the effective cause (illa) of the general text.
C) It must be documented by reliable scholars.
D) It must have been present at the time of the Prophet (PBUH).

Key Takeaway
A later custom can only override a general text if it negates the text's effective cause; otherwise, the custom is invalid and must be rejected.

Custom Overrides Qiyas: Bees and Dowry Claims
Two branches applying the maxim that custom overrides qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'al-urf yutarjahu 'ala al-qiyas' to two furu' and evaluate how changing custom affects the fatwa.

Core Idea
Custom can make permissible what qiyas forbids, and fatwa changes when custom changes.

This lesson examines two concrete furu' where Hanafi jurists allowed custom to override strict qiyas. The first concerns the sale of bees and silkworms; the second involves a wife's claim for unpaid prompt dower after consummation. These examples show that custom (urf) is a recognized source of law that can even override analogical reasoning (qiyas). The lesson also highlights that custom is not static: a footnote by al-Zarqa notes that in modern Syria, the custom around prompt dower has changed, so the fatwa must be revisited. This is a classic illustration of the principle that rulings can change with time and place.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qawida
State the maxim: 'Custom overrides analogy in case of conflict.' This is a wusta (intermediate) qawida governing situations where a textual proof is absent.
2
Analyze the first furu': sale of bees and silkworms
Abu Hanifa analogized bees and silkworms to vermin (lizards, frogs), which are not property, so the sale is invalid. Imam Muhammad considered the custom of buying and selling them, thus ruling the sale valid. Identify that custom (urf) formed the basis for a different ruling.
This is an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Hanifa vs. Muhammad al-Shaybani.
3
Analyze the second furu': wife's claim of unpaid prompt dower
Qiyas would require the judge to hear any claim. But Hanafi jurists rejected the wife's claim because custom shows that a woman is not given to her husband without receiving at least part of the prompt dower. Thus, her claim contradicts the apparent state of affairs (zahir al-hal) and is not heard.
4
Apply the istithna': changing custom
Al-Zarqa notes that today in Syria, many women are married without receiving any prompt dower. So the custom is no longer universal. Therefore, the claim should now be heard. This shows that the fatwa depends on the prevailing custom.
This illustrates the dynamic nature of custom-based rulings.
5
Compare with Maliki view
Al-Qarafi reports that Imam Malik also gives precedence to the husband's statement in this case, but for different reasons. This shows that custom can be treated differently across madhahib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢/٧٤ - ومن فروع هذا المبدأ في الاجتهاد الحنفي التطبيقات التالية : ١ - أن بيع النحل ودود القز غير جائز عند أبي حنيفة لأنه لم يعتبرهما من الأموال، قياساً على سائر هوام الأرض كالوزغ والضفادع ولكن الإمام محمداً من أصحابه حكم بماليتهما وصحة بيعهما لجريان التعامل بهما في عرف الناس بيعاً وشراء.
۲ - أنَّ الأصل القياسي يقضي بأن على الحاكم أن يستمع إلى كل دعوى ترفع إليه، ثم يقضي للمدعي أو عليه بحسب ما يثبت لديه . ولكن الفقهاء تركوا هذا القياس فيما إذا ادعت الزوجة المدخول بها أنَّ زوجها لم يدفع إليها شيئاً من معجل مهرها وطلبت القضاء عليه بجميع المهر المعجل فقالوا لا تسمع دعواها هذه بل يردها القاضي دون أن يسأل عنها الزوج؛ وعللوا ذلك بالعرف لأنّ عادة الناس مطردة لا تكاد تتخلف في أن المرأة لا تُزَفُ إلى زوجها ما لم يدفع بعض المهر المعجل أو كله. فتكون دعواها هذه مما يكذبه ظاهر الحال بعد الدخول، فلا تسمع (ر: رد المحتار باب المهر ٣٦٣/٣ وتنقيح الفتاوى الحامدية أول المهر ۲۰/۱).
(1) يلحظ هنا أن هذا العرف في زماننا لم يبق اليوم مطرداً في بلادنا الشامية وإن كان لا يزال غالباً، فكثير من النساء يزففن دون قبض شيء من المهر تيسيراً على الزوج. فينبغي سماع هذه الدعوى اليوم.
وقد نقل شهاب الدين القرافي في كتابه الإحكام في تمييز الفتاوى عن الأحكام، وتصرفات القاضي والإمام عن مالك أن الزوجة إذا ادعت بعد الدخول بها عدم قبض صداقها فالقول للزوج، مع أن الأصل القياسي عدم القبض.
2/74 - And among the branches of this principle in Hanafi ijtihad are the following applications: 1 - The sale of bees and silkworms is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa because he did not consider them property, by analogy to other insects of the earth like lizards and frogs. But Imam Muhammad, one of his companions, ruled that they are property and their sale is valid because people customarily deal in them by selling and buying. 2 - The qiyasic principle requires that the judge must hear every claim brought to him, then decide for the plaintiff or against him according to what is established before him. But the jurists abandoned this qiyas in the case where a wife who has been consummated claims that her husband did not pay her any of the prompt dower and seeks judgment against him for the entire prompt dower. They said: her claim is not heard; rather, the judge rejects it without asking the husband about it. They justified this by custom, because the consistent habit of people is hardly ever broken: a woman is not given to her husband unless he has paid part or all of the prompt dower. So her claim is contradicted by the apparent state of affairs after consummation, and thus it is not heard (see: Radd al-Muhtar, Bab al-Mahr, 3/363; Tanqih al-Fatawa al-Hamidiyya, beginning of Mahr, 20/1). (1) It is noted here that this custom in our time is no longer consistent in our Syrian lands, though it is still prevalent. Many women are given in marriage without receiving any dower to ease the husband. So this claim should be heard today. Shihab al-Din al-Qarafi reported in his book al-Ihkam fi Tamyiz al-Fatawa from Malik that if the wife after consummation claims she did not receive her dower, the husband's statement is accepted, even though the qiyasic principle is non-receipt.
Source pages p. 873

Commentary
This passage provides two practical applications of the principle that custom can override qiyas in Hanafi fiqh. The first example concerns the sale of bees and silkworms. Abu Hanifa analogized them to vermin like lizards and frogs, which are not considered property, so he ruled the sale invalid. However, his student Imam Muhammad considered them property because people customarily buy and sell them. This illustrates that custom can establish the legal status of objects as property. The second example deals with a wife's claim after consummation that she did not receive any of her prompt dower. The general qiyas principle would require the judge to hear and decide any claim. But Hanafi jurists ruled that such a claim is not heard because custom dictates that a woman is not handed over to her husband without receiving at least part of her prompt dower. Thus, her claim contradicts the apparent state of affairs. The author adds a crucial footnote: today in Syria, this custom is no longer universal, so the claim should now be heard. This shows that fatwa may change with changing customs. A Maliki view from Qarafi is cited for comparison.

Key Terms
المهر المعجل (al-mahr al-mu'ajjal): The portion of the dower that is paid promptly at the time of marriage.
المدخول بها (al-madkhul biha): A wife with whom the marriage has been consummated.
ظاهر الحال (zahir al-hal): The apparent state of affairs that is consistent with customary practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Imam Muhammad consider the sale of bees and silkworms valid despite Abu Hanifa's qiyas?
A) Because bees are mentioned in the Quran.
B) Because people customarily buy and sell them, making them property.
C) Because qiyas is always weaker than individual opinion.
D) Because Abu Hanifa later changed his view.

Key Takeaway
Custom can override qiyas in Hanafi law, but the fatwa depends on the continuation of that custom.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate
10 min
Custom Overriding Analogy: The Qawida and Its Applications
Explaining the qawida with three furuat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'custom outweighs analogy' to identify when a customary practice overrides an analogical ruling in mu'amalat.

Core Idea
Custom can override analogy when it is consistent and prevalent.

In Hanafi fiqh, custom ('urf) is a recognized source of law that can sometimes override analogical reasoning (qiyas). This lesson examines the qawida 'al-urf yutarajjah 'ala al-qiyas' and its practical applications in sales and family law. Understanding this principle is essential for a mufti to issue rulings that align with community practice while respecting the framework of ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogical baseline
First, determine what the ruling would be purely by qiyas, without considering custom. For example, in the case of bees and silkworms, Abu Hanifa ruled their sale invalid by analogy to vermin.
The analogical baseline is the 'default' ruling before custom is applied.
2
Assess the customary practice
Check if there is a consistent and prevalent custom ('urf mutarid ghalib) in the community regarding the matter. If people commonly engage in the transaction or behave in a certain way, that custom is considered.
Custom must be general and consistent, not isolated or rare.
3
Determine conflict with analogy
See if the custom runs contrary to the analogical ruling. If yes, the custom may override the analogy.
Custom can only override if it does not contradict a clear text (nass).
4
Apply the custom to modify the ruling
If the custom is valid, apply it to either permit or restrict the analogical ruling. In the bee example, Imam Muhammad allowed the sale due to custom.
The custom becomes the basis for a new ruling, often via istihsan.
5
Consider change over time
Custom is dynamic; if it changes, the ruling may revert to the analogical baseline or adopt a new custom. The footnote about Syrian customs shows this principle in action.
The mufti must be attentive to temporal and regional changes in custom.

Source Text

Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom
... It is based indirectly, so it indicates that it also outweighs istislah (public interest) which is not based on text, but only on temporal benefit that is subject to change with changing times and emerging circumstances and requirements.
2/74 - Among the branches of this principle in Hanafi ijtihad are the following applications: 1. The sale of bees and silkworms is not permissible according to Abu Hanifah because he did not consider them as property, by analogy to other crawling creatures like lizards and frogs. But Imam Muhammad, one of his companions, ruled that they have monetary value and their sale is valid due to the custom of people dealing in them by sale and purchase.
2. The analogical principle requires that the judge listen to every case brought before him, then rule for or against the plaintiff according to what is proven. But the jurists abandoned this analogy in the case where a wife who has been consummated claims that her husband did not pay her any of the prompt dower and demands judgment against him for the entire prompt dower. They said: her claim is not heard; rather the judge rejects it without even asking the husband. They justified this by custom, because the normal practice of people is consistent and rarely deviates that a woman is not given to her husband until he pays part or all of the prompt dower. So her claim is contradicted by the apparent state of affairs after consummation, so it is not heard. (Radd al-Muhtar, chapter of Mahr, 3/363 and Tanqih al-Fatawa al-Hamidiyya, beginning of Mahr (1)(20/1)
(1) Note that this custom in our time is no longer consistent in our Syrian lands, though it is still prevalent. Many women are given without receiving any dower to ease the burden on the husband. So this claim should be heard today.
Al-Qarafi in his book al-Ihkam fi Tamyiz al-Fatawa 'an al-Ahkam, and the actions of the judge and imam, reported from Malik that if the wife after consummation claims non-receipt of her dower, the word is the husband's, even though the analogical principle is non-receipt.
932

Chapter (74) Conflict of Custom and Ijtihad
3. The analogical rules require that it is not permissible to pay a debt to other than its owner, and its receipt does not bind the creditor unless the receiver has authority from the creditor (guardianship or agency). But the jurists abandoned the analogy in the case of an adult virgin daughter if her father, or her grandfather in the father's absence, received her dower from her husband at her marriage. They considered this receipt effective against her and discharging the husband's liability due to custom and habit, unless she forbade payment to others.
There are many branches where jurists abandoned the ruling of analogy for the conflicting custom.
3/74 - Secondly: On analogy based on a customary cause: If this is the authority of custom in fiqh with respect to analogy based on inherent similarity in the causes between textual rulings and analogized rulings, then it indicates a fortiori the change of analogical rulings with the change of custom when the cause of the analogy itself is based on custom. In that case, change in ruling following the change of custom is not a departure from analogy but rather in line with it.
1. In the Hanafi school, the opinion of its Imam Abu Hanifah was that if a usurper dyes a garment black, that is considered a defect, so the owner has the choice: either leave it with him and claim its value as it was when usurped undyed, or take the garment with compensation from the usurper for the decrease in value due to this dye, according to the general analogical ruling on defects in usurped property with the usurper.
= And after him it is narrated from Qadi Isma'il that this is based on the custom of the people of Madinah in Malik's era that a man does not consummate with his wife until she receives all her dower. He said: Today their customs are contrary to that, so the word becomes the woman's with her oath regarding non-receipt due to differing customs.
(See al-Ihkam fi Tamyiz al-Fatawa 'an al-Ahkam, Answer to Question 39, p. 68)
933
Source pages p. 873

Commentary
This passage presents the theory of custom ('urf) in Hanafi fiqh and its interaction with qiyas (analogy). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that custom can override analogical rulings. He gives three examples from Hanafi jurisprudence: (1) Abu Hanifa forbade selling bees and silkworms by analogy to vermin, but Imam Muhammad allowed it due to customary dealing; (2) a wife's claim for unpaid dower after consummation is normally rejected because custom dictates she wouldn't be given without payment, though the footnote notes this custom has changed in modern Syria; (3) a father's receipt of his adult daughter's dower is valid by custom, despite the analogical rule requiring agency. Then he discusses a second principle: when the illa (cause) of a qiyas is itself custom-based, the ruling changes with custom. He gives the example of dyeing usurped cloth: Abu Hanifa considered it a defect, but if custom values the dye job differently, the ruling adjusts. The text is a qawida (maxim) with its furuat (applications), showing how custom operates as a source of law that can modify existing rulings.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): عادة الناس المستقرة في معاملاتهم وأقوالهم
القياس (al-qiyas): إلحاق أمر غير منصوص بحكم منصوص لعلة جامعة
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): المصلحة المرسلة غير المعتبرة بنص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the wife's claim for unpaid dower after consummation not heard according to the Hanafi jurists' example?
A) Because the husband is always believed.
B) Because custom dictates that she would not have been given without receiving dower.
C) Because the claim is filed too late.
D) Because the wife lacks legal standing.

Key Takeaway
Custom overrides analogy in Hanafi fiqh when it is consistent and prevalent; this principle is applied across sales, family law, and other areas.
